To him that has is given, from him that has not taken is.
Court, City, Country, Camp, and I, at ods thus even be,
I intermeddle not with them, they intercept not me,
For still I tether thence mine eyes, so here my heart is free.
Believe me, Sir, such is this world, this crosse-blisse world of ours
That Virtue hardly hides her self in poor and desert Bowres:
And such be best that seem not best: Content exceeds a Crown:
They may be richer, but more sweet my penny than their powne.
For wrest they, cark they, build they, sport they, get they worlds together,
At first or last they die from al, and pass they wot not whether:
Then comes their pelf in plea, themselves not praised at a feather.
And then (for so the Princes of great Alexander did)
Greedy of his, they strive and let the dead-man stink vnhid.
Or he that had a Country has, perhaps a Coffen now:
Perhaps less Cost, a Sheet and corse: perhaps, his heirs allow
The toombe himself alive had build, else toombles might he lie,
As, save for fashion, tearelesse. And it matters not: for why?
Testators and Executors so give and so receive,
As doubtful whethers joy or grief is more to take or leave:
For, as do hogs their troughes to hounds, so these give and get place:
Death, not the Dier gives bequests, and therefore but Graue-grace.
Nor all die testate: if they do, yet wieles may wills prevent:
Or what by rigor was misgot, in ryote is misspent.
Then Churles, why are they Churles unto themselves and others too:
The good that comes of their goods is good themselves shall do.
But men do walk in shadoes, and disquiet themselves in vain
To gather Riches, ignorant to whom they shall remain.
The world thus brooding Vanities, and I observing it,
Here in the world, not of the world, such as you see me sit.
The Earl did well allow his words, and would have lived his life,
Durst he have stayed, for whom pursuit in every place was rife.
He reconuenting arms therefore, and taken Prisner so,
Died to his Countries friends a friend, and to her foes a foe.
NOr might ye Queen and Kings own Son escape the Spe^cers pride,
But, fearing, fled to France, and there as banished abide:
Til thence supplanted, safety at Henaude they provide.
John, brother to the Earl, a Knight of Chivalry the chief,
With little, but a luckie band, was shipte for their relief.
No sooner had the Zealand ships conuaide their men ashoare,
But English Succors daily did increase their Standerds more.
Yeat first the Queen, Prince Edward, and the Nobles humbly crauc
Theirs and their Countries enemies, but no redress might have:
And then Sir John of Henaude shewde himself a warrior brave.
The King, his wicked Counselors, his big Vpstarts, and all
Were overcome: So Spencers both from heaven to hell did fall,
Put to a foul and shameful death: with others that misled
The King in Outrages more great than erst in England bred.
Prolers, Blood-thirstie, Parasites, Make-shifts, and Bawdes did thrive,
Nor was an ancient English Peer vnbanisht or alive:
For foreign and domesticke Swords, Plague, Famine, and Exile,
Did more than tithe, yea tithe the Tithe of men within this I Will.
Of Baldricks, Hoodes, Tabrides, and Furs, from Knights disgraded tore,
Attaintures of Nobility, and Arms reuersed store,
So many Spurs hewn off the heels, and Swords broke over head,
Were through a King so light and lewd a Council never read.
The King in prison and deposed, tyrannised, he did
By Trecheries of Mortimer that ruld the Roste that tide,
Whilst Edward, in Minority, his Fathers throne supplide.
CHAP. XXVIII.
THIS third of that same Name, as yet in Nonage for a time,
Although a King was vnder-kept by some that ouer-clime:
Queen mother and proud Mortimer, familiar more than should,
Did and undid more than they might, not less than as they would:
Till Edward, better counseled, hong Mortimer, the death
Of many a Peer, who Earl of March, and haughty for his birth,
Was Lord of nine skore dubbed Knights, his other traynes except,
For greater pomp than did his Prince this Lord of VVigmore kept.
But more he had been happy though less hautie in his Halls,
More honor in humility than safety in walls:
Proud Climers prove not monuments, save only in their falls.
The senseless pride of Fools therefore, whom reverently we ride,
Should lessen, at the least because that earth their earth shall hide.
The Country purged of Fleecers, and of Flatterers the Court,
The King became a Mars for Arms, a Jupiter for Port:
Th- Olymp^ds, the Pythea, and the prowess of the Earth,
Did seem even now, and not but now, to have in him their birth:
East, South, and North, gave aim farrc off, admiring so the West,
As if that Mars discarding them had set our Realm his Rest.
Philip Valois, and David Bruz, of power and courage more
Than any French or Scottish Kings since or of long before,
Confedrate with three other Kings and Princes far and near,
War all at once on Edward, but did buy their warring dear.
David debelled, left his land, but lastly did return,
And, whilst our King did war in France, much did he spoil and burn,
And proud of mighty Troops of men, of unresisted prays,
And Edwards absence, prosperously he on advantage plays,
Until, not sending hence for help, the Queen did muster Knights,
And with the Foe, though tripled-wise, victoriously she fights:
The Scots for most did perish, and their King was Prisner taine,
And Scotland wholly for a pray to England did remain
Mean while was Paris scarcely left, to rescue Philips Goal,
Whom Edward ferrits so from hold to hold as Fox from hoale,
That Melancholy he deceast, and valiant John his son
Was crowned King of France: and then the wars afresh begone.
But after many fields, unto the Foes continual wrack,
The French King captiuated to the English Monarke, back
His Victor sails, the Prince of Wales, Edward surnamed black:
The flower of Chivalry, the fear of France, and scourge of Spain,
Wheare Peter, dispossessed of Crown, was crownde by him again.
Four years the French, eleven years was the Scotch K. prisners here.
Whose, and the Dolphines ransomes were as great as good their cheer.
PRince Edw. John of Gaunt, and all their Fathers sons might boaste
Of famous Sier, and he of sons matchless in any Coaste:
Howbeit, King and Prince at last, misled by counsel ill,
Through Taxes lost a many hearts that bore them erst good will:
Thence finding Fortune contrary to that she was before,
Yeat either dying seaz'd of French and Scottish Conquests store:
Yea Callice late, and Barwick yet of their Exployts is lest,
Though Son before the Sier and both of lives long since bereft.
When Barwick was besieged, and stood bravely at defense,
Sir Alexander Seiton, theare chief Captain, had pretense
To linger forth the Siege till Scots should draw the English thence
In rescue of Northumberland, and therefore sent his son
A Pledge of treated Truce: and when the guile-got Truce was done,
And Barwick not relieved nor resigned, as it ought,
Two sons of Seiton were before the walls besieged brought,
They ready for the Iybbet and their Father for his Grave,
For either he must yield the Town or them he might not save.
In grief he then his Countries cause and Childrens case reuolues:
But, partial unto either, he on neither Choice resolves,
To be a loyal Subject and a loving Father too
Behooued him: but both to be was not in him to do.
Nature and honor wrought at once, but Nature ouer-wrought,
And, but his Lady it preuents, to yield the Town he thought.
O what pretend you Sir, quoth she, is Barwick worth no more
Than error of such love? I joy that I such Children bore
Whom cruel Edward honors with such a cause of death,
For that especial cause for which we all receive our breath,
Even for their Countries cause they dye, whose lives for it be dew,
Why see their faces, (constantly she did their faces view)
The same, my Seiton, seem so far from dreading any woe,
As if they skornde that Barwicke should redeem them from the Foe.
Full dear they were to me unborn, at birth, and borne, and now,
And Mother like I moan their death and yet their death allow.
More Sons and such you may beget, your honor if you stain,
Defected honor never more is to be got again.
Prevent not then your self, your Sons, and me so great a blis:
Adieu, and dye (sweet Sons) your souls in heaven shall live for this
With such persuasions did she win her husband from the walls:
And Edward executes their Sons, and to assault he falls
So long that Barwicke yields at length, and still us master calls.
THese were the days when English arms had every where request,
And Edw. knights throughout the world had prick and praise for best.
Not Knights alone, but Prelates too, and Queens: whereof were twain,
The quondam and in esse Queens, by Armor honor gain:
By War the Queen that was did cease her husbands tragic Rayn,
And by the Queen then being was the Scotch King Prisnet tayne.
It follows then, that as the Pawnce does circkle with the Son,
So to the vice or virtue of the Prince are people won.
O that our Muse might evermore on such a Subject run:
But Vulcan forgeth other Tools, and sharpneth deadlier swords,
For little else then civil wars our following Pen affords.
French Expeditions badly thrive, whereof we cease to speak:
Not foreign, but Domestic wars, grew strong to make us weak.
Melpomen here might rack her wits, Sylla and Marius hate,
Pharsalian Fields were gentle Frayes, regarding this debate.
The second Richard, son unto the black Prince (Edward dead)
Was crownde an Infant, and from him the Stratagem was bread.
The bace attempts of Ball, of Straw, of Lyster, tag, and rag,
Of Villains, Of-skoms, Clownes, and knaves that checkmate durst to brag
With Richards self, and to their deaths his chiefest Princes drag.
Till Wal(...)ths girdle-Armor made the Arms of London more,
Because his courage chiefly gave an end to that uproar,
And what-so-els Occurrants much may interrupt our Vain,
Digesting York and Lancaster, acquiring eithers Rain,
Our Pen shall not endenizen: Now drops it sacred blood
Of Men-Gods, English Potentates that in this Faction stood.
Richard begun that civil war, that till the Seventh from him
Did last: though often fields with blood of Citizens did swim.
Against the Nobles the vphild innoble, and his Peers
And Commons went alike to wrack, nor God nor man he fears.
In few, Ambition, Avarice, and Counsel lewd had wrought
In him a nature worser than into the world he brought:
Whereby, and thus, himself and house at length a down-fal cought,
Twixt Mowbray D. of Norffolke, and the D. of Hertford, son
To John of Gaunt, close Conference of better days begun.
The King (said Henry Hertford) more remiss than does beseem,
Leaves France to French, Scotland to Scots, and us to woes extreme:
His Flatterers do fleece the Crown and Commons, not a State
Does or dares counsel, ancient Coats that on the Crown should wate
Give aim to bastard Armorie: what rests then but this?
Pluck down those grating Harpies that seduce our King amiss,
If worthles still, set up a King worthier than he that is.
The other, saying little then, immediately reueales
The secret, and before the King his Foe-made friend appeals:
Whose Gauntlet raised by the Duke defendant, at the last
It grew to single Combat, when the King his Warder cast,
And to the Duke of Norffolke judged for evermore exile,
And self same law Duke Henry had, save for a lesser while.
Thus That did This, but This and That their Judge did thus begile:
And to his Coffers did escheate a world of wealth, a Pray
Unto his Parasites, which thriu'd by other men's decay.
Mean while (whose actious life had lawd) did John of Gaunt decease:
So to the banished Duke his Son five Cronets did increase.
But with his kindly air the King withheld him all the same:
Till entering, aided by his friends, he won beyond his clame:
For Richard was imprisoned, and by Parliament put down,
And Henry Duke of Lancaster elected to the Crown,
The Line Lancastrian frollicke, but the house of York did frown.
For to those Hydra-kinded wars that after did ensue
Those Families gave name: though first the Diadem was due
Unto the house of Clarence, till to York that interest gre we
By marriage, here omitted: for we only give a view
How York mis-raigning Lancaster did enter, then how This
Was dispossessed, That repossest, and how their Union is.
THE SIXTH BOOK OF ALBIONS ENGLAND.
CHAP. XXIX.
HEnry (the fourth so named) hild the King deposed strate
In Pomfret Castle, howebeit in honorable State:
And got an Act, that who so wrought the Prisner to restore,
That Richards self, to void their hope, should dye the first therefore:
Whose birth brought Nature, gentle Lord, returning whence it straid,
Now altered him, erst altring it: and Richard mildly said.
I must not say I am, and would I might not say I was,
Of great the greatest: less they grieve from whom does little pass:
Nor more it grieves to contrary the same I have been, then
To have deserved not to be vnmaliced of men.
Thus humbled and full penitent lives he, less mal-content
Than was the Duke of Exeter his brother, whose intent
Was as at a lusts to have destroyed King Henry, but descryed;
Himself, four such, and many Knights the death of Traitors dyed:
And by these primer Yorkests thus King Richards date grew out:
But whether brayned, famisht, or exiled rests a doubt:
For often Vprores did ensue for him, as vndeceast,
How beit solemnly interred, himself, or Sign at least.
Twice by confedrate Chivalry the P^ercies and their friends
Did fight and fall, for either war to Henry's honor ends.
He never had but war, and was victorious evermore,
As Well at home, as also of his Foes on foreign Shore:
Till lastly Armor overcame all Envy, and he lives
Of all beloved, and his death a common sorrow gives.
HOt spur his Son, Henry the fifth, hung at his Fathers eyes,
To watch his Ghost, and catch his Crown, and that or ere he dies.
And where the Father doubted if he got it well or not,
The Son did swear, how so it came, he would it not forgo.
His bad did bliss the Bad, the Good despair all good: But neither
Did aim aright, for suddenly his change deceived either:
Of good becoming best, that was of ill the baddest, and
The true perfection of a King was not but in this Land.
He lead good fortune in a line, and did but war and win:
France was his Conquest: Scots but brag and he did beat them in:
A friend unto weldoings, and an Enemy to sin.
Yeat of the Yorkests never lackt he Princes that rebel:
Nor other than confusion to their still coniuring fell.
In few, if any Homer should of this Achilles sing,
As of that Greek and My^midon the Macedonian King
Once noted would I note both Prince and Poet happiest men,
That for deserving praise, and This for well employed pen:
For well this Subject might increase the Worthies unto ten.
He, aged thirty six, deceast and left his infant Son,
His Kingdom, Conquests, and his Queen, whose Fathers Realm he won,
To grave protection, Regents, and so royal for the port,
As that his Orphants Cradle seemed an Alexanders Court.
Queen Katherin, Daughter of the French, King Henryes wife of late,
The fairest Lady in the West, hild with her son Estate.
She often behild, and hild her peace, a brave Esquier of VVailes,
That tide her fancy to his form, till fancied form preuailes.
All liking was reuersed Love, save Owen Tuder, all
Save him (that durst not dream such good) to her was less than small.
She formally, by quaint degrees, attracting him to favor,
Did nourish burnings in her self, by noting his behauor.
She pitched Tewe, he masshed: She vncompaned, To fly
He bids her solitary moodes: She asks the remedy:
Disclosed pangues sometimes, quoth he, in Physic Physic is:
Which sometimes to observe, quoth she, does Patients patience mis:
Cureles to Aesculapius and Apellos self am I:
The latter felt my languor, and, immortal, wished to die:
And yeat, save one, no one disease lay hidden to his Art:
For you were bootlesse then to guess how to vngreeue my smart.
Had Daphne to Apollo been Apollo, Tuder said,
His might have been, and so may be your Graces humor staid.
He, other Gods and Goddesses, found more contented Love
Beloe, in diffring bosoms, than in equal beds above.
I aim at Love (for thereto your Aenigma does incline)
And aim to him a Deity for whom I so divine.
But gladly doubt I of the Man, for if I doubted not,
I should but massacer my lack in envy of his lot:
Yeat are unworthy of the Moon Endymions lips, I wot.
But (for I will disperse the mistes of further Mysteries,
And toogh the Pinuesse of my thoughts to kenning of your Eyes)
If Ge^try, Madame, might convey so great a good to me,
From ancient King Cadwallader I have my pettigree.
If wealth be said my want, I say your Grace does want no wealth,
And my suppliment shall be love, employed to your health.
It has been when as hearty Love did treat and tie the knot,
Though now, if gold but lack in grains, the wedding fadgeth not.
The goodly Queen in bashful signs blushed out a dumb Reply:
Which he did constur as she meant, and kissed her reverently.
Tuder, quoth she, I greater am than would I were for thee,
But can as little master Love as Lessers in degree.
My Father was a King, a King my Husband was, my Brother
He is a King, a King my Son, and I thy Sovereigns mother:
Yeat Fathers, Husbands, Brothers, Sons, and all their Styles together,
Are lesser valewed than to live beloved of my Tuder:
Should England, France, and thou thy self gainesay thy self for mine,
Thy self, France, England, nor what els' should bar me to be thine.
Yea, let them take me wilful, or mistake me wanton, so
My self in love do please my self let all the world say no:
Let Pesants matte their marriages, and thrive at peradventure:
I love for love: no gentle heart should fancy by Indenture.
But tell me, Owen, am I not more forward then behooues?
I am, Sweetheart, but blame me not, the same that speaks loves.
And long may live, quoth he, to love, nor longer live may I,
Than while I love your Grace, and when I leave disgraced die.
But Lady, if I do deserve, I then desire dispatch:
For many are the jealous Eyes that on your beauty watch.
Good hap is like to hit me well, to hit so well is rare,
And rareness does commence my suit, let suit conclude my care.
Should Caesar kiss (he kissed her) it were but such a kiss:
And he, and I, here, or elsewhere, in other sport or this,
Do act alike: no bettring but as your belouing is.
You may experience, when you please, what difference in the men:
And if King Henry pleased more, blame Owen Tuder then.
But am I not (yes, Sweet, I am) more sawsie than behooues?
Yeat for my heart forgive my tongue, This speaks, and That loves.
How he imprisoned did escape, and else what else-wheare reed:
The Queen and this brave Gentleman did marry, and their Seed
Began that royal Race that did, does, and may still succeed
In happy Empire of our Throne, a famous line in deed.
Once, when this Match was at a point, they merrily disposed,
Did descant what from vulgar tongues thereof would be supposed.
They will believe me amorous, or thee so wiued as
Vulcan the Smith of Lemnos that to Venus married was,
The Queen did say. And Tuder said: I hope of hansell better,
In Venus and in Vulcans names more lies than the letter:
For he was as I would not be, She as you should be never,
Either so apt to give and take as pity them to sever.
I pray thee, Owen, quoth the queen, how met they, can thou tell?
I can he said, and more then so, then mark the process well.
When Vulcan was a Batcheler, and Venus was vnwed,
Thus wowde he her, thus won he her, thus wowde and won he sped.
CHAP. XXX.
VEnus the fairest Goddess, and as amorous as fair,
Beloved of Mars, and loving Mars, made oftentimes repair
To Vulcans forge, as to see wrought for Jupiter his Fire
And thunders, Mars his Armors, and the Sunwaines curious tire,
When they, indeed, of merriments in Love did theare conspire:
And lastly did conlude the Smith a Stale unto their sport,
Wherein did Venus play her part, preuayling in this sort.
Vulcan (quoth she) no God there is, I think, but needs thee:
For Thunders Jove, Ceres for Sieths, for Armors Mars I see,
Bacchus for prewning Knives, and Pan for Sheep-hookes, Phoebus he
For Cart-tiers, Dis for shakling chains, Neptune for Ankers, and
No God but lackes thee, saving I that ask not at thy hand.
My Swans do draw in silken Geeres, my wheels be shod with down,
No hardines is in beauties Coach: But thou, by birth no Clown,
But Jove his Son, a God as we, art made a drudge too much,
When, if that Venus might be heard, thou should not be such.
How apt are all in those same toils that tend to their behoof
To let thee bear till back does break? but common is the proof,
That cunning is not cunning if it stands not aloof
By this had Vulcan hammered his heat, and bad to stay
The Bellows, and he lymping from the Anfeeld thus did say.
My business, Venus, is ydoe, now may I tend to play:
What woudst thou? for I member scarce thy arging by my fay.
Wodst that I leave the forge, and thrt I god it with the Gods?
If so thou meanst, thy meaning and my meaning be at ods.
Sweeter my Bellows blowing and my hammers beating is
To me, then trimmest fidling on the trickest kit ywis:
Ask whatso-else I have to give, thous maunde it for a kis.
As if, quoth she, my kisses were so current unto all?
No, not at all to Vulcan, if his kindness be so small.
I ask thy proper ease, then earne thy proper ease, and ask
More than a kiss: at least wise do thy self from Mars vntaske.
He is my Foe, friend thou not him, nor forge him Arms, but let
Him luske at home vnhonored, no good by him we get.
What lets but that we may become superlatiues? Of us
All stand in need, we need not them. Then gave she him a ^us.
And say me so, quoth Vulcan, and unto the trough he hies,
And skowres his coly fists and face, and with his apron dries
Them, badly mended, and unto the Queen of Dalliance says
That Mars should lusk at home for him. Then guilefull Venus plays
Her part so well, that on her lap his head the Dotardlayes.
And whilst upon her pressed Thies (no Haven for such Hulke)
He ^olls, and loades her with the weight of his vnwealdy bulk,
And whilst she coyes his sooty Cheeks, or curles his sweaty top,
The Groshead now and then, as hapt, a threadbare term lets drop.
Then laughes he like a horse, as who would say, trow said I well?
But soon his wits were Non plus, for his wooing could but spell.
This fitted her: for so before twixt Mars and her was meant,
Though not that she so cunningly should Mars of Arms prevent.
But him to stawle in store, not else employde, was her intent.
Her Lubber now was snorting ripe, and she mean while was glad,
That for to serve her turn else-wheare so good a Staile she had.
What pass I, thinks Venus, on his form or fashions rude?
For, letting form and fashion pass, one fashion is pursude
In getting Children: at the least, who so the Child shall git,
It shall suffice that Vulcan is the same shall father it.
Now Mars in heaven, Anchises and Adonis on the earth
May earne for Babes, for Vulcan shall be parent at their birth.
Nay, be it that he should espy false carding, what of it?
It shall be thought but jealousy in him, or want of wit.
Him frowns shall threat, or smiles entreat, and few will judge, I win,
If it shall come in question, that to Cockhole him were sin.
Whilst thus she thinks in her self the Cyclops did awake:
And, to be short, more doings pass and they a marriage make.
But wonder did the Deities, when bruted was the match,
That he so foul a thick-skinne should so fair a Lady catch,
They flout him to his face, and held it alms to arm his head.
Well, Venus shortly bagged, and ere long was Cupid bread:
And Vulcan (in like heresy of fathering as more)
Did rack his Art to arm ye Lad with wings, with shafts, with bow,
Most forceable to love or hate, as lists him shootes bestow.
When Vulcans Venus had obtaind her Cupide armed thus,
Then (for we wish that all besides be suitable to us)
She, of the Gods and Goddesses before the wanton noted,
Was of the Gods and Goddesses for wantonness out-coted,
Not one but wexed amorous, yea even Diana Doted.
Loves Mother had direction of his arrows, and she wild
Him hit the Son-God: for because he, blabbing had behild
Her daliance with Adonis: so that vexed Phoebus loves
Fair Daphne, whom nor wooes, nor vows, nor gifts, nor greatness moves.
Succes^es therefore, and enraged, he bastards Cupid and
(For stoutly on their honesties do wily Harlots stand)
Venus did chaife, and of the Gods their strife came to be skand.
Dispersing then her goodly hairs, she barred so sweet a face,
As from the sternest Godhood might extort suborned grace.
Fast at her side clung naked Love, a lovely boy in deed,
And Vulcan, benched with the Gods, his wife did thus proceed:
(When Phoebus had already told his tale with sense and heed.)
He says, quoth she, for chastity my hauiour was amiss:
Which proved or disproued, then in you to sentence is.
Ah, listen whence it is, ye Gods, that Venus is abused,
Because that Phoebus making love to Daphne was refused.
If that were wrong, the wrong must then by Phoebe be excused:
Who, rescuing her Votarisse, did so prevent her brother.
But be it that this Boy of mine, not seeing one from other,
Did hit him, for the Sons offense should he malign the mother?
And shall I tell the Childs offense? Why thus forsooth it was:
He fitted him to such a Love as did for Beauty pas.
But if he say it needeles was, because it booted not,
I say, that Beauty beggeth if by posting it be got.
He wooing like himself in post did kiss the post, and she,
Too good to be his forced Trull, is now become his Tree,
His speeches too, though spoke by one, concerns in credit three.
Mine Husband, and my self, and Son, Gods, and as good as he.
Now woe am I, we severally are, as it were, arayned
Of Cuckolrie, of Spous-breach, and of Bastardy, though feigned,
Yeat too too forcible I fear to be forgot of some,
For slander set on foot, though false, is talkatiuely dome.
Malicious (for thy malice is thy matter all in all)
Is it to harlotize, think thou, a Goddess wrong too small,
But thou must forge it from the Earth, even from the Sheep-cote? Nay,
That color lacks color thou thy self I troe wilt say.
Ambitious, fair, and amorous thou termest me: if so,
Unlikely to disparge my self or bacely stoop so lo,
But being such, and knowing thee in very deed the same,
Might, leaving petite loves, have found thy self my readiest game:
For Phoebus is a Leacher, else are many tongues to blame.
Better no bad of mine (nor need I fear that fault in thee)
Thy bad does pass by probate, but a Quere is for me.
Perhaps (such as it is) my form may forge to his pretense:
Since Beauty is a common mark, apt therefore to offense.
Well, be it Beauty does atract, atracting is beloved,
Beloved courted, courted won, and won to action moved,
Yeat from such causes such effects what Consequence has proved?
For Daphne was, I wot, full fair, and well can Phoebus court,
Yeat Daphne chastely did withstand, and Phoebus mist his sport.
My husband though by trade a Smith, for birth out-brau'd of none,
And lovely unto Venus, (though mislik^ of many a one)
May for his plainnes also fit my foes inuectiue drifts:
As who would say, I wedded him to salve up other shifts.
By Styx I vow, although I should exceed my self for fare,
Yet Venus would be Vulcans, and he knows I truly swear.
He is indeed no Gallant, yeat a God, and merely free
From imperfections, such at least as pay not marriage fee.
And for his plainnes, to be plain, the rather choose I him:
For such as he live best, love best, and keep their wives most trim.
When Roysters either roue at change, be peevish or precise:
Fair women therefore matching thus be not, say I, unwise:
Judge not by such presumptions then, they add but to his lies.
Thus have you now a Medley of his malice and my moan,
His vice, my vow: and lastly rests your sentence to be known.
If Mercury should plead my cause, he could but set me clear:
Good causes need not curious terms, and equal Judges hear
The Equity, not Eloquence, and so I hope will ye:
And so shall grateful Venus sail under your gracious Lee.
So, putting finger in the Eye, the Deities descent:
Some hild with Phoebus, some with her: Which strife did Vulcan stent.
My wife, quoth he, more honest than her Cuser is, I troe,
Shall not ywis be bused by the squand^ing Pollo so:
She loves me, I durst swear, and save my self she loves no more:
And why should you or I believe his yea before her noe?
Troth, said the Gods, since Vulcan is contented we are pleased:
And so the variance was by him thus wi^tely appeased.
Phoebus his Plainte did quash: but so he aftertimes did watch,
As that Sir Hornsbie had by proof he was a loving Patch,
When Mars and Venus playing false his wier Net did catch.
Now riddle, Madame, if those tongues that make Sunonamies
Of them and us prove Oracles, what should thereof arise?
That more, quoth she, which you have said than in the letter lies.
But names infect not, nor receive your Riddle Prophecy:
If ought fore-sayd be ominous should any fear it is I.
When so the Queen had said, then to this more proceeded he:
Vulcan, Venus, Cupid, Sol, and Daphne turned to Tree
Were tennis balls to every tongue of every Dei^ee.
Tush Tush, quoth Pan, gay Venus and the gentle youth her son
Are blameles blamed: What think you, would Phoebus the^ have don
Had he in love bin crossed as I? And then he thus begun.
CHAP. XXXI.
THe Goteheards of Hyrcania hild their Orgies unto me,
And theare was I, unseen of them, the Festifall to see.
Now had they censed, and with glee eat were the hollowed Kids,
When as they fell to Rowndelaies, and I the Rownd amids.
Not Satires, or the Naiades, were half so nimble as
This country Consort (for each Lad was sorted with a Less.)
There was a tricksie Girl, I wot, albeit clad in gray,
As peatt as bird, as strait as boult, as fresh as flower in May,
As fair as Cupids Mother, or through him it is I err,
Ifso I err (for why his shaft had fixed me to her)
She daunsing dyed her lilly Cheeks, whilst I for love did di^:
And as unvisible I stood (what boots it me to lie)
And drew with breath her sweet-stole breath, so acting spiritually,
The feast was done and all undone that I did wish to do:
My Deity adiornde therefore, in human form I wowe.
And first (because that first they should approach us Gods) I feign
My self a Priest (for well I wot they sildome woo in vain.)
I made me smug, and with a Tex did intermix a toye,
And told how fine and fair a life our Clergie-Femes enjoy,
And how our leisure fitted Love. And let it fit (quoth she)
To such as lust for love: Sir Clarke, you clergefie not me.
Then came I curious in my silks (But who would think that Pan
Could play the Courtier?) and did feign my self a jolly man.
I talked of Castles, Manors, Parkes, and all things more than mine.
Too course (quoth she) am I for you, and you for me too fine.
Then Souldier-like I sued, and did boast of Battles many,
And standing on my Manhood would not be coriu'd of any:
And sometimes proffered kindness, such as came not to the push,
But checked for my boystrousnes was balked with a blush.
Then play I master Merchant, and did plye her by the book:
I spoke of great Accounts, Receites, nor little care I took
For rigging and return of Ships, (her lips mean while my Pex.)
Ply Sir (quoth she) your busy trade, you are besides the Tex.
I seem a country Yeoman: Then a Craftsman: both in vain:
The former was too lumpish, and the latter worse of twain:
Do what I could, I could not do whereby her love to gain.
Then thought I, out of doubt as I a God feign Manhood, so
This is transformed Diana for some practice meant beloe.
A year was past, and I past hope through coyish chaste denial,
And yeat I could not but persist in quest of further trial.
I met God Pryapus (for he, not Venus son it is
Abuses us, This darteth Love, That drives to lust amiss)
See yonder Clown? quoth Priapus (not far-off was a Loute
With near a handsome rag, himself less handsome soole to snout,
Less wel-form'd, or more il-fac'st, and like Clenchpoope look and lim,
Less mannerd, and worse gated than this Saturns-Eeue-made Slim,
God never made since God made Man, if ever God made him.)
That Lob, quoth he, and yonder Less that this way drives her Gotes
Do mark them Pan, you may observe from them vnthought-of notes,
I knew her for my Minion wench of whom I erst did tell.
First blend they herds, and forthwith lips, and after billing fell
To other sport, such sport ywis as would have likt me well.
Must I, thought I, give aim to such a skrub and such a Saint,
That Skowndrell, and this Counterfeit: confounded so I faint.
How cheer you Pan, quoth Priapus, the shameless God of lust,
Thus can I fit such friends as you with such a Trull of trust:
(We were indeed ere then at odds.) So Priapus he left me,
When he had brought me to this sight that near of sense bereft me.
But thus I loathed where I loved, and learned not too late
That coyest are not chastest, that the gayest Females mate
With Loutes as soon as Lords, that Love is luck not shiftles fate,
That cowled, celled, he, or she, whosoever, or wheresoever,
Or Votarie, or Secular, scarce one pryaped never.
To Pans report did Mercury reply and thus recite,
Of Cupide and of Priapus does Pan distinguish right:
But let be Lust, a word or two of Love and of his might.
I Entering Guest-wise on a time the frolic Thabane Court,
Mine eye presented to mine heart a Nymph of lovely Port:
Her knew I not, nor knew she me, unknown therefore vnkist
I loyter on the Earth, mean while in Heaven not vnmist.
My Senses held a Synod, and vnacted Acts dispute,
And nothing else I did affect but to effect my suit.
For whencesoeuer Love proceeds, or whatsoere it be,
Or whosoever loves, Love tormenteth in degree.
Mine Eye conuaid it to mine Heart, mine Heart controwld mine Eye:
Yeat Love retriu'd it self, I loved not knowing whom or why.
Then did I seek, and find (who am no Milksop as ye wot)
Acquaintance in the Court, the which the nicest balked not.
Nor smally did my shape, my tongue, and tunes (no common geere)
Prefer their Master to a place about their Mistress near.
When she did sigh then I did sob, I laughed if she did smile,
And by officious Forgeries pretended to begile.
But her, not coy, I found so chaste, as save a kiss or twain,
I nothing got, although in all I vained to her vain.
From ill therefore I grew to worse, from worse to worse, for why?
Through ouer-louing at the length I loved ielously.
My Stomach lest me, every sense had imperfection then,
My color ceast and, sick, I forge contrary cause to men.
So many Quames came ore my heart as news to ear or eye
Of others commoning in sport, or courting seriously.
No Corsiue to Coriuals, and no death unto despair:
I did not hope, yeat held I on with cost to nourish care.
Sometime, attyred by the book, I faind a merry cheer:
Sometime I drouped, and did wear disorderly my geere.
But how-soere I came to her, I found her still the same,
Gamesome enough to intertaine, and yet for me no game.
And though enuiously I aimed at others better speed,
Yeat, too precisely, did I sift such doubts were more than need.
Then rowsing up my self, I with my self did reason thus:
No folly were in Love, if so no folly were in us:
Wheare Mercury is laid asleep may others lay a straw:
The Lover and Beloved are not tied to one Law:
Because I am the same I am should she not therefore be
The same she is: mine is too love, but hers to disagree.
Then Mercury be to thy self thy self, these thoughts begile
With meeter thoughts, thou lingerest in loss too long a while.
Think not thy greatness, or thy gifts, or gracious eyes may get her:
A Fool more foul may seem more fair, Love may think bad the better.
If she determine Chastititie, then falls thy suit to ground:
Or if some other be preferred, then better lost than found:
Likte, or misliked to thy Love should reason be the bound.
Or Women love to be beloved of change of Clyents, or
Uncertain wheare to find them, with the Eagle or the Dorr.
Albeit Beauty mooues to love, and Love does make thee sue,
Better at first be Non-sute, than at length not to subdue.
Such Reasons seeming plausible, I fleeting whence I loved,
By absence and new Exercise old Passions were removed.
So did I love, and so I left, so many a skorne, and skoffe,
Care, cost, disgrace, and loss of time were and may be cut off:
And women so less stand aloof, when men can so be wise:
So lesser suit has lucklier speed, than to be too precise.
Not women, but our wilfulness, does work our own unrest:
Though Beauty, Love, and they lack fault, we may abuse the best.
SO help me Jupiter, (quoth Mars) in Love so may Ispeede,
As Mercury and Pan do err in points of Love indeed:
Precisians and plain Plodders (such is This, and so is That)
In Love do swallow Cammels, whilst they nicely strain a Gnat.
Why what be Women? Women, geld the latter sillabell,
Then are they nothing more then Woe, their names remain does tell.
Their yea, or no, even when they swear they love or love us not,
Believe who list: soon be they gone, as suddenly are got.
What need we creep the Cross to give unto a begging Saint?
Tush tush, a Fly for booke-Loue, none be fortunate that faint.
Not paper, pursse, or kerchiefe Plea lets Fancy sooner loase
Then at the Shrine to watch the Saint, She is not coy, but cloase:
Pollitians know to cheapen, what to offer, when to skoase.
The Clown, no doubt, that potted Pan lackt Art to gloss and flatter,
And yeat nor Pan nor Mercury went roundlier to the Matter:
He found right Method (for there is a Method, time, and place,
Which Fools observing do co^mence ere Wiseme^ have their grace.)
Though dastard Hawks do sore aloft and dare not seize upon,
Or Bussards-like do sit aloof until the game be gone,
Kind killing Hawks but wag the wing, and work tolsowse anon.
Once Love, surreuerence, made my self vale Bonnet, So submis
My ceremonial wooing was, as common wooing is:
With rufull looks, sighs, sweet Pigs-nye, and Fooleries more than few
I courted her, so much more stout by how much more I sew:
Till aptly singled, as it hapt, I say not what did hap,
But Love that late did load my Head, did load her willing Lap.
Nor this Lad Love of that same Love is guilty any whit,
For why? nine Moons did wexe and waine between his birth and it.
Alas poor Boy, before he was, Love was a common game,
The first-made Man, the Rib-reft Man in Eden showed the same:
For when his sudden eyes admired the boan-flesht fair Conuart
Derived from his Side, his tongue, directed by his heart,
Forthwith pronounced Woman, but a moment erst vnknoen,
So dear as flesh of his own flesh, and bone of his own boen:
Quit then, ye Gods, this Lad and let your search of Love alone:
Who will in power be felt of all, in person found of none.
Or rather is not real, but some Fancy: if not, then
Fantastical in Women, but essentially in Men.
If Love be such in Women (But mistake me not, for why?
I note them but fantastical in fault of Destiny)
Defer were then to err: When all is done that do we may,
Labor we sorrowing all the night, and sewing all the day,
The female faulty Custom yields less merit greatest pay,
And ventrous more then virtuous means does bear the bell away.
Now touching Venus (worthy such a Pheere, not such a Foe)
Vulcan, me thinks, observes well slight proof in yea and noe,
The Court therefore is well advised to Sentence not to groe.
The Gods, that did ere while but aim at Vulcans wives sons Father,
Saw Venus blush, and held that aim autentical the rather.
End Gods and Goddesses, quoth Jove, to argue to and fro:
Like good and bad is either Sex. Nay more, behold, than so,
I viewd erewhile the Destenies, and thence I thus did know.
Zimois, when Troy must perish, shall send down her Floods a Fleet,
And world it were our Father ruld when Create thought him unmeet:
But long time hence, and far Stars thence, that World shall world an I Will
Enuyrond with the Ocean waves, then famous in short while
Through often Triumphs over Foes and Traffike every wheare,
Howbeit thrice orerunne, and once a Conquest shall be theare.
*Those Changes notwithstanding they a People shall remain
Vnchased thence, and of that Streene shall Five at length re-raigne.
Dread, terrene Gods, the Fifth of those, a terrene God desse, She
Even at the fiery Trigon shall your chief Ascendant be:
Right Phoebe-like (Phoebe may like a Compeere like to her)
Retriue her named Name, to time the trial we refer.
This said, he bids adiorne the Court, and willed Mercury
Thencefoorth not to convent the Gods for such a Foolerie,
As Love, the idle Bodies work, and Surfeit of the Eye.
And thus the Queen and Tuder chat. But thought of nothing less
Then that from them Jove's noted five fated to such success
Should spring, as sprung, and part springs yet. But cease we to digress
And show we how her Son did long and lucklesse Reign possess.
CHAP. XXXII.
IT rests, fifth Henry's Son, that made the Henry's more by one,
Did in his Infancy possess his Conquering Fathers Throne,
And happily was ruled a Child, and ruled an happy man,
Till with his Parrasites his Peers and he with them began
A bloody quarrel: offering so unto the Yorkests spright
For to reclaim, in bold attempts, their discontinued right.
Richard Plantagenet the Duke of York, by VVarwicks aid,
Did get the Jail, not long enjoyed, for he in Arms decade,
Subdued by King Henry's Queen, when as by friends and force
He had in Parliament obtaynde in every clause his corse:
For, mounted thear the Kingly Throne, that Yorkish Heroes said,
Here should I speak, and shall I hope: and so his Claim conuayd
From Clarence his Progenitor, with reasons such among,
As, he Protector of the Realm, King Henry's heirs were wrung
From all Reuersion: hearts and ears did so applaud his tung.
Edward his Son then Earl of March (the Duke his Father slain)
Won, by the Earl of VVarwicks aid, in double battle Reign.
King Henry fled to Scotland, and the Queen and Prince their Son,
From France sollicet Succors, which unto their loss they won.
Henry was taken, they and their Confedrates were subdued:
Yeat still the Queen escaped, and she armor still pursued.
But, Warwick pleased, all attempts did fail to Edwards Foes:
Displeased, Edward failed, and declined Henry rose:
He crowned Either, and the same discrowned them again,
Admyrd of all, beloved of all: howbeit lastly slain
By Edward, whilst he did uphold vnchancie Henry's Reign.
So Warwick perished, Henry so refalne from Kings estate
Was reimprison'd, and his Queen did land her aids too late:
But landing, when of Barnet field she heard the luckles fate,
(Albeit Knights Lancastrians store did flock in her defense)
She stood a second Niobe, bereft of speech and sense:
And whilst the Duke of Somerset an ouer-hardie Knight,
Did bravely marshall out her force to ouer-matched fight,
Hers and King Henry's Son, the Prince of Wales a proper Lad,
In comforting his mother did continue her more sad.
Ah Son, quoth she, through often mishaps mishaps I can digest:
I fear for thee, for thee the hope that to our House does rest.
Now all are tried we can trust, if now we fail we fall:
Thy death is in the same request as is thy Fathers thrall:
And (which I would it were the worst) the Foe does thirst my life,
To end his Triumph in the deaths of Husband, Son, and Wife.
Though thy great-Grandsier, Grandsier, and thy Father won and wore
The King-ring, which thy Father hild years thirty eight and more,
Though by the cappitall Remote of Lancaster withstood,
Yeat fail prescription and discents, now lack they but our blood:
Then learn against thou prove a man (ah hardly hope I so)
The Line Lancastrian naturally does labor of that Foe.
The Queen, concluding thus in tears, did then to Armor go.
Fierce was the Field, and either part did valiantly offend:
But, Edward ouercomming, when the Battle was at end,
The Queen was carried Captive thence, And Edwards men did bring
Her Son the Prince (sole son and heir unto the captive King)
Before the Victor, whose demands receiving answers stout,
He thrusts the manly Boy from him, whom Gloucester about
The King (Churl that he was) did stab. (So tragic was the spite
Betwixt those Linages that often each others so requite.)
His death was more than death unto his Parents: but not long
His Father moned vndispatcht alike for death and wrong
By foresaid Duke of Gloucester, of whom succeedes our song.
THus won the Yorkestes ancient Reign: six bloody Fields did seat
Edward the Fourth in Englands Throne, possessed a while in queate.
He won his Subjects love, and love was debt to his deserts,
But, as must ours, so lastly his vn-bodied Soul departs.
He left his Kingdom to his Son, his Son to be protected
By Richard Duke of Gloucester, Who, piety rejected,
Grew treble-wise tyrannical, malicious to the blood
Of his deceased brothers Queen, And what so Yorkest stood
Betwixt the Scepter and himself, alive, he pricked dead,
A Foe to all Lancastrians, as the same by nature bread,
This common Deaths-man of those Kinnes, and every Nobles fall,
Whom he but gest Coriuall or might cross him near so small,
This stoope-Frog Aesops Storke, alike tyrannous unto all,
To guilty, giltlesse, friend, or foe was not secure one day,
But Either dies as eithers death might fit him any way.
Yea, even whilst his Brother ruled, when all Lancastrians, and
His Brothers twain, his Nephews twain, and Neeces three did stand
Betwixt himself and home, even then by blood he hunted Rain:
For when his own and ruthles hands King Henry's heir had ^^ayue,
Then Henry's self, Henry the sixth a giltles King in bands,
He stabd: his brother Clarence did through him, by other hands
But, now Protector, as do Wolves the Lambs protected he,
And fared as if fearing that one wickedder might be.
Queen mother and her kindred hild the Orphant King a while,
Her Kin he murdered, and from her he got the King by gile,
Whom (though uncrowned tituled fist Edward) rest his mother,
He made be murdered, with the Duke of York the younger brother
When neither Yorkest his Allies, and of Lancastrians none
Were left to let it, who should let but he might leap the Throne?
He wore indeed the wrested Palm: But yeat, to better bad,
By murder of his wife he sought new marriage to be had
With that Elizabeth that was the Eldest daughter to
Edward the fourth: But all in vain the King his Niece did wowe,
For Henry Earl of Richmonds friends such doings did undo.
Which Henry and Elizabeth by secret Agents were
Contracted, he of Lancaster, and she of York the heir:
Of which letigious Famelies here mapped be the Lines,
Even till the Heir of these two Heirs both Stocks in one combines.
CHAP. XXXIII.
Henry (as if by miracle preserved by Forraines long
From hence-ment Treasons) did arrive to right his Natives wrong:
And chiefly to Lord Stanlie, and some other Succors as
Did wish and work for better days, the Rival welcome was.
Now Richard heard that Richmond was assisted and a shore,
And like vnkenneld Cerberus the crooked Tyrant swore,
And all complexions act at once confusedly in him:
He studieth, strikes, threats, intreates, and looks mildly grim,
Mistrustfully he trusts, and he dreadingly did dare,
And forty passions in a trice in him consort and square.
But when, by his conuented force, his foes increased more,
He hastened Battle, finding his Coriuall apt therefore.
When Richmond orderly in all had battelled his aid,
Inringed by his Accomplices, their cheerful Leader said.
Now is the time and place (sweet Friends) and we the Persons be
That must give England breath, or else vnbreath for her must we.
No Tyranny is fabled, and no Tyrant was in deed
Worse tha^ our Foe, whose works will act my words if well he speed:
For ill to ills Superlatiue are easily intist,
But intertaine amendment as the Gergesites did Christ.
Be valiant then, he bids so that would not be out-bid
For courage, yeat shall honor him, though bace, that better did.
I am right heir Lancastrian, he in Yorkes destroyed right
Usurps: But, through Either ours, for neither Claim I fight,
But for our Countries long-lackt weal, for Englands peace I war:
Wherein he speed us unto whom I all Events refarre.
Mean while had furious Richard set his Armies in array,
And then, with looks even like himself, this or the like did say.
Why, Lads, shall yonder Welshman with his Straglers ouer-match?
Disdain ye not such Riualles, and defer ye their dispatch?
Shall Tuder from Plantagenet the Crown by craking snatch?
Know Richards very thoughts (he touched the Diadem he wore)
Be metal of this metal: Then believe I love it more
Than that for other law than Life to super sead my Clame,
And lesser must not be his Plea that counter-pleads the same.
The weapons ouer-tooke his words, and blows they bravely change,
When, like a Lion thirsting blood, did moody Richard range,
And made large slaughters where he went, till Richmond he espied,
Whom singling, after doubtful Swords, the valerous Tyrant died.
THus ended Englands war and woe, usurping Richard dead,
When Henry and Elizabeth uniting titles wed:
Of which two Heirs th'vndoubted Heir of either Line did cum,
The Epilogue unto these wounds, digested in this sum.
Fourth Henry first Lancastrian King put second Richard down:
Fourth Edward of the House of York re-seazd sixth Henry's Crown:
Lad-Princes twain were stabd in Field, of either Lineage one:
Four Kings did perish: Sundry times now-Kings anon were none:
Six, three of either faction, held successively the Throne:
But from the second Richard to seventh Henry we pretend
Eight Kings this Faction to begin, continue, and to end.
The Princes, Earls, Barons, and Knights this quarrel did devour
Exceed the tale of Gentry best and bacest at this hour:
So plagueth civil War, and so from Robe to Ragge does scour.
Then luckiest of the Planets wear Predominants, say we,
When by this Bedmatch either Heir that Bloud-mart did agree:
When Seventh begot the Eight, and Eight the First and Last for like
Our now Pandora: nor till her our humbled sails we strike.
For should we at her Grandsier rear our Colome, yet too poor,
We could not write (as Hercules on his) Beyond no more:
For he lackt search, our Muse has Kend an Ocean is in store,
Even matter that imports worth coparing all before.
THE SEVENTH BOOK OF ALBIONS ENGLAND.
CHAP. XXXIV.
NOw let us poste-alone to Mars and Mercury repa^r,
At least so farforth as we may without controwlment daer.
Richard the third, Henry the Seventh (last subjects of our pen)
Was slain, was crownde, with hate, with love, as worste, as best of men:
So not with York and Lancaster does wonted envy reign,
Nor can Aeneas Offsprings now of Orphansie complain.
But that Cadwalladers Fore-doomes in Tuders should effect
Was unexpected, save that God does destinies direct.
Else Owen Tuder had not wiu'd Fifth Henry's noble Queen:
Nor had they of their bodies Earls Penbrooke and Richmon seen:
Nor Margret, Somersets sole heir, to Richmon had been weade:
Nor they the heir of Lancaster, Henry the Seventh, had bread:
Nor he of Yorks Inheritrix, Elizabeth, had sped:
Nor they united either house all other titles dead.
Yeat, ear this union, Either so arrived to their right,
As Psiches on an errant sent to Hell by Venus spite.
Worse Ferrymen than Charon, Hoods contagious more than Styx,
Worse Porters than foul Cerberus were pleased, past, stood betwix.
How therefore Either dangerously their Labyrinth did pass
Shall not be ouerpassed: Thus their several fortune was.
Henry's the fourth, the fifte, and sixth successively did reign,
Until fourth Edwards sword to him did lawful Empier gain:
Lancastrians droupe, the Yorkests had their long expected day:
Sixth Henry and the Prince his son, by stabbes were made away:
The foresaid Margaret, sole heir of Somerset, erst wife
Of Tuder, Earl of Richmond, had by him a Son in life,
To whom, from her, the Crowne-right of Lancastria^s did accrewe:
He from his English foes himself by secret stealth withdrewe
To little Brutaine, wheare he found the Duke a frendfull true.
This Henry Earl of Richmond, now poor Lancasters Remain,
Was by fourth Edward practiced home by many a subtell train:
Whom once the gentle Duke (beguilde with promises vnment)
Delivered to the Englishmen, with whom he homewards went.
Forsaken Ladd for yet he was a Ladd) what did remain
But certain death, so to assure his foes uncertain rain:
Which to establish many a Prince of his Allies wear slain.
But him ear broughta-boorde, the Duke (advised better, stayed,
And him (as if by c^^^nce escapte) to Sanctuary conuaid.
The Lamb so rescued from the Wolf, that privileged place
Assured him till Edwards death, and then he hoped grace.
But he that was Protector of his murdered Nephews than
Usurped England, and became a Monsture not a man:
Richard the third omitting all his tyrannies beside)
To be possessed of the Earl by many a message tried.
Great wealth was sent, greater assum'de, but nothing might preuail:
The gracious Duke abhord to set his guiltles friend to sail:
But furnished with money, men, and armor shipped him thence
To win his right: yeat churlish Seas did let such kind pretense*
Full hardly Richmonds threatened Ship escapt our armed Shoore,
For Richard of the Rival got intelligence before.
Return'de, the Duke did sicken and Landoise did bear the sway:
And he for Masses great was brib'de Earl Henry to betray,
Yeat through wise Bishop Murtons means by stealth he scap'te away.
In travel then from Brutaine to his Grome himself was Grome,
By interchaunged raiment, till to Angers they wear come.
The French King, pitying his distress, pretended asked aid:
And secret platformes for his weal his English friends had laid.
Henry in France, at home his Friends bester them, and the Foe
Mean time with hope, with fraud, with fear imployde his wits also.
Now of the Earls conspiracy the total drift was this:
Elizabeth the daughter of fourth Edward vowed he his,
And she was vowed to him, if God with victory him bliss:
Our wounded Englands healing balm, for thus thereof ensew'de:
The factious Families unite, the Tyrant was subdew'de,
And thence the surname Tuder does Plantagenet include.
AS hardly as her husband did Elizabeth escape:
For why? like Stratageme for both did bloody Richard shape.
Whilste that her Father lived, now a King, and now exilde,
Her Crosses then did happen from such victors as wear mild.
But now the same that murdered her Brothers to be King,
That did withfraud begin and then with blood conclude each thing,
That flattred friends to serve his turn, and then destroyde the same,
That was her Uncle, yeat did hate her Mothers very name,
That thought he lived not because his Neeces wear vndead,
These now (and blame her not) in her a world of terror bread.
But of vnpriuiledged blood yet had he store to spill,
Yet sanctuaries wear not forc'te, yet but expecting ill.
Theareofte the Queen her Mother, She, and Sisters would report
Their happy and unhappy da^es, the fewer of first sort.
Happy was I (the old Queen said) when as a Maid vnweade,
Nor Husbands weal nor Childrens woe mistempered my head.
Yeat I, beloved, loved and so left that free estate,
And thought me happyer than before, for lovely was my Mate,
John Gray (a sweet Esquier for his prowysse dubbed Knight)
Was, as behooved, all my joy: who, slain in factious fight,
Your Father, Daughters, late my Lord and Husband now in earth,
From me had many a secret curse, as motyue of his death:
Lancastrian was my husband, and that faction had the wourste,
So, to relieve my Widdowhood, I kneeled to whom I curste.
Edward (for Henry was depoes'de, and Edward seaz'd the Crown)
(I wot not for what form of mine) did raise me kneeled down,
And gave me cheerful words, and took me courteously aside,
And played the ciuell Wanton, and me amorouf^y he eide:
His plea was love, my suit was Land: I plie him, he plies me:
Too baece to be his Queen, too good his Concubine to be
I did conclude: and on that point a while we disagree.
But when I was his Queen (sweet King) not for I was his Queen,
But for himself, and for the love that passed us between,
I held me happiest vnderheauen: yea, when his adverse Line
Discrowned him, I had enough that I was his, he mine.
Then, after fortunes often change, he died, and I survive
A life exceeding death for grief and greefes Superlatiue.
My heart, ah Sons, my heart (dear Hearts) was dead ear ye did die:
Too young wear ye to censure of your uncles tyranny.
Then wept she, and her daughters wept: their only talk always
Was passed joys, or present woes: nor hope they better days,
But in Earl Richmonds good success, that now a power did raise.
Too soon had Richard notice that Earl Henry would ariue,
By precontract his eldest Niece Elizabeth to wiue:
And well he knew in Yorks descent she was immediate heir,
And Henry like in Lancaster: a Match for him to fear.
Which to prevent he flattered his Neeces from their mother:
Who, fearful Ladies, did expect like deaths as had their brother.
And as they fear did he affect, which for the troubles then
Was vneffected: now behou'd to win him love of men.
Yeat casts he how he might convey to him his Neeces right,
Soon compassing his Wifes dispatch, whose life stood in his light.
Then plyes he his amazed Niece to his in^^^tious bead
Of her abhorred, She in conceit by faith fore-plighted spead.
This marrage motion gawles her more than any former grief:
Her self, Friends, Realm, Conspiracy, and all it touched in brief,
And therefore death, late feared, now she fantaseth in chief.
Mean while did Henry land, incampe, fight, and subdewe his Foe,
And, marrying her, long civil wars in England ended so.
CHAP. XXXV.
Seventh Henry's foreign busenes had succesfull honor: here
Three schol'd D^dalien Icarists (whose mounting cost them dear)
Did interrupt the peace. The first a Priests bace Puple: he
By his Complottors was pretenst'e Duke Clarence son to be.
A many of our native Peers, some foreign Princes too,
Submissiuly behight him aid in all that they might do.
The Lad was lofty, for himself he harrollized well,
At full he could his lessons, and a formale lie would tell.
For him was fought a bloody field, the Victory the Kings.
Lambert the forged Yorkest, and the Priest (that framed his wings)
Wear taken: For minority the Icarus was quit:
The Dedal^s for clergy tites was but intowr'd for it.
Thus scapte the Priest. The mother Queen to her that now was Queen
Found harder sentence for a crime more venale, as I ween,
She that did forward Henry with her friends, her purse, her wit,
That had conspired, conceil'd, concurred, for him the Crown to git,
And had him now her son in law, vnchauncy Queen, fore-went
Her whole revenues, and her age as if in durance spent:
Because against her heart, good Soul (for bootles to withstand)
See yielded all her daughters to the late Usurpers hand,
Whereby the Union might have quaild, and for it might she must
Endure such law, strict law to her of malice not unjust.
THan good old Queen Elizabeth our next young Phaeton
Had gentlier Judgment: He till then from Realm to Realm had gone,
And now in Ireland (hoping no such honor) was at Cork
Saluted by some Rebels theare for Richard Duke of York,
Fourth Edwards second son. Those Styles to him were strange, but they
Did feofe them on the bace-borne Muffe, and him as King obey.
The Yorkesh Faction (though they knew the error) let not slip
Occasion, that they now might have Lancastrians on the hip:
Margret fourth Edwards sisters heart for joy hereof did skip.
She had him soon to Burgone, and informs him cuery thing
That might concern Yorks pettegree, or apted for a King:
Maliciously repining still at Lancasters success,
And often would thus or thus-like her heart with tongue express.
God has forgot our house of York: nay York it self forgot:
To my late Brother Richards soul cleave evermore this blot:
He made away our friends to make a way unto our Foe,
To Lancaster, proud Lancaster: I, thence these tears doc floe.
Had he stock't up that hated stock, had he ra^'st out that Race,
Python had ceast, and he had been Apollo in that case.
That Henry was Lancastrian, and that Henry was alive,
And where he lived, and that he should not live if we would thr^^
He knew ywis: yeat knew he not his death how to contriue.
The Duke of Brutaine is no God, then how the devil y'ste
That both my brothers, laboring him, for whom they (...)
Their Sinons wear too simple, and their bribes but petite geere:
When had they bought him with their souls they had not bought him dear.
The heir of Lancaster (fie how it loathes to sound that name)
Enioyes the Crown: nay worse, enioyes to wife a Yorkesh Dame:
Worser, the name Plant^genet is buried in the same:
And, worst of all, their Title such as law bids us disclame.
Who would have looked such change to chance? oh how I feed like will
As Ae^as daughter Aesons house with tragedies to fill?
Who can endure to see their friends decline, their Foes ascend?
I see it, and for seeing so do wish my life had end.
When that her darling had his looer she left him to his wings:
Who flead not to worse company or at less game than Kings.
He lights in the French Kings Court, wheare (honored as the same
From whom he falsely would contriue a Crown by forged name)
He had Supplies, and English ayds, and Irish troops also,
With which he lands in England: where King Henry met the Foe.
On either part the Battle was right bloody, but at length
The King subdues, and Perke^ flead the land, dispoyld of strength.
Then, as the French, the Scotch King did repute of him: whereby
He wyu'd a Lady passing fair and of the Kings Allie,
The Earl of Huntlies daughter of the scotch-blood-royall bread:
She both before and after that her low-pris'd Mate was dead,
When well she knew his parentage, and felt his ebbed state,
In only sorrow did abound, in love no whit abate:
Howbeit in the English Court preferred to high estate.
Theare (for she was of comely parts and vncompeered face)
She, often bravely courted, yields no Courtier labored grace.
To one among'st the rest that most admierd her aunswers chaste
She said: besides the sin and that I so might live disgrac'ste,
A Presedent of wrong and woe did make me long since vow
Chastely to live the Love of him whom Fates should me allow.
I knew (quoth she) a Knight (a Knight he was in each respect)
I knew a Lady (fair she was but foully to be chect)
They loved long (if that to love and leave may love be said)
Till lastly she conceived love wheare love should be denayd.
Then he, whose Sowles Soul goddiz'd her, perceiving her untruth,
Became unlike himself, and moved, save her, each one to ruth.
At last he runs'distraught about, and what his moods conceited
He did: confusedly he wept, asked, answered, and entreated:
Ah many a time (for though his words lackt method, yeat they moved)
He had these speeches, arguments how earnestly he loved.
CHAP. XXXVI.
MY Mistress is a Paragon, the fairest fair alive:
Atrides and Aeacides for fair less fair did strive.
Her color fresh as Damask Rose, her breath as Violet,
Her body white as Iuorie, as smooth as polisht Iet,
As soft as Down, and were she down Jove might come down and kiss
A Love, so fresh, so sweet, so white, so smooth, so soft as this.
The Cleon^an Lions spoils for her I would redress.
I would the Lernan Hydras heads with sword and fire suppress.
My force the Erymantheon Bore should bravely ouermatch.
The swift-foote golden horned Stag I, running, would or^catch.
My bow the Birds of Stymphalus from wastfull prays should chase.
Of her proud Baldricke would I spoil the Amazon at Thrace.
Augeas washed Stables should my seventh Labor end.
I with the Bull of Calidon, victorious, would contend.
On horse-deuoured Diomede like honor should be won.
(...) Spanish Robber Geron should by me to death be done.
(...) ^p^ght of Spite in Hespera I golden fruit would pull.
Three-headed Cerberus in chains should make the Jury full,
B^ast, Snake, Bore, Stag, Birds, Bealt, Planks, Bull, Thief, Fruit, Dog,
Diome^d,
^hokt, feared, p^uncht, cought, pearst, prized, washed, thrown, slain, pulled,
chaned, horse* fead.
Were labors less than I would act, might I of her be spead.
D^ll mal-contented Saturn ruled the hour when I was borne:
Had Jupiter then starr'd I had not lived now forlorn:
Or Mars had steeled my milkie heart with manlier moods than thees:
Or Mercury had apted me to plead for Lovers fees:
Or Sol infused sense to search what better me behou'd:
Or Venus made me lovely, so for love to be beloved:
Or Luna (Contrary to Love) had bettered the best:
Ah, could seven Planets and twelve Signs constell one such unrest?
Then loved that Sier of Gods when he had vowed his childrens death:
That Son of his made wanton scapes with Lasses on the earth.
D^rus, ask Vulcan and his Art if thou did love or noc.
And Hermes that he Hearse loved will not disclaim I troe.
Nor w^art thou Phoebus chaste although thou wor'st a willow with.
Thou Citherea had a leash of Loves besides the Smith.
End^^ion gaynst Diana could vouch farther than the eye.
Thus loved ye all, ye churlish Stars, yeat let ye Lovers dye.
This said he, and for this he said, I for the ruth of this
Did vow, that whosoever once were mine I would be only his.
Why? these his words did savor wit, not one distraught (quoth he.)
Nay hear the rest of his unrest, it follows thus (quoth she).
Often would he kiss a senceles Tree and say, sweet Mistress mine,
I was, I am, and will be still the same and ever thine.
B^leeue me, or if so you doubt, Anatomize my brain,
And ^re my Senses see your self the Sourentesse to reign.
Believe me, or if so you doubt, rip out my heart, and see
Your self in it, in it you are and evermore will be.
Believe me, or if so you doubt, command I forthwith dye,
And see your self the only heaven whereto my soul does fly.
If such I seem and be not such, let naught betide me well.
If such I seem and be not such, I wish no heaven but hell.
If such I seem and be not such, your Favors let me mis.
With that he blessed himself and said, ah, what a wish was this?
Then steps he to some other Tree, and, as unto a friend,
Bewayles himself, with long discourse of love to little end.
And (as it were a mystery) thus many a time would tell
Of one Erickmon, as might seem, with him acquainted well.
Who would (quoth he) have thought that he had doted on a Lasses
Who rather would have thought the Girl so gilefull as she was?
Once brau'd he it and often found with silken Wenches grace:
Yeat (and I wonder) faults he not, though having time and place.
He never hild but gracious thoughts of women, yeat I win
The fairest She he ever saw might quit his thoughts of sin.
When of the Court and City both he could sufficient say,
From eithers busy Vanities he gets him away:
Amongst the woods his happiest days by-come or to be past
He found, had not Gynettas face intrapped him at last.
Nor Court nor City had she seen, yeat eithers praise she had:
So much more worth by how much less she was vnnicely clad.
At sixteen years such was she as at Twenty, and at boeth
Well worth the loving, for her loore, her face, and comely groeth.
Thence, waxing amorous, he checked his eyes that checked him so,
Which checks as often were countercheckt by Love his mightier For.
He loathed to live, that lived to love, and loved to loss, for why?
He scorned that wontlesse passion, or an amorous Fool to dye.
Full often therefore would he balke her sight that pleased him most,
And, if perceau'd to be in love, false freedom would he boast.
But all for naught, not absence or sweet exercise of wit,
Or ought besides might put aside Loves ouer-mastring fit.
Thus pyned ere he pleads love, yeat pleasing her so well
As none had fitter time and place his hearts unrest to tell,
At length he flatly says he loves, when (words too sweet for true)
Her answer was she liked him, and so atonement grew.
Then vncontroulled kisses and imbracings (often mixed
With less than love too gross, though more than should be such betwixt)
Were current: And if ever man did fish before the net,
If ever man might credit her did by her credit set,
If ever man for hearty love deserved honest meede,
Erickmon might believe himself to be beloved indeed:
More arguments of earnest love gave never Maid than she,
Less cause to falsify that love gave never Man than he.
How beit, on advantage played Gynetta all this while,
And by external smoothnes did obscure internal guile.
There was a Swayne, a wily wagge, that with his apish toys,
His Pedlarie, and pype-notes, such as pleases girls and boys,
So changed (I would have said bewitched, but that she often changed)
Gynetta, that her former love was suddenly estranged.
Erickmon hardly brok't such bace coryuing of the Swayne,
And of her love and wits did wish reducement all in vain.
Was never Girl so ouergon that had so good a wit,
So well reported of ere then, and well deserving it,
Than was Gynetta: guilty then both of her own reproof
And of her Lovers grief, that sat and sight thereat aloof.
And, were it not that she was young, and that Erickmon knew
She rather seemed than sinned in deed, he might have erred in view.
With weeping heart he her remaunds to be with him at-one:
And many restless days and nights consumes he in moan,
To think upon her madness, which her self believed none.
Her too much wronged Relict might (as well he might) be grieved,
Perhaps offended, but God knows no whit the more ^eleeu'd.
So wilful she, so wily and officious was her Squier,
That, craft intrapping craft, they both did enter bootles fire:
She bore a mind more haughty than to humble her so much,
And he a bacer mind than that he hoped his fortune such:
Yeat either liked at randon, not resolving any end,
Unless, perhaps, she dallied him as erst her former srend.
Mean while (for Apes be ever Apes) somewhat did he not well
That moved a discord, and through it their loves deuorse befell.
Er^^kmon languisht all this while not re-beloued long,
For she that ^ayl'd to do him right did feofe on him the wrong,
Who, giltles, pleads guilty (for what was it he would not
To reconcile her favor, lost might seem ere it was got?)
So love, again a foot, gave both re-intertainement hot.
Not any, loved they nere so much, seemed more to love than they,
Nor any, loved she any whit, in love made shorter stay
Than she: for he do what he could, did often times offend:
For why? even impudently she grew toyous in the end,
That was so modest comely erst as none might lier amend,
A Supersedeas for her love was every new-come friend:
And being now in much request, and waxing proud of favor,
By artificial pride she changed her natural behauour:
Her face was Maskt, her locks were ^url'd, her body pent with buske,
And (which was needles, she more sweet) her raiment sented Musk:
By all she did might seem to be unlike her self she me(...):
Yeat (worst of all) to sanctum-Sinne too aptly is she bent:
Erickmon when that followed her unpitied, not v^pearst,
Reformed his wits, his suit and hope of her, not now as erst,
And scornd her mind that scornd his love to her so firmly geason,
For why^shee offered double wrong to wrong and scorn a reason.
Thus whilst he hopt he hild her least: so alters the ca^e
With such as she: Ah such it is to build on such a face.
This said he, and for this he said, I for the ruth of this
Did vow that who so once were mine I would be only his.
Why? this concerned not him, nor showed a man distraught, (quoth he)
Nay hear the rest of his unrest, it follows thus (quoth she.)
Then (sheading tears) he to the Tree so spoken to would say,
Was not Gynetta false that did Erickmon so betray?
But has my Mistress cause to change? what cause, think you, should moue^
I framed me hers, she fayn'd her mine, my love is ever love.
May ye fair face prove one foul botch, those shining eyes prove bleard
That sweet breath stench, like proof to all that fair or sweet appeard
In her that wrongs her true-loue: let her loathed ever lust,
Beg may she, and vnpittied pine, rot, perish on the dust,
And, dead, be damned, that unto her true-loue is unjust.
Ye men say all Amen, or if amend your selves ye must,
Curse not (this Madman said) but swear that women be untrue,
Their love is but a Mummerie, or as an Aprils dew,
Got with a toy, gone with a toy: gifts, flattrie, gawdes, or wine
Will make her check and fly to game less fair, perhaps, than thine,
More amorous than men, and men convey their love less fine.
If such they are (as such they are) and will be whilst they be,
Why am I then so true of love? because not borne a she.
Witlackst thou then (fond Fool). I sigh to say true aim you give.
Wheare grew that lack (fond Fool?) I sigh to say wheare now I live.
Whence grew that lack (fond Fool?) I sigh to say from joys removed.
When grew that lack (fond Fool?) I sigh to say when first I loved.
And do thou love? ah, too too well I wot I love indeed.
Why do thou love? with luck too ill I love for lovers meede.
Whom dosst thou love? ah, too too well I wot a lovely She.
What time in love? with luck too ill in love too long for me.
Wit lackst thou then that wilfully do err and nourish it.
Wit do I lack, not wilfully, then blame not will but wit.
How shall I do? my Heart is lost, and I am left in woe:
Met any man a poor tame Heart? the Heart, good Folk, I owe:
Stricken, maimed, all of gore, and drouping does it go.
A Less once favored, or at least did seem to favor it,
And fosterd up my frollicke Heart with many a pleasing bit:
She lodged him near her Bower, whence he loved not to gad,
But waxed cranke, for why? no Heart a sweeter Layer had.
But whether that some other Dear estranged her or not,
Or that of course her game is Change, my Heart lackt brouse I wot:
Despysd, displeasde, and quite disgrac'st, my Heart even to this day
Dislodged, wandering, woe begone, I wot not wheare does stray.
But see, ah see, I see how Love casts off Desire his Hound,
A fell fleet Dog that hunts my Heart by parsee each-wheare found.
Sweet Cynthea rate the eger Curte, and so thy foe prevent:
For lo a far my chased Heart imboste and almost spent.
Thanks, gentle Goddess: now the Lad pursues a bootles chase:
My Heart recouers Covert wheare the Hound cannot hold pace.
Now tappas closely, silly Heart, vnrowse not and so live:
The Huntsmans-self is blind, the Hound at Loss does ouer-giue.
But list, alas, Loves Beagles be vncoupeld, Beauty praites
And drives my Heart from out the thicks, and at Recei^ awaites
Vaine-hope, and either now falls in, and now my Heart must dye,
Now have they him at Bay, and now in vay^e he fights to fly.
Auaunt Desire, ha Curre, auaunt: the Bore so raze thy hyde.
Unto the fall of my poor Heart see see how Love does ride.
Hark how he blows his death: ah see, he now the Say does take
Of my poor Heart, that never more for Love shall pastime make.
Thus lived he till he left his life, and for the ruth of this
I vowed, that who so once were mine I would be only his.
Yeat (said her Suitor) he, not she, was punished, as may seem.
Yes yes, quoth she, a Conscience pricked is deeply plagued I deem.
Then Scotland warred on England, and in that same wa^re did end
The Knight that had coryued: so the Lady lost each friend.
Often saw I her in tears, and often I heard her to complain
For faith erst lost, for loss now sound, dividing ^^ghs in twain.
There be that say (if truly said) unbodied Souls have walked,
And of the Ghosts of these two Knights the like abroad was talked:
Her ears had this and she the heart that dared not her eyes,
For thither whence the brute did grow she (feareles faulty) hies:
Her Followers stood aloof when she, alone approaching, said,
Beloved both, what means this fight? (they seemed as if they srayd)
Ah, pardon me, sweet First-belou'd, my guile I grant was great,
So is my grief: My latter Love refrain, let me entreat.
But whilst she spoke of deadly wounds they both did seem to fall,
And after vanished, leaving her perplexed in fear not small:
Who thenceforth fared as the Knight that did for her distraught,
Still haunted of the Ghosts, and haunts ye place where they had faught,
Until of her despayring life her self the Period wraught.
Thus lived she till she left her life, and for the ruth of this
I vowed, that who so once were mine I would be only his.
Tush, this was but a Fantasy, quoth he, of subtle Feends,
Deluding her simplicity in figure of her friends:
I hear not that they said or did aught taching her untruth,
But foolish was her fear, the like I censure of your ruth.
And shall I tell what they did tell, and say what they did do?
I will, for so, perhaps, you will surcease (quoth she) to wowe.
The Ghost resembling him to whom she had disloyal bin
Said: I, and This, and thou be thus and shall be doomed for sin:
For dotage in my love, for his deceitful lust, wetwaine
Of freesh-sore wounds do hourly faint, hurt, heal, heal, hurt again:
Nor can I utter half we see, and fear, and suffer still
Of endless Torments: only thou art Auctresse of such ill.
Who love beloved believe no life but wheare their love does live,
To fault is then their murdrous fact that first defect does give.
He had not faulted or I fallen had thou hild faith to me:
Ah little feel we in regard of Plagues prepared for thee.
Thus said he, and for thus he said I for the ruth of this
Did vow, that who so once were mine I would be only his.
Well, Madame, quoth the Gentleman, be this so, or a shift,
I see to frustrate my demand is honestly your drift:
Then so, or not so, or what so you shall infer of this
It matters not, Perkin is yours and be you only his.
For, sooth to say, wear all said false, it were indeed a hell
To have a Loues-Coryuall, and as none could brook it well,
So none should ask, and none should yield to alter love begun,
Therefore, sweet Lady, I conclude such il is well vndun:
Mine amorous suit has here an end: and would you might prevail
With Perken too, that proudly strives to bear too high a sail:
So may you, if perhaps you have for him so apta tail
As this you told to me for me, although more hardly true
As this which I shall tell, that does include a moral view
Of matter worth the note for him, the rather told by you:
Then hear it: for our leisure and the order of my Q*
CHAP. XXXVII.
SVppose (for so must be supposed) that Birds and Beasts did speak:
The Cuckooe sometimes loved the Owl and so with her did break.
Then flew the Owl by day, so did the Cuckooe all the year,
So did the Swallow and the Batte: but how it happened hear.
The Cu^kooe by the Swallow (then the Swallow was his Page)
Did send the Owl a sucking Mouse, a tydie for the age:
The Bat (the Bat then served the Owl) preferred the Bringer and
The Present to her Mistres sight; that in her Tod did stand.
My master to your Owleship, quoth the Swallow, sends by me
This Modicum, desiring you to take the same in gree.
The Owl, that never till that day had tasted flesh of Mouse,
Had quickly lopte a Limb or two, and feasteth in her house
The Swallow with a cut see of her then disgorged wheat:
When, talking of the dainty flesh and elswhat, as they eat,
The Bat (then waiting at the boorde) fetched sighs a two or three:
The Owl did ask the cause. And do you ask the cause, quoth she,
Why thus I sigh when thus in sight my kindred murthred be?
My self was sometimes such, and such am still, save now I fly:
With that she freshly wept: and thus proceeded by and by.
A fresh, quoth she, now comes to mind mine Ancestors ill hap,
Who^ pride made prays to Kestrels, Kites, Cats, Weasels, Bae^, and trap:
My Grandsier (for wheare Nature fails in strength she adds in wit)
Was full of Science: But, insooth, he misapplied it.
The Weasell, Prince of Vermen (though besides a virtuous Beast)
By shrewdnes of my Grandsiers wit his Holes with hoords increaste,
And seemed to conn him thanks, who^ none besides had cause to thank:
For Princes Favors often make the favored too cranke.
Not only Mice, but Lobsters, Cats, and noble Vermen paid
In coming Coram Nobis for some crime against them laid.
But, God, it is a world to see, when purposes be sped,
How Princes, having fatted Such, are with their fatnes fed:
