The Weasel served my Grandsier so and every Vermen laughed
To see himself in Snare that had in Snares so many caught.
Now also live some wily Beasts, and fatly do they feed
Among Beasts of chase and birds of game, with less then needful heed.
My Graundsier dead, my Father was in favor nerthelesse:
Nor did his Father more than he for high Promotion press.
And (though I say it) long time he deserved favors well,
For quayling Foe men, and at home such Vermen as rebel:
And for the same the Weasell did him mightily prefer:
But Honors made him haughty, and his haughtines to err.
I will be plain, he waxt too proud, and plotted higher drifts
Than fitted him or fadged well, for who have thriu'd by shifts?
Nor will I say (because his son) he wronged the Weasell, but
The Weasell died, and that that did succeed to shifts he put:
For which his Fathers Fortune did oretake him at the last:
Such fickelnesse in earthly pomp, which, flowing ebs as fast.
This double warning might have i^kt unto my wit, but I
Did follow Kind: Nay, more, I did importune Dis to fly,
And he did give me these black wings, resembling him that gave the
A proper Gift, and hardly got, to shame me now I have them.
But know ye Dis? some Pluto him or Limbos God do call:
Or, aptlier said, in Hell of devils the Chief and Principal:
And somewhat now of him and how I changed say I shall.
I happened on a Cranny, whilst my Mouse-daies lasted, which
I entering, wandered crooked Nookes and paths as dark as pitch:
Theare, having lost my self, I sought the open air in vain,
Both wanting food, and light, and life well near through travels pain
The Moole by chance did cross my way, and (as ye know) her smell
Supplies her want of sight and serves her purpose full as well:
I heard a tracting sownd and, skar'd, my hair did stand upright,
Nor could I see, or fly, but fear and bless me from a Spright:
She had me, hild me, questions of my being theare the cause,
And in mean while peruseth me with favorable claws.
I was about to plead for life, when she preuents me thus:
Ha, Cosen Mouse, what Fortune gives this meeting here to us.
Fear not my Son (I call thee Son because I love thee much)
Do hold thy self as merry here as in a Pantlers hutch:
What know not me? or see thou not? with that she leads me
Into an higher room, wheare her to be mine Eame I see.
I did my duty, and my heart was lightened when mine eye
Encountered a friend whereas I made account to die.
Before me sets She Viands, and my stomach served me well:
And, having fed, my Grandsiers and my Fathers ends I tell,
(For She enquires for them, ere I acquaint her what befell.)
The reverent Moole, then sighing, said: ah, let no Vermin think
That Fortune ever favors, or that friends will never shrink:
I did fore-smell their lostie flight would cost them once a fall,
And therefore, Cosen, see thou be forewarned therewithal.
Here see thou me (I tell thee, though I prise not Gentry now,
Thine Eame and of the elder house) that long ago did vow
My self a Recluse from the world, and, celled under ground,
Least that the gould, the precious stones and pleasures here be found
Might happen to corrupt my mind, for blindness did I pray,
And so contemplatiuely here I with contentment stay.
Admit the Weasell graceth thee, the more he does the more
The other Vermen will malign and envy thee therefore:
Himself, perhaps, will listen to thy ruin for thy store:
Or thou thy self, to mount thy self, may run thy self a shore.
That Vermen that has reason, and his own Defects espies,
Does seem to have a soul, at least does thrive by such surmies,
For what is it but reason that human from brutish tries?
But man, or beast, neither has troth that this for true denies,
He has enough that has wherewith pure Nature to suffies:
In overplus an ouercharge for soul and body lies,
For Soldiers, Lawyers, Carrions, Thieves, or Casualties a Prize,
His comber-minde that lives with it and leaves it when he dies,
From whom to catch it scarce his heir stays closing of his eyes:
O wretched wealth, which whosoever wants no Fortune him enuies.
Here may thou feast thee with a Mad: and here no Pickethanke pries
Into thy life, nor words well spoke to ill vnmeant applies:
No Flatterer to vndermind: no tongue no ear for lies:
No gleaning from the Orphant: no oppressed widows cries:
No bribes to give, no hands to take: no quarrelling for flies:
No wrongs to right: no laws to break, because no law that ties,
But what we lust we do, nor do nor lust badd enterprise:
And find less want in Nature, than wits-want in Arts disguize.
Nor any here in force, in friends, fraud, wealth, or wit affies:
O do thou not so rich, so safe, and just life despies:
Theare lacks not of noble Births to star the courtly skies.
Theare be enowe Politians, thou may for thy Soul be wise:
Then leave thou matters of estate to States, I thee advise:
And rather sit thou safely still, than for a fall to rise.
Not for she was my Elder or mine Eame, but for the place
I hild my peace, that would have said her Moolships mind was bace.
But she perceiues me to dissent, and says, Cosen Mouse,
Do as you like, you shall not find a prison of my house:
Stay while you will, go when you will, come and return at pleasure,
And ever welcome: Virtue is an uncompelled Treasure.
This past, and thence pass we through deep dark ways, save here and theare
The veins of gould and precious stones made light in dark appear:
Vaste Vaults as large as Isles we pass, great Rivers theare did flow,
Huge worms and Mo^sters there I saw, which none on earth do know.
On go we, till I saw a glimps and she heard noise of flame,
Then said she prayers, bidding me to bless me from the same.
I, musing, frain'd her meaning: She her meaning thus did tell.
That flaming Region, ever such (quoth she) is Plutos Hell:
All gould, all metals, wealth, and pomp that nourish Mortals pride
Are hence and his, and hither they do theare Mis-guiders gide:
He them inchaunteth, and the same inchaunt the folk on Earth,
Until their dying dotage theare finds here a living death.
Still nertheles I wished to see the hellish Monarch Dis,
When he (more ready to be found then for our profit is)
Ore-heard us, and vnhid himself, and shinde in rich array,
And seemed a glorious Angel, and full gently thus did say.
That slandrous blind bace-minded Moole, friend Mouse, deceiues thee much,
And prates of me, of Hell, and Earth more than is so or such:
Believe him not, but rather do believe thine eyes, and see
If any earthly pleasure is vntripl'd here with me.
Then shewde he sights (which since I found illusions to betray)
Of greater worth than Earth affords, or I have Art to say:
Nay, more, he bids me ask what so I would, and I should have it:
Then did I pause, bethinking what was rarest I might crave it.
My Holes were stored with corn and croomes, on Earth I walked at will,
And in her Bowels now had seen indifferently my fill,
Upon it, nor within it, not sufficing to my pride,
I asked wings, scarce asked when they grew on either side.
Short leave I took, and mounting left the Hell God and the Moole,
And soared to the open Air through many a sorry hoole.
It was at Twilight, and the Birds were gone to roust, but I
(Inchaunted with the novelty of flight) vnweared fly,
And had the Sun been up, I ween (such pride bewitched my wit
To Egel-fie my self) I had assayd to soar to it:
Not seeing that my limber wings were Leather-like vnplum'de,
But at the Dawning also I of wing-worke still presum'de.
The Swallow (and I ween it was this Sallowes father) he
Was earliest up, with him I met, and he admired me.
I hild him wing, and wistly he suruaies me round about,
And lastly, knowing who I was, did give me many a flout,
And fled to tell the other birds, what uncouth Foul was bred,
Who flockt to see me, till with gibes and girds I wished me ded.
Then, shifting out of sight, I hung till Twilight in a hoole,
Transformde, derided, hunger-spent, and (minding still the Moole)
In vain I wished reducement of my shape, and (which was worste)
My hap was harder than to own in that distress a Cruft.
Then fled I to my wonted Holes of hoorded food to get,
Too narrow by mine added wings that did mine entry let.
Now Mise fled me, not to the Moole I would return for shame,
To Dis I durst not, mong'st the Birds I was a laughing game.
Then cursed I mine aspiring mind, then knew I Dis a Devil,
The Devil the Prince of Pride, and Pride the root of every evil.
Hell, Earth, Air, Heaven, and what not? then conspiring mine unrest,
What might remain but death for me that lived so unblessed?
But as I, fainting, flew that night your Ladyship, Dame Owl,
Did call me to your Todd, and glad to see a new night-fowle,
Did take me to your service, thence your Chamberlain to be:
Ha Jupiter reward it you that so relieved me.
It is a sweet continual feast to live content I see:
No danger but in high estate, none envy mean degree.
Then all this process (quoth the Owl) does tend, belike, to this,
That I should eat no Mouse-flesh: Nay, Sir Bat, so sweet it is
That thou, so near of Kin to them, shall also serve my lust:
And there withal in ruthles claws the hapless Bat she trust.
Here meant the Courtior to have left, whom Perkens Lady prays
To tell what end such wowing had: And thus hereof he says.
The Swallow saw that cruel pranke and flies aloof and said,
Ungrateful Glutton, what offense has that thy Servant made?
Choke may thou with the murder So he left her, and unto
The Cuckooe tells what the Owl unto the Bat'did do.
Varlet (he waxed choleric) and what of that, quoth he?
Was not the Bat her bond-Slaue, such as thou art now to me?
What tel'st me then of other news then what her answer is
Unto mine amorous Message, says my Lady nay or yis?
The Swallow told him that through such occurrant of the Bas.
He, interrupted, came away vnaunswered in that.
A mischief, quoth he, both on that and thee ill sauoured Elfe:
And in a stammering chafe he fled to wowe the Owl himself.
The Swallow Man's him thither, whom the deu'lish Owl did hate,
And all because he had reprou'd her tyranny of late.
The Cuckooe, offering to have bilde, she coyely turnde her face,
It Is more, quoth she, than needs that we kiss, as stands the case:
Rid hence yonn same your knauish Page, you sent him with a Mouse
To spy my secrets, or belike to brave me in my house.
Gods precious, would you knew I bear a mind less bace then that
I can digest your Drudge with me so saucely should chat:
Jack Napes, forsooth, did chase because I eat my Slave the Bat.
O what a world is this that we can nothing private have
Vncensur'd of our Servants, though the simplest Gill or Knave?
Well, rid him of your service: Nay, it skils not if of life,
At least if so you mean that we shall love as man and wife,
For such Colecarriers in an house are ever hatching strife.
The Cuckooe, hearing this complaint, flew on his trusty Page,
And vndiscreatly gave him strokes that killed him in that rage.
Yeat, ear he left his life, he thus unto his Master said:
Thus many honest Servants in their Masters hasty brayd
Are Dog-like handled, either yeat like dear in Jove's just eyes:
Of Harlots and of hastines beware, said he, and dies.
When now her gluttony and spite had thus dispatched twain,
The Cuckooe, plying amorously her favor to obtain,
Even then, and looking very big, in came the Buszard, who
Did swear that he would kill and slay, I mary would he do,
If any Swad besides himself fair Madam Owl did wowe.
The Cuckooe, seeing him so bog, waxt also wondrous wroth:
But thus the Owl did stint the strife. She calls them husbands both:
Now fie (quoth she) if so you could betwixt your selves agree,
Ye both should have your bellies full, and it no hurt to me.
The Buszard faintly did consent, the Cuckooe said Amen:
And so was Hen enough for Cock, not Cock enough for Hen,
For she deceyues them both, and had besides them other game:
The Gende Buszard dying soon for sorrow of the same.
The Cuckooe wisely saw it and did say but little to it,
As nooting she was set on it and knowing she would do it.
But what the Swallow warned him of Harlots proved true,
For, as was gessed, also him by treachery she slew.
The Goddess Pallas, to give end unto these tragic deeds,
Descended, and (the dead revived) to Sentence thus proceeds.
The Bat, because begild of Dis, See pittieth partly and
Permits him Twy-light flight: to give thereby to understand
That to aspire is lawful, if betwixt a Mean it stand.
The Swallow, for that he was true and slain for saying well,
She doomb'd a joyful Summers Bird, in Winter time to dwell
Even with Mineruas secret store, as learned Clerks do tell.
The Buszard, for he doted more and dared less than reason,
Through blind bace Love enduring wrong reuengeable in season,
She eie-blur'd, and adjudged Prays the dastard'st and least geason.
Unto the Cuckooe, ouerkinde to brook Coriuals, she
Adiudg'd a Spring-times changeles note, and whilst his young ones be
By others hatched, to name and shame himself in every Tree.
But live, quoth she, unto the Owl ashamed of the light,
Be wondered at of Birds by day, fly, filch, and howl all night,
Have lazy wings, be ever lean, in fullen corners rucke,
When thou art seen be thought a sign to folk of evil luck:
Nor shall thine odious form, vile Witch, be longer on my Shield:
Whence racing forth her Figure, so the Goddess left the field.
Just Guerdons for Ambition, for poor Souls oppressed for well,
For dastard Dotards, Wittolrie, and Harlots nice you tell,
Said Perkens Wife. But thus now of her husbands pride befell.
At last when sundry Arms had end, Henry victorious still,
And Perkens passage was fore-stald, he yields of his own will
Himself from Churches Privilege to Henry's Mercy, who
Did only limitte his abode, and less he could not do.
But when he sought escapes he then had petite punishment,
And after, for some new attempts, to Tower was he sent:
Whence practicing escape, it was said, he won to his intent
Young Edward Earl of Warwick, that indeed was Clarenes son,
And ever had been Prisner theare ear Henry's reign begone,
And now by law, too strickt me seems, for this to death was don.
Perken was hanged, and hang may such: but that the Earl should die
Some thought hard law, save that it stood with present policy.
Sir William Stanley did for this (often King-law is do thus)
Deserving better of the King: but what is that to us?
The last of our three Phaetons was tuter'd of a Friar:
Who, being fitted now by Art and nature to aspier,
(The foresaid name of Warwick feigned) seduced folk thereby:
As I my self in Essex heard and saw a Traitor dye
That feigned himself sixth Edward, and to grace-out such his lie,
Looks, body, words, and gesture seemed heroycall, to view
He had like age, like marks, and all that might enforce it true:
Whereby to him assisting minds of simple Folk he drew.
Our Cowle-mans foresaid Actor so prevailed that the Friar
In Pulpets durst affirm him King, and Aids for him requier:
But lastly both were taken: both did fault in one same ill,
Yeat rope-law had the Youth, the Friar lived Clergie-knaued still.
When Armor ended Avarice began (for then begins
The slye Mercuriles^, and more by wyles then valor wins.)
Beneuolences, Taxes and sore Fines for penal laws,
To Henry hoords from Henry hearts of many a Subject draws.
Empson and Dudly (fur'd Esquiers more harmefull, being gown'd,
To Englands friends than Englands foes, through Avarice profound)
In such exacting chiefly Act, applaused of the King,
To whom their civil Thefts, not Thrifts, exceeding wealth did bring.
Yeat when the gracious King found out their tacking Rich and Poor,
He then did pardon much, and much did purpose to restore:
But, dying, those two Harpies lost their hated heads therefore.
So hardly favored of Kings themselves in bownes contain,
That they, securely stout, at length do perish through disdain.
So hardly too some Princes are from private Lucar won,
As, though their bags ore-flow, they think no harm abroad undone.
Henry (acquit his latter days of Avarice fore named)
Deceast for Prowess, Policy, and Justice highly famed.
THE EIGHT BOOK OF ALBIONS ENGLAND.
CHAP. XXXIV.
EIGHT Henry (heir indubitate of York and Lancaster)
Succeeded, and with Kingly rites his Father did interr.
His mind, his words, his looks, his gaet, his lynaments, and Stature,
Wear such for Majesty's as showed a King composed by Nature.
All Subjects now of civil strife, all counter-minds for Reign,
All envious of his Empier now wear rid, wear pleased, or slain.
Rich wear his sundry Tryumphs: but his cost had foyzen than
When Terwin and strong Turnay in resisting France he wan:
When Maximilian Emperor did under Henry fight:
When English Ships did often put the French Sea-powre to flight:
And that the French King was inforc'st to crave and buy his peace,
Who, wiuing lovely Mary, so the wars for then did cease.
This sister to our King, and then the French Kings goodly Queen,
Was welcomed with Tryumphs such as erst in France unseen.
Iustes, Barriers, Tylts, and Turneyes were proclaimed each wheare for All:
Whereforeto Paris at the time flockt Caueliers full tall,
With Princes brave, and Ladies fair of every Realm about,
And hence, with more, Charles Brandon, in fine Chivalry most stout,
Whose body fitted to his mind, whose mind was puesant, and
Whose puesance yielded not to Mars, this Mars in France did la^d:
With who^ incou^tred valiant knights, but none might him withstand.
The English-French Queen standing theare, admired for beauty rare,
Behild the Tryumphs, in the which high Feats performed ware.
But Brandon (yet no Duke) he was the Knight above the rest
That in her eye (nor did she err) acquitted him the best.
For whether that he trots, or turns, or bounds, his barded Steed,
Did run at Tylt, at Randon, or did cast a Spear with heed,
Or fight at Barriers, he in all did most her fancy feed.
Weak on a Couch her King lay theare, who^ though she loved well,
Yeat likte she Brandon, and the same loved her ere this befell:
For chastely had they fancied long before she came to France,
Or that from mean estate to Duke Henry did him advance.
The days of Triumph wear expired, and English Peers with praise
Come home, and Jews King of France decea'st within few days.
Charles Brandon, Duke of Suffolk then, with honor furnished hence,
Was sent to France for to return the widow Queen from thence,
Who had been wed scarce th^ice three weeks unto a sickly King,
To her, a fair young Queen, therefore small time might solace bring.
Yet less did timetha^ brave Duke Charles assuage fair Mary's grief:
He chats, she cheers, he courts, she coyes, he wowes, she yields in brief.
No winds (thought she) assist those Sails that seek no certain Shore,
Nor find they constant lives that but they live respect no more.
Let each ones life aim some one end: as, if it be to marry,
Then see, hear, love, and soon conclude, it betters not to tarry.
To cast too many doubts (thought she) wear often to err no less
Than to be rash: And thus, no doubt, the gentle Queen did guess,
That seeing This or That at first or last had likelihood,
A man so much a manly Man wear dastardly withstood:
Then Kisses reuel'd on their Lips to eithers equal good,
And, least King Henry should dissent, they secretly did wead,
And then sollicet his good will, and of their wishes spead.
The periut'd valiant Scotch-King James, slain at brave Flodons Slaughter,
Had also left in widowhood Englands fair elder Daughter.
She also weds a Scottish Earl, vnlicenc'st of her Brother:
And was to her Sons Daughters Son, now sixth James, great-Grandmother.
A Scruple, after twenty years, did enter Henry's mind,
For wedding of Queen Katherin, a Lady fair and kind,
Spaynes Daughter, then the Emperors Aunt, and for her virtuous life
Well worthy Henry: But for she had been his Brothers wife,
And also of their coiture surmise directed Laws,
He seemed in conscience touched, and sought to rid him of the Cause.
Then was the matter of Deuorse through Christendom disputed,
The Match of all adjudged void, and so the Queen non-suted.
She, after tears to him from whom she was to be deuorste,
Did humbly say: and am I not, my Lord, to be remorste?
That twenty years have been your Wife, and borne your Children, and
Have loved and lived obediently, and unsuspected stand.
I am (ah too too sweetly erred) I was, poor Soul, the same
Whom once you did prefer, nor now of me you need to shame.
The blossoms of my beauty was your Booty, nor my favor
Now alters so to alter so from me your late behauour.
But Conscience is the color of this quarrel: well I wot
I also have a conscience that in this accuses not:
But as the same, perhaps, might say that me succeedes say I,
That for the pleasure of a Prince go many things awry.
Which her Fore-doomes seemed to effect in her that her succeeded,
In Queen Anne Bullyn: who, for she in Luther^sme proceeded,
Was hated of the Papists, and enui'd because preferred,
And through the Kings too light belief (for Kings have sometimes erred)
She lost her head, and might have said (some thought) ere she did dye,
That for the pleasure of the Prince go many things awry.
So did the gracious Mother of our now most glorious Queen,
Whose zeal in reverent Fox his works autenticall is seen.
The Kings four other Queens (for why? he did a Sexamus)
Shall pass, though Jane did bear a Son to him, a King to us,
Edward the Sixth: and of the same we shall deliver thus.
CHAP. XXXV.
SVCH as was Love in Figure of Ascanius, when the same
In kisses sly did sheade himself into the Tyrian Dame,
Or such as was sweet Hyacint, Apollos lovely Boy,
Or Jupiters Ganymaedes, rapt up to heaven from Troy,
Or rather like young Solomon, in sentencing betwixt
Two mothers claiming one same Child, was young Edward the Sixth.
Now Rome fell sick in England, but how long she lay in trance
We list not write, alonly death to her did never chance:
For old Rome never lackt that durst their lives for her bestoe,
Not new Rome that to Hell for her dare souls and bodies go.
Then true Religion might be said with us in Primative,
The Preachers and the people both then practiuely did thrive:
Our decent Church-Rites, still in print, not practice (worthy those
Whose reverent heads collected them from whence true wisedo^ groes.
Not mangled then of Nouesses and curious Doults, which now
Would have they know not what, and would reform they know not how,
Omitting or admitting as their own Conceits allow)
Did then put forth her Branches, and wear fruitful in the bood:
And, wear our Church-Lords now for zeal as Church-lawes now for good,
Soon might like union be, now by indifferency withstood:
For give to vulgar Heads the head and look for all confused,
At once they publish and repeal, al else, save Order, used:
And as Kytts Camp ill-form'd good form at their reforming Tree,
Sons ost by aim consorting voice their Fathers hanged should be,
So wheare the Multitude prevail they censure ere they see.
But (might I be so bold to speak to them should speak to me)
A good example would do good in Churchmen, seeing they
In saying ^^oth are less believed, not doing as the say.
I know our Churchmen know that Faith is dead where lack good works.
Yeat know I not what policy in Alms vnpreached lurks.
Some teach (and well) that these concur, but few do urge the Theme
Of charity: affeard, perhaps, our More should urge their Beam.
But fear not, Fathers, preach at full Love, good works, and Remorse,
More will your bad Examples let than shall your words enforce,
To preach by halfes is to be worse than those tongue-holly Iauells,
That cite good words, but shift off works and Discipline by Cauells.
Often have ye handled pithily (not preached without need)
What good to give, what hurt to take, from those that Souls do feed:
But so obscurely has been blancht of good works elsewheare done,
As many, boasting only Faith, Faiths fruits selfe-aptly shonne:
When such a faith is but the faith of that faith-fruitles Devil
That cited Scripture unto Christ, applying good to evil.
Tell whether that the Leuite or Samaritane did better:
Tell wherein Dives lived and did to Lazarus a Debtor.
Vnknot sententious Solomon his Parable which is,
Full Clouds will rain upon the Earth: How thus is meant by this,
Rich me^ by Clouds, poor men by Earth (else Clerks expound amiss)
Tell, how some Clouds but misell Rain: that is, if so they give
A penny Alms or twain a year they think they much relieve.
Some Clouds flash down their Shewres: that is, some set up two or three,
And beggar so themselves and theirs: say such are foolish free.
Some Clouds hayle down their Rain, beat flat, hurt, and help not ye ground:
That is, vpbraid who^ they relieve, and hold the^ servile bound.
Some Clouds give Snow, that lights and lies a moisture moystles: so
Do those that say, alas, God help, and nothing else bestoe.
Some Clouds do shewre into the Seas: say such do give to such
Whom Alms make idle, or belike to recompense as much.
Some Clouds with lightning, thunder, and loud winds drip down their rain:
That is, give sildom alms, and those proclaimed and seen, ere ^ayne.
Some Clouds retain but form of clouds, with figure black as Coal:
That is, look big, Examine long, but Scriptum est their doale.
Some gracious Clouds shed temprate Shewres on thirsty earth indeed.
That is, the Orphant, Widow, Thrall, succor, protect and feed.
Say also whatsoere we give, to whosoere it be,
Though given in sight of men, if not because that men should see,
But with Devotion, as a work from Faith that cannot sever,
God for such cheerful Alms will be our bounteous Almner ever.
Say, make not as it wear a Quest of quere ere Ye give,
But give ye Alms as men be poor, not as poor men to live.
Provided common Beggars nor disordered Lossels, who
Men know provided for, or can but labor none will do,
Than whom do say (for so is sooth) no Creatures worse desatue,
Take you no Orators for them, but that they hang or statue.
And thus for this. Our Ouerture to it reduces me.
The Uncles ofthis Orphant King, so long as they agree,
Vphild Religion, King, themselves, and Realm in happy state:
Which then began to ruinate when they begun debate.
CHAP. XL.
SOme say their falling out was through two haughty women's strife,
The Admiralls Queen Bigama and Lord Protectors wife:
These (for what glory enuies not one woman in an other?)
Began a brawle that ended in the blood of either Brother.
It Is thought the Earl of Warwick threw close fuel to this fire,
And nourisht it to cut off them, that so he might aspire:
For when the one had lost his head, he forthwith took in hand
To forge the Lord Protector false unto the King and Land:
Who, though he tried by his Peers of treason was acquitted,
Yeat also of a Statute new he being then indighted,
Was hardly found a Felon, and too stricktly sentenc'st so:
For meanest fault is high offense urged of a mighty Foe:
The King thus lost his Uncles both, to his no little woe.
Now Warwick was become a Duke, feared of high and low,
Full little thinking that himself the next to Block should go:
The two Prince-loyall Semers erst made let unto his lust,
But now remained none whose faith or force he did mistrust.
The Orphant King fell sick (but here suspend what some suspect)
The new Duke of Northumberland mean while did all direct.
It was contrived King Edward from his Sisters gave the Crown,
Their Fathers Former Act and Will by wrested law put down:
The Sisters Daughters Daughter of Eight Henry, Lady Jane,
Was published heir apparent, and that right from Mary taken,
And from Elizabeth, though both collatrally preceed her:
And when by full Confedracie the Crowne-right was decreed her,
And Gylford Dudly fourth-borne Son unto Northumberland
Had married her, and nothing seemed the Plot-forme to withstand,
King Edward (entered seauenteene years of age, and seven of Reign)
Departed to that endless rest his virtuous life did gain.
The Council then convent. But who will think, perhaps, that one
Should alter All, to alter true Discents unto a Throne?
^ane, Suffolks Daughter, Gylfords wife (One worthy such estate
For righteous and religious life, who nerethelesse should wate
Her Intrest after others Two, The younger of which twain
Did match, yea Mate her virtues) was proclaimed Queen to reign,
And in the tower of London hild Estate and princely Train.
Mean while fled Mary, doubting less her Scepters loss than life:
But sildome fayles a rightful cause that comes to open strife:
The Commons knew our either Law preferred a Sisters Right
Before a Cosens, and fot it did many fadge to fight.
Northumberland with Arms pursued the Lady Mary, and
Observed directions from the Peers: who when they understand
Of Mary's strength of flocking Friends, on sudden came to pas
That they proclaimed Mary Queen, and Jane her Prisner was:
And well was he, that late did seem a Foe, might first salute
The Queen, and all unto the Duke did their Amiss impute.
For, soothly, more the Peers did fear than favor always,
Who, though he seemed as forward now in Mary's cause as they,
Yeat was he taken and in-towr'd, and lost his head for this:
A Warrior brave. But than his Sier, himself, one Son of his,
Like rare Politians seldom liu'de: who in three several Reigns
Successively did show them such, though loss did prove their gains.
THe Duke thus dead, Suffolk, Lord Gray, Lord Gylford, Lady Jane,
Wear executed: But we blanch the rest excepting twain,
That is, Lord Gylford and his wife, young and less worthy blame,
Because the Dukes their Fathers, all the Council, all of name,
Yea and King Edwards Pattents sealed for them, not they, did frame
What so was done in this, yeat they must perish for the same.
Who higher then this Couple late? and who more wretched now?
Of more then much remained naught, nor law did life allow.
Unhappy Youths, not for they die, but for the mutual grief
Of him for her, of her for him, which tortured them in chief.
Come was the day, the tragic day, wherein they both should die,
When Either, passing to their end, each other did epsy,
She in her lodging, waiting death prepared her that day,
And he in being lead thereto he Lodging in his way.
Assending and dissending Signs then fly and fall apace,
And each bemones the other more than minds their private case.
Their E^es, that looked Love ere while, now look their last adieu,
And stain their faces, faultles ere this dismal enter-vew:
Their Ears, erst listening ioies, are deaf, unless to sighs profound:
Their tongs, erst talking ioies, those looks and sighs did now confound:
What parts soere of them had felt or tasted joys ere this,
Wheare senceles now of any joy, save hope of heavenly blis.
Whilst Either thus for Earthly Pomp no longer time did look,
He passes to the fatal Block, she praying on her book:
Wence (having made a godly end) he was returned, whilst She
Prepared for like, and of her Lord the senceles Tronke did see:
A sight more deathful than her death that should consort him strait,
And for the which her feareles eyes did every moment wait.
She vnabashed, mounting now the Skaffold, theare attends
The fatal Stroke-and unto God her better part commends:
And as she lived a virtuous life so virtuously she ends.
CHAP. XLI.
OMitting Knights, three Dukes, three Lords, also a Queen elect
Then perished thus, and somewhat some of Edwards death suspect.
All which fell out a Stratagem, in God his secre Dome,
That should induce a Tragedy to England meant by Rome:
For when these mighty Protestants, through Ones Ambition, fell,
Queen Mary seemed to shut up Heaven, and set wide open Hell.
Whence swarmed papish Tyrants, that false doctrine did erect,
Whilst that seduced Mary did Gods threatened cause neglect.
Blameless she was not, for a Crown that could her Foes ore-goe:
Nor all too blame, for mighty States do and have erred so,
To Whom the Scriptures wear obscured by Christ's Italian Foe.
Her courage was not common, yeat abused over much
By Papists chiefly, She her self too naturally such.
Here hence she is reproved of a most tyrannous Reign,
And of a thriftles Marriage with the trustles King of Spain.
But when rich Brabants supreme Fair, the Bakers daughter, staide
The King in dalliance, and the Queen had news that false he plaide.
It Is thought his tarriance grieved, and told that one should counsel this
Have patience, Madam, so it was and will be as it is:
Fourth Edward did the like, yeat loved his Queen no whit the less:
Nor did the like impatient her, that knew him to transgress
As guilty of a Leash of Loves, Shores wife and other twain:
She knew as Streams, if stopped, surrownd so Kings will show they reign:
As did our second Henry, whom his Queen often crossed in vain^.
Which, and one other Story, if it please you that I tell,
I shall. Yea doc (quoth she:) Then thus (quoth he) it once befell.
NOt known of Rosamund, his eye had stoed her in his heart:
Fair Maid, quoth he, believe me fair and all so fair thou art
That, wear I Henry Engla^ds King, thou should be Engla^ds Queen:
But so must fail, for Elenour already is between.
He bod me buy thy love, if so it might be bought with Gold,
If not, he bod me swear he loves: in faith he loves, be bold:
He bod me ask, if so he came, what should his welcome be,
And if, perhaps, he lated wear if he should lodge with thee:
Protesting secreasie thereof to all, unless to me,
With promise to perform at full each promise as I make it:
I promise Love, Wealth, Secrecy, then promise thou to take it.
Content you Sir (quoth To (amund) you aim your marks amiss:
I am not for his Highness, nor for me his Highness is.
And should he know (I shame he should) of this your Brokage bace,
He would acquaint you what it wear your Sovereign to disgrace.
Whosoever you be, be still the same, or better if you may,
Think not Lord Cliffords daughter will vn-maiden her for pay,
But know, if Henryes self were here, himself should have a nay.
Then know (quoth he) which being known, well may thou know I love thee,
I am the King, and for I am the rather let it move thee.
In sooth, sweet Wench, thou say nay thou know not whereto:
For, wear my wish at work, less good wy wish than work would do.
What, fearst thou shame? no shame to be beloved of a King:
Or dread'st thou sin? The Pope for pay absolueth every thing?
Or doubt'st thou jealous Elenour? I will remove that doubt:
At Woodstock shall she find thy bower, but never find thee out:
Theare shall thou pass a pleasant life, commanding me and mine:
Then love, beloved Rosamund, a King subjects him thine.
He kissed, She blushed, and long it was ere love from her he wroung,
For, whilst up played in her heart, it paused on her tongue.
Not Sibils Cave at Cuma, nor the Labyrinth in Creat
Was like the Bower of Rosamund, for intricate and great.
The Pellicane theare neasts his Bird and sporteth often with her,
Conducted by a Clew of thread, else could he not but err.
Besides her Maids, a Knight of trust attended on her theare,
Who suffered for her Beauty, long concealing it for fear:
At length at full and formally he courted her for grace,
But all in vain, naught booted him to have both time and place:
Henry, quoth she, begone and he shall end my thoughts unchaste:
Nor peach't she him, nor he, dismissed, did hold himself disgrac'st.
The Kings three Sons had notice of their Fathers Leiman now,
So had the Queen, and the yof such coriuing disallowe.
Came I from France Queen Dowager, quoth she, to pay so dear
For bringing him so great a wealth as to be Cuckquean'd here?
Am I so old a woman, he so young a wanton grown,
As that I may not please, that pleased, and still might with his owen?
What is the Drab, or tempting Devil? or wherefore doteth he?
The French King once, himself even now, for fair preferred me.
And has he toyled up his Game? and settels he to love her?
Nor Heaven nor hell shall cross my course but that I will remove her.
Like Phrogne, seeking Philomel, she seeks for and found
The Bower that lodged her Husbands Love, built partly under ground:
She entered, but so intricate wear Turnings to and fro,
That welneere she had lost her self, but could not find her Foe:
Yeat out she got, and back she goes with her Attendants, who
Admire their furious Mistress, and mislike what she would do.
With her Confedrates often she went, prevented of her will,
Howbeit lastly did prevail: For hap did hit so ill,
That whilst the Knight did issue out, suspecting no assaut,
He was assailed, and from his giding Clewe they caught.
So won they unto Rosamund: Whom when the Queen did view,
Most bravely clad in rich Attire (her self more rich of hew)
The beauty and the brauenes of the Person and the place
Amazed her and hers, who stood at gaze a certain space.
No marvel, quoth the Queen, that often the Court did mis the King,
Soon such an Hebe hither such a Jupiter might bring:
Now, trust me, wear she not a Whore, or anies Whore but his,
She should be pardoned: But in faith I must not pardon this.
A Queen coriuall with a Queen? Nay kept at Rack and Manger?
A Husband to his honest bed through her become a Stranger?
Abide who list, abye she shall, how so I buy the danger.
Fair Rosamund surprised thus, ear thus she did suspect,
Fell on her humble Knees, and did her fearful hands erect:
She blush't out beauty, whilst the tears did wash her pleasing face,
And begged Pardon, meriting no less of common grace.
So farforth as it lay in me I did, quoth she, withstand,
But what may not so great a King by means or force command?
And daerst thou Minion, quoth the Queen, thus article to me?
That then were Nonplus when the King commenced Lust to thee:
Nay, best he take thee to the Court, Be thou his Queen, do call
Me to attendance, if his Lust may stand for law in all:
I know it, Strumpet, so harps he, and thou do hope the same:
But lo I live, and live I will, at least to mar that game.
With that she dashed her on the Lips, so dyed double red:
Hard was the heart that gave the blow, soft were those lips that bled.
Then forc'st she her to swallow down (prepared for that intent)
A poisned Potion: which dispatched, from whence they came they went.
The wronged Wench, the Quintessence of Beauty, and the same
(Save that intised of a King) stood free from all defame,
Did forthwith sicken, so that help for her might none be found,
When to the Knight that guarded her, then grieved of a wound,
She said: Wear it that Henry knew his Rosamund wear thus,
No weighty busnes might withhold but he would visit us:
Full well I loved and love him still, that should not love him so,
And for I should not worthily I labor of this woe.
Ah Beauty, that betraies thy self to every amorous Eye,
To trap thy proud Possessors what is it but Wantons try?
Wheare-through it seldom haps the Fair from meant deceits to fly:
At least the nicest Fair alive shall vanish once as I.
Vain Beauty stoop to Virtue, for this latter is for ever,
Wheareas that former altreth with every Air and Fever.
I pray the Queen of Pardon, whom I pardon from my heart:
Farewell my present Friends: But thou, sweet King, wheare so thou art,
Ten thousand times farewell to thee: My God, whom I offended,
Vouch safe me Mercy: Saying which, her life she sweetly ended.
Thus did fair Rose (no longer Rose, nor fair, in sent, or sight)
Whom pensive Henry did inter, and soon her wrong did right,
The Queen imprisoned and his Sons, rebelling, put to flight:
Thus wrought they sorrows to themselves in wreaking of their spite,
Nor loved the King thenceforth the Queen, or left to err anew.
Now rests our other promised Tale, a common Tale (if true)
Like less had harmed Elenour, and more may profit you:
Be bitter and it betters not, be patient and subdue:
King Phillip is not gone but to return, which when he shall,
Your Majesty must not exclaim if so you would recall:
Impatience changes smoke to flame, but Jealousy is Hell:
Some wives, by Patience, have reduc'st ill husbands to live well,
As did this Lady of an Earl, of whom I now shall tell.
CHAP. XLII.
AN Earl (quoth he) had wedded, loved, was loved, and lived long
Full true to his fair Countess, yeat at last he did her wrong:
Once hunted he, until the Chase, long fasting, and the heat
Did house him in a peakish Graunge within a Forest great:
Wheare, Known, and welcomed (as the place and persons might afforde)
Brown bread, whig, bacon, curds, and milk were set him on the Board:
A Cushion made of Lists, a Stool half backed with a houpe
Wear brought him, and he sits down besides a sorry Coupe.
The poor old Couple wished their bread were wheat, their whig were Perry,
Their baco^ beef, their milk and curds wear cream to make him merry.
Mean while (in Russet neatly clad, with linen white as Swanne,
Her self more white, save rosy wheare the ruddy color ran,
Whom naked Nature, not the Ayders of Art, made to excel)
The Good man's Daughter sterres to see that all were feat and well:
The Earl did mark her, and admire such Beauty theare to dwell.
Yeat falls he to their homely Fare, and hild him at a feast:
But as his hunger slaked so an amorous heat increast.
When this Repast was past, and thanks, and welcome too, he said
Unto his Oste and Ostesse, in the hearing of the Maid:
Ye know (quoth he) that I am Lord of this and many Towns,
I also know that you be poor, and I can spare you pownes,
So will I, so ye will consent that yonder Less and I
May bargain for her love, at least do give me leave to try:
Who needs to know it? Nay who dares into my doings pry?
First they mislike, yeat at the length for lucar were mislead,
And then the gamesome Earl did wowe the Damsel for his bead.
He took her in his arms, as yet so coyish to be kissed
As Mayds that know themselves beloved and yeeldingly resist:
In few, his offers wear so large she lastly did consent,
With whom he lodged all that night, and early home he went.
He took occasion often times in such a sort to hunt,
Whom when his Lady often mist, contrary to his wont,
And lastly was informed of his amorous haunt elsewheare,
It grieved her not a little, though she seemed it well to bear.
And thus she reasons with her self: Some fault perhaps in me,
Some what is done that so he does: Alas, what may it be?
How may I win him to my self? He is a Man, and men
Have imperfections: It behooues we pardon Nature then.
To check him wear to make him check, although he now were cha'ste:
A man controuled of his Wife to her makes lesser haste.
If duty then or daliance may prevail to alter him,
I will be duetifull, and make my self for daliance trim.
So was she, and so lovingly did entertain her Lord,
As fayrer or more faultles none could be for Bed or Board.
Yeat still he loves his Leiman, and did still pursue that Game,
Suspecting nothing less then that his Lady knew the same:
Wherefore, to make him know she knew, she this devise did frame.
When long she had been wronged, and sought the foresaid means in vain,
She rides to the simple Graunge but with a slender Train:
She lights, enters, greets them well, and then did look about her:
The guilty household, knowing her, did wish themselves without her,
Yeat, for she looked merrily, the less they did misdoubther.
When she had seen the beauteous Wench (the^ blushing fairnes fairer)
Such Beauty made the Countess hold them both excused the rather.
Who would not bite at such a Bait? (thought she) and who (though loath)
So poor a Wench, but gold might tempt? sweet errors lead the^ both:
Scarce one of twenty that has bragged of proffered Gold denied
Or of such yielding Beauty baulkt but (ten to one) has lied.
Thus thought she: And she thus declares her cause of coming thither:
My Lord, often hunting in these Parts, through travel, night, or wether,
Has often lodged in your House, I thank you for the same,
For why? it does him jolly ease to lie so near his Game:
But for you have not Furniture, beseeming such a Guest,
I bring his own, and come my self to see his lodging dressed.
With that two Sumpters were discharged, in which were Hangings brave,
Silk Couerings, Curtens, Carpets, Plate, and all such turn should have:
When all was hansomly disposed, She prays them to have caer
That nothing hap in their default that might his health impaer.
And, Damsel, quoth she, (for it seems this household is but three,
And for thy Parents Age that this shall chiefly rest on thee)
Do me that good, else would to God he hither come no more.
So took she horse, and ere she went bestowed gould good store.
Full little thought the County that his Countess had done so,
Who, now returned from far affairs, did to his Sweetheart go.
No sooner set he foot within the late deformed Cote,
But that the formal change of things his woondring Eyes did Note*
But when he knew those goods to be his proper goods (though late,
Scarce taking leave) he home returns the Matter to debate.
The Countess was a bed, and he with her his lodging took:
Sir, welcome home (quoth she) this Night for you I did not look.
Then did he question her of such his Stuff bestowed so.
Forsooth (quoth she) because I did your Love and Lodging knoe,
Your Love to be a proper Wench, your Lodging nothing less,
I hild it for your health the house more decently to dress.
Well wot I, notwithstanding her, your Lordship loves me,
And greater hope to hold you such by queat then brawles I see:
Then for my duty, your delight, and to retain your favor,
All done I did, and patiently expect your better Hauour.
Her Patience, Wit, and Answer wrought his gentle tears to fall,
When (kissing her a score of times) Amend, sweet wise, I shall
He said, and did it: And your Grace may Phillip so recall.
But he (whosoever he was) that thus had dubled Tales to cease
Queen Mary's grief, for Phillips guile, as well had hild his peace:
Her no persuading might dissuade from pe^siuenes of heart,
Until that his Unkindness in her Deaths-Scene acts it part.
But howsoere or whatsoere her cause of death might seem,
Her death did many a good man's life from Tyranny redeem:
For, as in Passion, so was she in Papistrie extreme.
And were not the abodement bad at her to end our Theme,
Our Cattle unto stronger draughts we should and would vnteame,
But to the Head* land shall our Plough, unless we break our Beam.
Yeat ere we ear to it (for it shall be our Holly-day)
Of somewhat said and somewhat escaped rests thus much by the way.
CHAP. XLIIII.
BEFORE we touched (and little else) what Courses hapt at home,
But now, in few, at foreign Acts of Native Kings we rome:
Of Bri^tish and of English Kings, more famous than the rest,
This sparing Catalogue ensewes: whose deeds we thus digest.
* Ae^eas Offspring famous Brute did set from Greekish thrall
Six thousand Phryg^an Knights; by him did Guyan* Guffer fall:
He conquering this I Will, his Name unto his Conquest gave:
And of his Cornish Cambries men courageous yet we have.
* Torkes Builder Ebranke that subdu'de the ^imbrians and the Gawles,
And built the best of Scottish Towns, next in our Number falls.
*When Brennus and Belynus had Eight spacious Kingdoms won,
Had slain two Consulls, sacked Rome, and matchles Arms had done,
And built ten Cities, best that be in Italy this day,
Those Kingly Brothers, as must all, their debt to Nature pay.
* G^rg^^nus slew the Dactan King: won Tribute: and the same
Gave Spanish Exiles Ireland, whence our Scottish Nation came.
* C^ssi^^lane did twice beat back from British Seas and Shore
The worthy Coesar, that but then was victor evermore:
And thirdly had prevailed but for Luds revolted Son,
When as brave Nennius hand to hand of Caesar honor won.
* Guydar and Aruiragus won of Claudius Caesar spoil:
The former in a second Field did s^out Vaspasian foil.
*When as the wandering Scots and Pichts King Mar^us had subdude,
He gave the Livers dwellings, less than where they since intru'de.
* Constanti^s, wedding Coyls heir, was Monarke of the West,
Who, with this Islands Scepter, of Romes Empire was possessed.
*Great Constantine, that worthily a Worthy might be said,
The British Roman Emperor, throughout the world obayd,
He made his Siege Bizantium, that retains his name ere since,
And made (but so unwitting marde) the Priest of Rome a Prince.
* Maximian as Imperial and as valerous as any,
With British armor did subdue both Kings and Kingdoms many.
*What speak we of great Arthur, of his Chivalry or Court?
Precelling all, sole President of virtue prow's and port,
A King of many Kings, his Knights in all Exploits were seen,
He was in deed a Worthy, and the Worthiest of the Neene.
*Five Crowns King Malgo prized. *And in Battles fifty five
Against the Mis^reants valiantly did King Alured thrive:
^ollo (whose Seed should conquer us) he hence did bravely beat:
That, ma^ger France, in Normandy his Scythian Troops did seat*
*He that re-monarchiz'd our I Will King Athelstone did slay
Six Kings, twelve Dukes, and countlesse tale of Heathen in one day:
The one of Nine, his Knight Sir Guy, we touch but by the way:
Omitting other Kings and Knights, too long in few to say,
Of British race a many, and of Saxon Princes some,
Whose blood by Normaine Mixture now is tripartite become:
O^ (For, perhaps, from such Consort the Brutes casseerd will be)
Three blended bloods of Nations three has given us Natures three,
The Saxon prowess, Dan^sh pompes, and Normaine Pollicee:
And of the Romans and the Pic^ts we are no portion small:
Four of which Nations Scythia bred, we thriuing in them all.
*KIng William, Englands Conqueror, from Rollo sixth, with pray
Of twice five hundred Towns in France vn-^o-met sayld away.
* Henry the second, upon whom the Scotch-King tendant was,
(Which Scots their often overthrows we henceforth ouerpasse,
Who to our Kings, Lords Parramounts, not wars but vprores bring)
Spoylde France, won Ireland, and deceast of Judah chosen King.
*Next Lyon-harted Richard he won Cypris, Siria, and
Jerusalem, debelling quite the Sowldan from his Land:
He skalde the strong Egyptian Oste, and king'd his Sisters son,
And plagued France and Austrich for the wrongs they had him don.
*First Edward made the Turks, Sauoies, the French, and Flemings tremble.
*The third so named to them and more did Mars himself resemble:
Whose Knights, in 2. Richards days, so tickeld France, and Spain,
And parts Lugdinian, that no King but Richard seemed to rain:
Ten thousand were his household: Scotch digests we here disdain.
*The fourth and *fifth of Henry's were as actious as the rest:
Especially the latter was the foremost with the best.
*Not yeat Fourth Edwards honor from his Ancestors digrest.
On these do vulgar Ears and Eyes so brimlie wait and gaze,
As they distaske our private Pen notorious Lauds to blaze.
Our Catalogue omitteth some for Arts and Justice good,
Some natur'de well, advised ill, some worthy Laethe flood:
Not one forecited but deserves at least an Homers Muse,
Although with Agamemnons Veil Apelles shift I use:
But colors to that Painter, Art unto that Poet none
So good, to paint and praise at full our following Crowns, save one,
Since Tuders Seed, Henry the Seventh, arrived Englands Throne.
NOr superstitiously I speak, but H. the letter still
Might be observed ominous to Englands good or ill.
First, Hercules, Hesione, and Hellen were the cause
Of war to Troy, Aeneas Seed becoming so Out laws.
Humbar the Hu^n with foreign Arms did first the Brutes inuaide.
Hellen to Romes Imperial Throne the British Crown conuaide.
Hengest and Horsus first did plant the Saxons in this I Will.
Hungar and Hubba first brought Danes that swayed here long while
At Harold had the Saxon end: at Hardiknought, the Dane.
Henry's the first and second did restore the English Rain.
Fourth Henry first to Lancaster did Englands Crown obtain.
Henry litigious Lancaster and York unites in peace.
Henry the Eight did happily Romes Irreligion cease:
The Father of our Mother Nurse, our common Joys increase.
Which double H. and H. H. here our homely Poemes Lee,
He save that salueth all our sins: And, God, voutsafe thou me
A prosperous Course in sailing through the Ocean deep and large
Of her now-Highnes Scepter, for I here assume that Charge.
TO THE RIGHT HONORABLE LORD, SIR GEORGE CAREY Knight, Baron of Hunsdon, etc.
THough late I wright, too soon I wot, I here Occasion take,
(May this to none, wish I, but me Retreat to Sorrow make)
Both to remember Hi^ We meant our Penn-Lee tore-use,
As to trans-patronize from him to you mine orphant Muse.
Not one alive that shall not die, thrice happy then the same
That, as your Noble Father, shael survive them selves in Fame.
Both Epitaphes and Elegies his Ghost has many an one:
Alas, what can I say not said, or more than moend him moan?
Not matter more than much, nor more for truth than matter skante,
Or less of Glory than of both, would to his Poet want:
But, were a Nonplus of his Praise, all were but this in gross,
His Good to his own Soul is Good, his Death to us is Loss.
What should I add his ancient Creasts, and noble Petegrae?
Such as that Henry (Primer of you Hunsdon Barons) be
Your Lordshippe, to your Country, Prince, and such in high Degree*
And such as you, when you are not, succeed the Careys blood
In stiil-Descents: and than this wish how may I wish more good*
My Syrinx sounded first to you, and may ear long anewe:
B^ you Mecaenas also to this Poesy does ensewe.
His had it been, had he yet been, that had the rest, which now
Yields humbly yours, if it for yours your Lordshippe shall allow.
Most humbly your Honors, William Warner.

THE NINTH BOOK OF ALBIONS ENGLAND.
CHAP. XLIIII.
AVert your eyes and ears, all ye, that envy her the fame
Of more renowned Regment than our fluent Thoughts can name.
Ye that conceit such Poemes as more learned not conceive,
Read not the rest, but silently even at this line do leave.
Ridled Poesies, and those significantly flow,
Differ in ears as do in mouths the Apricock and Slow.
Physicians bylles not Patients but Apothecaries know:
Some modern Poets with themselves be hardly inward so:
Not intellectiuely to write is learnedly they troe:
Whereby they hit Capacities, as blind-man hits the Croe.
Nor Those, not These, fear thou, my Muse, but mildly sing the prays
Of these our present times, less gross than those of elder days.
Our world has made it course that as the Moon does wax and waine
From gold to silver, then to iron, and now to gold again.
Of whose faire-cured Leaprosie from former twain to gold,
(For in a Quintessence was all ear Gods worlds-curse of old)
The vndeluding Alcumist is that Elizabeth
Whom English, yea and Alients, hold a Goddess on the Earth.
Elizabeth by Peace, by war, for Majesty for Mild,
Enriched, feared, honored, Loved, at well meant Toys has smilde,
Let then Mnemosines control, as well they may our Muse,
We shall such grace wheare we transgress for Supersedias use.
Phoebus, thine humbled Phaeton this second boon does ask,
'That thou would give, and jointly guide, to, and with me, thy Task:
Infuse Apollo too into thy self, thy self in me:
Yeat then our mighty Subject threats will much omission be.
But what implore I Fictions? that I well imploye my pen,
Eternal God, say thou (which thou all-only canste) Amen.
When Jupiter and Arcas young (the Sier, and Son) had clos'de
Saturnus in Italian Hell, not to be baylde suppos'de,
Then Proserpin, black Dis his wife, inlargd the Wrangler thence,
To vex her Sister, cross her Siers and Brothers sownd pretense.
Then she, even she whom Cynthia some, and some do Pallas call,
(Yt skils not whether, Eithers lawdes do aptly her befall)
By Saturn, Dis, and Proserpin, giltles molested long,
Unto the God y'clip't, I-Am, thus motioneth her wrong.
O mightiest Mighty, that of naught createdst all that is,
And gauste to Man (thy noblest Work) him self, and all for his,
And, save Obedience, did impose on him no other Fine,
And, disobaying, did redeem his Loss with decrest Thine,
Which self same Thine, and still same-Oures, I also intercesse,
For thee professing being wronged, to have at length redress.
Scarce this was said when thus ensewd, the Three wear rapted hence.
Saturn and Dis confinde their Hells: Proserpin her offense,
Grown through Mis-Guides, Venial perhaps, we censure in suspense,
And fair, loved, feared, Elizabeth here Goddiz'd ever sense.
For me to wreste from Hercules his Club as easy wear,
As in the Ocean of her fame, with choysest sails, to bear
That freight, yt with the India^ wealth may more then much compare.
Yeat how The Italian Feends did freat and hitherto inuaye
Against thy glory, gracious Saint, wear ouerlong to say.
But he that of a Prisnor thee so great a Prince did frame,
Thy loving, and beloved God, to thee is still the same.
King Phillips-selfe (so doting on his Pope-created Crewe,
That, he it suffering, his own Son and heir those Locusts slew,
Because they feared he would prove unto the Gospel true,)
Even Phillip, now that raketh hell for rascal brybed Skomes
To rid thee hence (indignities that badly him becomes)
Once had thee, then not having power to do thee any harm.
It is the work of God (let Rome vncursse, let Spain vn-arme)
That thou art Queen, to plant his word, when we could hope it least,
When Ours, and all the Kings had cast their Crowns before the beast,
When greedely the Priests of Baal did for thy ruin gape,
Thou did, vnharmde, the Lions den and fiery Oven escape.
Even the^ when Tryumphs were in hand, bells ronge, and bonfires made,
Because Queen Mary of a son delivered was said,
Even then, I say, God mortifide that womb, to bring to pass,
That unto thee, his Queene-elect, no Issue letting was:
That soon Religion and our Realm might welcome wished peace:
May thou, the Cause thereof, survive long after my decease.
But if it be his will, to whom are all Events fore-knowne,
That Papistrie shall in our Land again erect her Throne,
Let Spaniards, or what Tyrants else, be Masters here, so they
Take also with our Land our Lives, and rid us out the way.
For not they only die, but die in lingering Torments, who
Fault to their Inquisition, o^ their Damned Rytes must do:
Then better Bodies perish than should souls miscarrie too:
Of neither which has Rome or Spain remorse, but thristing Sway,
Regard not whom, by whom, or how, they treacherously berray:
How often have they, the rest ore past, subborn'd our Queens decay?
By wars, wiles, witchcrafts, daggers, dags, Pope, poison, and what not?
To her have they attempted death, for liewe the Traitors lot:
Vouchsafe, O God, those loves of thine be neuermore forgot.
Was never any thryued yet that threatened her amiss:
For of anointed Princes God sole Judge and Rector is.
And if Examples might prevail, then Traitors might perceive,
They perish in their purpose, or but Spyders webbes do weaue.
And here occasion apteth that we catalogue awhile,
And unto English Dukes, from first to last, address our Style:
Though numbers greater, and as great of power, did pride begyle,
Yeat in this one degree observe what headding and exile:
If then such Mighties felt Gods frown, shall Meaners hope his smile?
CHAP. XLV.
EDward, surnamed the black Prince, and third Edwards eldest son,
Third Thomas Duke of Norffolke, This did end, and That begone,
From Normaine Williams Conqueste here of Dukes the Title hie:
Of forty seven, but twenty two a natural death did die.
Edward ye Duke of York, that lead fifth Henry's Vaward Knightly,
Then slain at Agencourt with fame, we ouerpasse him lightly:
Nor Humfrey Duke of Gloucester here catalogue we rightly:
Nor William Duke of Suffolk, who, exilde, on seas was met,
And, hated, headded: howbeit sixth Henry's Fauoret:
Nor Edmund Duke of Somerset, that Henry's loved Peer:
Nor Humfrey Duke of Buckingham, to that same King as dear:
Nor sweet young Richard, Duke of York, of Glosters kyn too near:
We also two Plantagenets, both Dukes of Clarence clear,
Of which was Thomas slain in France, George drowned in Malmsie here:
For though these Eight remembered Dukes deceast not in their beads,
Yeat none of these had traytrous hearts that forfetted their heads:
Howbeit, otherwise, of these wear some that erred so,
That, stomack't for such Errors, did their ends untimely groe.
Nor was the Duke of Ireland, Vere, in second Richards time,
A Traitor, but a Parasite, which proud no less a crime:
He as ambitious, as he was envious, and envied,
Was banished by the Nobles, and in banishment he died.
Mowbreie of Norffolke (right or wrong) by foresaid Richard was
Exiled, and in Italy did hence to nature pas.
When second Richard was deposde, and Henry wore the Crown,
Two Hollands, to restore the one and put the other down,
Conspiring, wear detected, and at Cercester they both,
Of Excester, and Surrey Dukes, found death in Vulgar wroth.
Richard Plantagenet, the Duke of York, fourth Edwards father,
Sought to depose fixed Henry, and was heard therein the rather,
For that to him by law-Descents the Scepter did acrew:
But they compound, howbeit wars were reard, and him they slew.
John Holland, Duke of Excester, fourth Edwards sentence fleade,
And was long after in the sea suspuicusly found dead.
Then Henry, Duke of Somerset, rebelling lost his head.
Of Edmund, Duke of Somerset, the like is also read.
So Henry, Duke of Buckingham, third Richards friend-foe speade.
And that tyrannous Richard, Duke of Gloucester, his Reign
Usurped was suppressed, he for blood paid blood again.
John, Duke of Norffolk, fought, and fell at Bosworth field. His son
Duke Thomas, in Eight Henry's days, did heading hardly shonne,
Adiudg'd perpetual Durance, in Queen Mary's reign vn-donne.
Wolsey, that slye, officious, and too Lordly Cardnall, wrought
(Who could and would effect his will) that to the block was brought,
Eight Henry reigning, Edward Duke of Buckingham, whose end
That Prelate ioyde, the people moend, because his foe, their friend.
Seimer, the Duke of Somerset, envied more than reason,
By one that came the next to Block, though mighty at that season,
Sixth Edward in Minority, did, quit although of treason.
No sooner Marie got the crown, more liklie to be gone,
But John, Duke of Northumberland, beheaded was anon,
Who wrought to set the Diedeme Jane Dudlies head upon.
Her Father Henry Gray, the Duke of Suffolk, was also
A friend unto that Faction, and the Spanish Wowers foe:
But by that act he lost his head, as did too many more.
In blessed Queen Elizabeth her happy Reign of peace,
The Tragedies, and Tytles too, of English Dukes did cease,
Which Thomas, Duke of Norffolke, last Tragedian, did increase.
All these wear mighty in their times: yeat being Malcontents,
Both they, and hundreds powerful Peers, for like found like Events:
Howbeit of the common wealth none worser did desarue,
Than such as flattred Princes faults, who faulting, all did swerve.
Which, in their Policy, the States of Hell did then foresee,
When as they joined others, Aids, unto the Furies three,
Narcissus Shadow, and the Voice of Echo: than which twain,
How fabulous so-ere it seem, naught has or more does baine:
Wherefore to such as know them not we either thus explain.
CHAP. XLVI.
WHeare Cadmus, old Agenors son, did rest and plant his Reign,
Narcissus (of his Of spring) theare for beauty fame did gain.
His Mother was Lyriope, fair Thetis fairer Daughter,
Whom cheifest as the choicest wowde, and brave Cephisus caught her.
Boeotia was the fertile Realm, Parnassus Plain the place
Whear this admired Youth was borne, this Lass-Lad form and face,
No Nymph so fair but wished him hers, howbeit all in vain:
His self-love wrought his selfe-losse, and his beauty proved his baine,
Who, proud of Natures plenty, hild all others in disdain:
Till God, who had created Man the fairest Creature,
(Howbeit but a shadow of his proper Feature,
More differing far than Sunshine from the Suns selfe-substance pure)
Narcissus ouer-scornfull pride not longer would endure,
But from his form, that pleased him most, his plague did thus procure.
As this same fond selfe-pleasing Youth stood at a Fountaynes brym,
And proudly sees his shadow theare, admiring every lym,
Echo, an amiable Nymph, long amorous of him,
But loving, vnbeloued, now, at least to please her Eye,
Conuaies her self, unseen, into a Thicket joining by,
And thear, as much ore-gone with love, as he ore-gone with pride,
She hears, and sees, and would have pleased three Senses more beside
And nothing more than every part, thus stealth-seene, liked her,
And nothing less, than hidden with vnhidden to confer,
For well it had contented then in more then sight to err,
Although not meanly did his scorn against it her stomach sterre,
Mean while the Lad (such power has pride men's Senses to subdue)
Doats on his Shadow, now supposed to be a Substance true:
And lastly wowes so formally in words and gestures sweet,
That Echo found his error: and, he saying, Let us meet,
Let Us meet, quoth Echo, mockingly: which, hearing, he with speed,
(Believing that his shadow was a Nymph, and spoke in deed,)
Did leap into the Fountain, whear that Gallant, drowning thus,
Has left example how like pride may cause like plague to us.
How smooth-tongu'd Echo, that for him in all, save voice, did pine,
To quit his scorn, baind other Fools, alike vainglorious fine,
By soothing them, is Nas^es tale, no purpose here of mine:
But how Narcissus shadow and this Ecchos voice, though they
Have long been dead, haunt now the World, is it we mean to say.
PLuto, Minos, Radamant, and all the infernal States,
Did pytch a Session, to correct Remisnes in debates:
Devising Orders that on Earth might more Disorders be.
Tisiphone, Alecto, and Megaera, these same three
Wear shrewdly checked, because it was objected, though untruly,
That they wear idle, Hell lack't Guests, and men on Earth waxt ruly.
The hellish Potentates therefore a new Commission framed,
Narcissus ghost, and Ecchos voice, therein of Quorum named.
These twain, and those three Furies thus, Copattentees, leave Hell,
And diversely throughout the Earth to Soule-infecting fell.
Leave we those others, laboring their Mischiefs far and near,
Whilst Echo and Narcissus are more badly busy here.
Crab'd Saturn, and too-boystrous Mars, direct those Formers matter,
Soft Venus, and smooth Mercury, give Method to these latter:
Those Furies roughly do effect their tragic Taskes, these Twoe
So slyely work, that sweetly men their proper sorrows wowe.
Heer-hence our gold-imbased World in view and value fayld,
For Echo and Narcissus much in many things preuayld.
Hence is it some of high estate themselves do ouer-like,
Whilst deadlier wound them Ecchoing tongues than should a foe them strike.
Hence, whence dishonorde, some of them believe of men belyed
Their glozing Grooms, as Tyrants so by them they wrong envied.
Hence oftentimes Authority looks biglier than a Bull,
With Suitors poor too sternely quick, in helping them too dull.
Hence both in preaching, harboring, and humility, it is
Some Prelates sooth, be soothed, lead, and they be lead amiss.
Hence Law, sometimes, as formde of wax, through new refined wit
Of Judge or Pleador, alters sans certainty in it.
Hence Martialists in Discipline and ordering their war,
Less happily, the ancient use, conceitedly, do bar.
Hence is it that the Lawyers more affect their fluent Tail,
Than what is right, or whom they wrong, or how they speed, or fail.
That Lawyer though who more by Art than right does ouerthroe,
Consents to sin, deceiues the Judge, wrongs Right, is Justice foe.
Hence flattred Gentry proudly does degenerat at last:
And some would be believed such, that of no Gentry taste.
Hence Citizens with Courtiours so do vayne-it for the time,
That with their paper Ladders they even stately Castles clyme:
Then proudly prick the mounted Sers, the Harrolds, all to blame,
Will they, nyll they, urging fees to gentellize their name.
Hence country Loutes land-lurch their Lords, and Courtiers prize the same.
Hence worldlings so much study wealth that they forget the use,
And, drudges-like, press out the Grapes, not drinking of the iuse.
Hence arrant Preachers, humming out a commonplace or two,
With bad, ill, naught, Pope, pots, play, mack, keeping a foul ado,
Cogging, and cog'd-with, of a sort of lazy knaves and queanes,
That they be Doltes, and preach to Doltes, will hear it by no means.
With those do these (who could they steal the Goose would stick the Fether)
The Brownist and the Barrowist, go hand in hand together.
Hence is the fashion-Founder of new locks, looks, bas-le-maine,
And John Deuisor in Attire, one Fool in persons twain.
Hence, more than with our selves our selves, do Flatterers prevail:
They make us proud of Virtues, known of us in us to fail:
But less Manslaughter hurts, than when Men-soothers say al-hayle.
Though Echo and Narcissus haunte, and hurt, each Sex and State,
(Our States Superlatiue except, still one for right and rate)
Yeat cheesely they with women-kinde prevailed have of late:
And thus of this two Gossips old, together met, debate.
CHAP. XLVII.
THE Younger of these widows (for they both had thrice been so)
Trots to the Elders Cottage, hers but little distance fro:
Thear, cowring ore two sticks a-crosse, burn at a smoakie Stock,
They chat how Young Men them in youth, and they did young men mock:
And how since threescore years a-goe (they aged foure-score now)
Men, women, and the world, wear chaung'd in all, they know not how.
When we were Maids (quoth. the one of them) was no such newfound pride:
Yeat served I Gentels, seeing store of dainty Girls beside.
Then wore they Shoes of ease, now of an inch-broad, corked hye:
Black karsie stockings, worsted now, yea silk of youthful'st dye:
Garters of Lystes, but now of silk, some edged deep with gold:
With costlier toys, for courser turns, than v^'d, perhaps of old.
Fringed and ymbroidred Petticoats now beg. But heard you named,
Till now of late, Busks, Perrewigs, Maskes, Plumes of Feathers framed,
Supporters, Pooters, Fardingales above the Loins to waire,
That be she near so bombe-thin, yet she crosse-like seems foure-squaire?
Some Wives, grayheaded, shame not locks of youthful borrowed hair:
Some, tyring Art, attyer their heads with only Tresses baire:
Some (grosser pride than which, think I, no passed Age might shame)
By Arce, abusing Nature, heads of antick't hair do frame:
Once starching lack't the Term, because was lacking once the toy:
And lack't we all those Toys and Terms, it were no grief but joy.
But lawful wear it some be such, should all alike be coy'
Now dwells each Drossell in her Glass: when I was young, I wot,
On Holly-dayes (for sildome else such ydell times we got)
A Tubb or Paile of water clear stood us in steed of Glass:
And yeat (which still I bear in mind) for it I schooled was,
Even by an holy Friar, that espyde me tooting so,
Who, softly stealing at my back, cryde suddenly. Ho, Ho.
I, starting, turned and saw the Friar, who, though no dyuell he wear,
Yeat, for he was a Friar, I did for soul or body fear,
That is, least this wear pride (whereof I sharply now should hear,)
Or that the cockish Cowle-man (none but we alonely theare)
His ghostly calling laid apart, might carnally appear:
But, meant he well, or mused he ill, my Mother coming in,
Mends all amys, and soberly the Friar did thus begin.
Young Damsels, and sometimes (quoth he) old Dotards vnawaer,
Do thus offend, whilst thus they seem upon themselves to staer:
But what they see is not themselves: And then a Tale did tell,
How Echo and Narcissus wear aucthorised from Hell,
That egging, and This acting pride, in Worldlings hearts to dwell:
And either often in Mirrors and in Waters beauteous seem,
To curious Gazers inn, who those to be themselves do deem.
Fly glass and water-tooting, Girl, Narcissus fall extreme:
Fear flattrie too: for Men to Maids be Ecchos to subdewe,
The Friar said: and all too soon I found his sayings true.
Yeat then he seemed to have told a Tale but of a Tubbe,
Which three score Wynters-aged Sores eue^ now, do freshly rub.
My Parents they wear wealthy, and my self in wanton youth,
Was fair enough, but proud enough, so Foole-enough in truth.
I might have had good Husbands, which my desteny withstood:
Of three now dead (ah, grief is dry, Gossyp, this Ale is good)
In faith not one of them was so: for by this drink I swear,
(Requarrelling the Cup, whose Lips and it vnparted wear,
When the other Beldamme, great with chat (for talkatiue be Cups)
The formers Prate, not worth the while, thus fondly interrups.
WHen I (quoth she) the Country left to be a London Las,
I was not fayrer than my self believed fair I was.
Good God, how formal, prankt, and peart, became I in a trice,
As if unto the Place it wear a Nature to be Nice.
Scarce entered I the Teenes, but that to Wed my will was hot:
Ye that be married, and intend to marry, well I wot,
The Priest no sooner said, but I upon my typ-toes stand,
As if that Lesson had concerned my self, even out of hand.
The same proportion with my heart my groeth nor age did hold:
Days seeming years, when I, Vn-wead, was sixteen winters old.
And growing was the green Disease, which men in Maids do cure,
When came a Lover, I, (for-sooth) becoming full demure.
For erst I had observed this Art, Delay gives men Desire:
Yeat loathe to hurt my haste, and least the Hansell should retyer,
I was not over coye, nor he to warm him at my Fire.
I Will blab (for why? for it and more that I in youth did do,
Long since I passed ghostly shriftes, penance, and pardons too)
Such match we made, that Maid, nor Wife, nor Widow, left he me,
But with my Maidenhead he crossed the Seas, and farewell he:
For from my fault could not, as chan'st, the Somner prole a fee:
My belly did not blab, so I was still a Maid, and free.
It comfort should in loss to think we had not once to lose,
And what we have as ever to be hild should none suppose:
But not in me this sentence hild: more eagerly than erst
I on the brydell byte, as loath to fast that late did feast.
Swift gallops tier both man and horse, soone-hot, is soone-cold love,
No Man, I mean, Loue-hot as mine, loves as the Turtell Dove,
And, in good soothe, a Sott is she, that cog'd-with cannot cogge:
As readily my Love did gad, as did my Lover iogge.
Tush, in those times wear no such toys as Gagate stones to try,
By toysting them in Potions, if a Maid had trod awrie.
But this was rather current, yea each holy Fathers lore,
That therefore Nature sweetneth Love, that it the world might store.
Which made me think it then a sin (so tender hearted I)
Beloved, not to Love again, indangring men to die:
(For so they swore they would, nor then believed I men would lie:
Whom now I know Camelions, whilst to pray on us they plye.)
Yeat better times were those than these for our avail, for why?
Even for good-fellowship at least then were they roundly to it,
Now ear they love (if ever love) sententiously they doc it.
Who loves not for the Person but the Portion loves no whit,
But he that loves for only Love, does reason quite forgit,
Say men, and article the Match, less by true Love than Wit.
Ungrateful Men, what would ye more than Love for Love? than we
Be close, neat, bountious, bucksome, and our bodies Masters ye?
Too curious, pettish, ielous, too imperious, too unstable,
Are Men, say Women: but to beat such Fools with their own babble,
As when that I was young, our Sex is now, troe I, as able.
Old doting Fool, one foot in grave, what prattell I of youth?
Contrition, not Shrift-pennance cure, if preach our Vicar truth.
Here, after little pause, they prate confusedly, I wot:
Whose talk had often ended, had the Period been the Pot.
They told how forward Maidens wear how proud if in request
How brybed, praised, promisd fair, men baddest out the best.
They talked of a Widows tears, her haste again to weade,
He gone forgot, his Orphant wronged, she spoyld, an Unthrift speade,
They pratteld too of London Youths, how late their headie gaer
Might have invited to a Sack, wheare they prepos'd no shaer.
They talked of times when Beasts could speak, of Foxes, and of Apes:
Much needles prattel, what through Drink, and Dotage, them escapes
Yeat Ecchos and Narcissus Voice and Shadow (now as then,
Working against humility, of wantes the worst in men)
Caused them as thus to use their prate, and me of this my pen,
And to these glorious Follies too those Furies say Amen.
CHAP. XLVIII.
THE Monarch of that Continent that does, in one, contain
Five ancient Scepters (for he would the world wear wholly Spain)
As Castile, Aragon, Nauarr, Granado, Portugaile
Be newly named, yea India wheare Castillian Powers prevail:
These, States in Affricke, Lumbardie, Peru, Assores, and
Both Cicells, Austra, Barbary, Naples, with Netherland,
And many other Provinces, by such ambition got,
As gives his Impressa life to verefie the Mot,
Him not sufficing (for it says, the World suffices not)
Even he, I say, affecting Raygne in England, Almaine, France,
And all Europa, lately did an holy League advance.
The Pope, Himself, Sauoy, and Guise, therein Confedrates chief:
^ome wroght by Bulls, Sauoy by Sword, Spain yielded Guise relief,
Guise did religious Treason act: but all did fail, in brief.
Geneua is vnsacked, France yields Rome small profit, Spain
By cost has lost, England, through God, tryumphant does remain,
Nauar has means to have Nauar: and, when shall God so please,
The rightful heir of Pertagale his Empire shall re-seaze.
Ye Christian Princes, pity him, in punishing his Fo,
That now afflicts him, and would on you tyrannize so.
If one selfsame Religion (if Religion Romistes have)
If Natures Line, or Native Laws, the Pope that so did crave,
If to have had possessed the Crown, with Portagales applause,
Might have prevailed, these and more had helped Antonius cause.
But brybed Traitors, fraud, and force, have made the Spaniard such,
That Castile, from a Cronet leapt, thinksmany Crowns not much.
The great Nauarrois, Sampson, stauld the first Castilian King:
But badly Castile quits his love whence did that honor spring:
And from his Line by treachery the Diadem does wring.
So, by what right or wrong so-eate, Spain cloureth Crowns together
And aims, even from Portugale, as is supposed, hither:
Not wanting English, willing to be hopled in that Teither.
But Traitors, Fools, and Fugitives, Whom prouendor does prick,
Ensew your proper sorrow, lean upon that rotten stick,
Your kindness shall not mis, at least, this kindly Spanish Trick.
WHen Spaniards and their Partizens eare-while should us inuaide,
In plotting of that Stratageme in Council much was said:
Some of our Queen to be destroyde, of murdering us some spoke,
Some this, some that, but all of all an altered World to make:
Least English Papists, then shut up in Elie and els-wheare,
Mean time by us might lose their lives, some One, by chance did fear,
Which scruple was removed soon by one, that well did know,
Not for religion, but a Realm, did Spain that cost bestow.
Our drift (quoth he) a Conquest is, the Profit meant to Spain,
Not that the English Papists should be Sharers of the gain.
So hope they, but (so help me God) our policy wear small
That they should flourish, nay, should live, could we procure their fall.
Theare be they great already, wheare we only great must be,
Whom, should they equal, woe to us when we shall disagree:
Now that therefore which we cannot with honesty contriue,
Our Foes shall finish, if of them they leave not one alive.
Thus said he, not gaine-saide: for hang who shall so they may thrive.
This Maxim has hild, and good, wheare they have ouerthrone
By treacherous Agents, of the same they have not trusted one:
Add, than the Spaniard, prouder and more cruel People none.
Even Cortes, that with cost and pain, rich India did subdew,
And gave to Spain his Conquest, found false Meede for Service true.
So they that won them Portugale, and Naples spead, in few:
And too imperiously do they, oppress their Conquests new:
Yeat boast not perpetuity, but (Spain) this Pattern view.
Caldea prized Assiria, and Assiria prized the same,
The Medes and Persians both, and them the Macedons orecame,
The Romans the^, that Empier next had France, the Germans now,
Which only Title hardly do the Spaniards them allow,
Spain has Navarre, that once had Spain, wheare once crowned we their King,
And once did Spanish-Irish-Scots us to subjection bring,
We Scots, French, Irish: too and fro thus often Scepters fly:
