Yea to Posterities old names of Lands and Peoples dye.
Sicambries, Gawles, Picts, Vandels, Goths, and Astrogothes, known late
In Europ by these names, have changed those names, and Europs State,
And with the Spaniards, part of those, may alter in like rate.
Each Bird shall then remaunde her Plumes, and naked leave your Iaye,
At least when Phillip shall decease: and why not ear that day?
For power and prowse lack Turnus Peer, yeat when his Fates withstoode,
Iturna, no not Iunos-selfe, could do him any good.
But Godhoode none in Indian Gold, and pope-buld hopes shall mis,
Nor Macedonian Phillips Son Castilian Phillip is:
But one who, whilst he wars for ours, has lost even part of his.
Not only Spain think destinate in Pomp not to impaier,
Our lived long, that breathes, envied, not else but odious Air:
And (which is monstrously too true) Religion is pretext,
Where through the Spanyard and the Pope all Christendom have vexed.
Witness their Powers, by Land and Seas, that threatened us er'while:
Of it a word: and note how God did patronize our I Will.
CHAP. XLIX.
THe Spaniards long time Care and Cost, invincible surnamed,
Was now a flote, whilst Parma to from Flanders hither aimed,
Like Fleet, of eightscore Ships and od, the Ocean never bore,
So huge, so strong, and so complete, in every Strength and Store.
Carikes, Gallions, Argosies, and Galliasses, such
That seemed so many Castles, and their tops the clouds to tuch.
These on the Lizardes show themselves, and threaten Englands fall:
But theare with Fistie Ships of ours that Fleet was fought withal.
Howbeit of a greater sort our Navy did consist,
But part kept Diet in the Port, that might of health have miste,
Had Spayns Armada of our wants in Plimmouths Haven wiste.
The rest had eye on Parma, that from Flanders armor threats:
Mean while Lord Charles our Admiral, and Drake, did worthy Feats:
Whose feareles fifty Moole-hils bod their trypeld Mountains bace,
And even at first (so pleased it God) pursewde as if in chase:
By this (for ouer-idle seemd to English hearts the Shore)
Our Gallants did imbarke each-wheare, and made our Forces more
But in such warlike Order then their Ships at Anchor lay,
That we, unless we them disperse, on bootles labor stay:
Not lacked Policy that to that purpose made us way.
Ours fyred diverse Ships, that down the Current sent, so skaerd,
That Cables cut, and Ankers lost, the Spaniards badly faerd.
Dispersed thus, we spare not shot, and part of them we sink,
And part we board, the rest did fly, not fast enough they think.
Well guided little Axes so force tallest Oaks to fall,
So numbrous Herds of stately Hearts, fly Beagles few and small.
Nine days together cha'ste we them, not actious, save in flight
About Eight thowsands perished by famine, sea, and fight.
For Treasure, Ships, and Carrages, lost Honor, Prisners tayne,
The Spaniards, hardly scaping hence, escaped not rebukes in Spain.
Well might thus much (as much it did) cheer England, but much more
Concurrancie from one to all to stop that common Sore.
Even Chatholiques (that erred name does please the Papists) waer
As forward in this Quarrel as the foremost Arms to bear:
Recusants and Suspects of noote. Of others was caer.
And had not our God-guided Fight on Seas prevailed, yet
The Spaniards, land whereso they could, had with our Armies met.
Our common Courage wished no less, so lightly feared we Foes,
Such hope in God, such hate of them, such hearts to bar^er bloes.
Here flamed the Cyclops Forges, Mars his Armorie was here,
Himself he sheads in us, and with our Cause our selves we cheer.
But (which had scar^^fide our wounds, if wounded, with the Balm
Of her sweet Presence, so applaus'd as in Sea-stormes a Calm)
Her royall-selfe, Elizabeth our Sovereign lawful Queen,
In magnanimious Majesty amidst her Troops was seen.
Which made us weep for joy: nor was her kindness less to us.
Think nothing letting then that might the common Cause discus,
Wheare Prince and People have in Love a Sympathy as thus.
Howbeit Force, nor Policy, but Gods sole Providence,
Did clear fore-bosted Conquest and behighted Thraldom hence.
He in Saneherib his nose did put his hook, and brought
Him back again the way he came, without performing ought:
He fought for us, Alonely we did shout and Trumpets sound,
When as the Walls of ^^rico fell flat unto the ground.
Y^a least (for erst did never here like strong Supplies befall,
Like loyal hearts in every one, like warlike minds in all,
Less spaer of Purses, more Foresight, and valiant Guides to act,
As shewde our hardy littell Fleet that battle never slackt)
Least these, I say, might have been said the cause that we subdew'de,
Even God, to Glorify himself, our gained Cause pursew'de,
Without our loss of Man, or Mast, or Foe once touching Shore,
Save such as wrackt, wea^e Prisnors, or but landing, lived not more:
And as in public Prayers we did his defense implore,
So being Victors, publicly, we yielded thanks therefore.
Her Highness self (good cause she had) in view of every Eye,
On humbled Knees did give him thanks that gave her Victory.
Remains, what she won, what Spain and Rome did lose in fame:
Remains, Popes use Potentates but to retriue their Game.
CHAP. L.
IF not with minds preiudicate, but holding in suspense
A while the Papacy, that takes and gives so much Offense,
With single hearts, and Eyes, and Ears, al christian Princes would
Peruse the sacred Scriptures (as in very deed they should)
Then might they justly censure, how in ^owlines, or Pride,
In Charity, or Cruelty, how strait to God, or wide,
In Doctrine, and in Discipline, Rome has, and now does guide,
With still Prouisoes from the Lay the Scriptures light to hide,
Least should (as would, and clearly does) their Cosning be espide^
Whereby, even ipso facto, is their Cause against them tried:
The sin of Pride made Lucifer against God him self rebel,
And through that sin he so sedu^'t that Adam also fell.
Then played he Rex ore all the Earth, except a faithful Few:
Till Christ, incarnate, on the Cross Synns Kingdom did subdewe.
Then Christ, was powrefull in the world, for Faith had practice right:
And what could more our Ghostly Foe then Faith and Practice spite?
His ancient Stratageme therefore to plant he casts anew,
And by the pride of only Works from Faith a many drew.
Then were such Honors heapt on Popes, (Worlds-A^iects ere that time)
That Lucifer him self than they presumed not more to clyme.
Their golden Legend, though not it autenticke be in aught,
To us does teach a Story that to them might now be taught.
Theare was a Man of stature big, and big withal in mind,
For serve he would, yet One tha^ who^ he greater none might find.
He, hearing that the Emperor was in the world most great,
Came to his Court, was (...)tertaynd, and, serving him at meat,
It chanced the Devil was named, whereat the Emperor him blessed:
When as, until he knew the cause, the Pagane would not rest.
But when he heard his Lord to fear the Devil his ghostly Foe,
He left his Service, and to seek and serve the Devil did go:
Of heaven, or hell, God, or the Devil, he erst nor heard nor card,
Alone he sought to serve the same that would by none be darde.
He met (Who soon is met) the Devil, was intertayn'd, they walk,
Till, coming to a Cross, the Devil did fearfully it balke.
The Servant, musing, questioned his Master of his fear:
One Christ, quoth he, with dread, I mind when does a cross appear.
Then serve thy self, the Giant said, that Christ to serve I Will seek.
For him he asked an Hermit, who aduisde him to be meek, found,
By which, by Faith, and works of Alms, would sought-for Christ be
And how and where, to practice these he gave Directions sound.
Then he, that sko^nd his Service late to greatest Potentates,
Even at a common Ferry now to carry all awaites.
Thus doing long, as with a Child he over once did waide,
Under his Loode midway he ^aints, from sinking hardly stayde.
Admiring how, and asking who, was answered of the Child,
As on his Shoulders Christ he bore, by being humbly mild,
So through Humility his Soul to Christ was reconcilde,
And, of his Carrage, Christo-fer should thenceforth be his name.
Then let this popish Scripture, Popes, at least you Conuerts frame:
Of which Converting, Christo-fers ye thenceforth shall be said:
If not, apply and perish in your Luciferring Traid.
Ye know, I know, that but in Christ may no Redemption be:
So your great friend, our Gardner, on his deathbed could agree:
But to the Vulgars open not, against Rome, that gap, quoth he.
So miserable is your state who, seeing, will not see,
That Christofer, ear Christo-fer, was Atheist less than ye.
Well answered once a King of ours the Pope, that bod him free
Two P^elates, terming them his Sons: The King seemed to agree,
But sent their complete Armor: look are these thy Sonne^, quoth he.
False Hesket too not falsely spoke, reporting lately this,
That such as Papists would seduce, and of seducing mis,
Are marked dead: For he to whom he so did say, fear I,
Earl Ferdinando Stanley, so dissenting, so did try,
As other Peers, here, and els-where, have found the like no lie.
Nor preached he the Pope amiss, that did to him apply
This Tex, to wit: This is the Heir, come on and let him die,
The Inheritance let us inioye: Naught seek they else, for why?
Those bad be good that give, those good be bad that Gifts deny,
From Annanias literal fault they Consciences would tie:
These death-eide Basilisques therefore in every Sense do fly.
Be of Religion iumpe with them, in all their Toys aff^e,
A Peter-Penny, if withhild, knocks all the rest awrye.
Hell, Heaven, Bulls, Pardons, Pope, and to be Pope, does money buy.
Yea, too blasphemous, they in^roch upon the Deity,
Though of these (...)ifers have been that perish through a Fly.
Each sin against God, how vile so-eare, will Popes with Pardons fit:
Cross but the Pope, pardon thou him, he will not pardon it.
For Faith his common Plea is sword and fire against his foes:
But who, but fools, believe that Faith exacted is by bloes.
Than those three Mighty Emperors, Fifth Charles, with Ferdinand,
And Maximilian, with the Pope did none more partial stand:
Yeat suffered they the Consciences of Protestants in peace,
But Philip, nor the Pope will now their Inquisition cease:
Though Either suffer Jews, and Stews, because of Gains increase.
CHAP. LI.
THis Spanish Inquisition is a Trappe, so slily set,
As into it Wise, Godly, Rich, by Blanchers bace as fett.
Direct or indirectly then to answer all is one:
From those Inquisitors escape but very few or none.
Even so by racking out the joints, or chopping off the head,
Procustes fitted all his Guests unto his iron beade.
Aue Maria, Credo, and the Pater noster say,
In Latin, else they penance thee, and take thy goods away.
Nay, these though said, both say and do as they, yeat all for naught,
They will exact by Torture what thou think, and have thought,
Of Mass, the Pope, and Popish points, til in the Lapse thou fall:
Then, Mercy God, amongst those men no mercy is at all:
A bloodier Law used bloodierly was never heard or shall.
Tormenting men until they guess by whom they are acus'de,
Which gessed, it against themselves for Evidence is vs'de:
As who would say, so be it must, because it so is mus'de.
Phillip, abusiuely seduc't, and World-deceiuing Pope,
Peruse all Laws, even Paganizme, past under heavens coope,
And rightly pondring those with this, the^ speak your thoughts indeed,
Was not your Draco Satan that himself could thus exceed?
Here add for badde we might your League, if That of This had need.
O happy and thrice happy Realm of ours, and other Lands,
Wheare, touching death by Clergie-doomes, the Policy withsta^ds.
THen freated Paul, Pope Paul the Third, when Peter came in Plea*
No Marvel, Luther preached gold, and glory from his Sea.
But, see an help: Ignatius then conceited had his Sect,
And craved Confirmance of the Pope, which Paul did then reject,
But when he heard that Orders vow might chiefly him avail,
Them Iesuists he incorporates: nor they his Pope ship fail:
But for his only Doctrine are Apostles, and as true,
As to the Devil the deadly-Sinnes to Popes this Order new.
Yeat these be those are underhand deceived whilst they deceive:
Witness the Gallows, wheare for most they end those webbes they weaue.
For unadvised are, me thinks, our Iesuistes in this,
In Cell-life since so strict, and here they hanging seldom mis,
That, for Applause at least (although even so wear bacely badde)
They change not Treachers unto them to whom from us they gadde.
But better course than either wear secure at home to stay,
And theare with loyal hearts attend what Subjects should obaye,
Wheare no tyrannizing is ore the Conscience, though it straye.
And Men of civil Carriage, though in bodies not imployde,
Win good Report, they shiftles shame that wish their Home distroyde.
Young English Fools, for cause too true, we pity you in deed:
First for ye are our Countriemen, then for that ye proceed
Against Scripture, Law, and Nature, and Fools palpable, in that
Ye note not how your Follies make our English Foxes fat:
Allen at Rome, Others els-wheare, that live hand-ouer-heade,
By tasking you to broach those Faults for which themselves be feade.
Whilst these lie burrowed, safe from skath, among you adoptiue Cubs,
They do observe the pregnanst wit, whom Coffeehouse rubs,
Of Atheist, factious, desprate, or vainglorious, him they school,
And then transport to England, thear to play the dangerous Fool:
Seduciugly insisting on performance of their vow,
That does Rebellion, Regecide, and breach of Oaths allow.
Though David knew his Sovereign Saul disfauored of God,
And might have killed, and then bin crowned, such sin his soul for-bod.
Which of the Pa^riarks, Prophets, or Gods People, whilst they saru'd
Even Ethnicks, traytrously unto their own Affections carved.
But thus do ye, nay worser, for your Darnell giddeth so,
As Gods Anointed and Beloved ye censure for his Foe:
But woorst of all, by murthring her to merit Heaven ye troe.
Put case the diulish Act were done, to escape yeat lacks your Charter,
And dead with Torments, not the pain, but cause, does make the Marter.
Say that the Pope lacks Malice, Say your selves do love the State,
Too grossly yeat your Doctrine faults in things of greatest wate.
Which your Absurdities do our Divines confute, I wot:
Ye say his Popeship cannot err, We prove ye err in, Not.
Nor in the Sacrament that Christ is really we hold.
Your Transubstantiation we justly have controld.
Yours for the Living and Deceast Propitiatory Masses,
And Purgatory, should we grant, ye might conclude us Asses.
Your Worshipping of Images, and Inuocating Saints,
And Supererogation, us with your Defects acquaints.
Besides the Supper of our Lord, and Baptism, we reject
More Sacraments: Howbeit ye Five other do erect:
As Confirmation, Penance, with Priest-Order, Marrage too,
And extreme Unction. Over more too much applause ye do
Works Meritorious. Of the Cup the Lay ye do bereave:
And of translated Scriptures, so, unspied, to deceive.
D^^ges* Vows, Indulgences, Confession in the ear,
Dissoluing Subjects from the Faith they should their Princes bear,
Dispensing with the Scriptures, for Revenge, to please, or gain,
Your Pardons, Pilgrimages, and your hallowed Paltries vain,
Pope, and what-so is Papistrie, well may we call profaine:
Ambitious Rome, that for thy Pomp not sparest Souls to baine.
Once weart thou truly Catholic, corrupted foully now,
Not outed quite the Church, A Spark in her we thee allow:
And wish thou weart, as then thou weart, when as the Fathers good,
As touching Antichrist, and whence, in both misunderstood.
No Marvel, even to Daniels-selfe in Mystery it was:
And dye did those good Fathers ere that Scripture came to pas.
But now, who-so can Antichrist but etymologize,
And knows the Pope and Rome, the Place and Man of sin descries.
Sheep-clothed Wolves, Christ's Pro-contra, the Popes have bin and be:
No place but Rome for Antichrist, none but the Pope is he.
CHAP. LII.
But human Pureness none is such, but it to err^ is known:
Think not we labor here your Faults, and o^uer-leape our Own.
For in the best of men the Flesh and Spirit combat still:
One thing the Spirit, and the Flesh the contrary does will.
We Virtue praise, but practice Vice: possessed weal we fly,
And tract off woe. At Heaven we aim, but with a worldly Eye.
Our selves we love, yeat than our selves we have no crosser Foe.
For peace we war, a perverse war that does our selves ore-throe.
At once we burn, and are key-cold. We seem to stand that fall:
To heal that hutt. We brag of bad. We dye ere death does call.
We triumph while we are subdude. We bliss our proper baine.
We gladly do subject our selves unto each giddie Vain.
Our gadding Thoughts conceit the Clouds, our selves mean while for got.
Our Nay is Yea, our Yea is Nay: we will, and then will not.
Our Souls like this, our Flesh lusts that As Proteus changes, so
Do our Affections: and our Thoughts be shifting too and fro.
Even Hydra-like we flesh our Faults: our Minds do waver still.
Our selfe-Conceits be winged: and we fly from good to ill.
Our Peace with Discord breeds our woe: the Contrary our Ease.
We never do but plague our selves, whilste that our selves we please.
We would be we, as if not we. Us Plenty makes poor.
We, partial, blame, enable, and disable us eremore.
All these, and wilful Sins besides, to us with you and all
Too common we confess. But of our Doctrine speak we shall.
Propitious be to us, O God, that Faith have practice too:
Which we omit as Publicanes, as Pharisees ye do.
Our Doctrine though is Gospel, yours Traditions but of Men:
Enough of Yours: Examine Ours by Touch of Scripture then,
Upon the only Scriptures does our Church Foundation lay:
Let Patriarchs, Prophets, Gospel, and The Apostles for us say:
For Soul and Body we affirm, are all sufficient they:
Yeat ye add Canons, part corrupt, some Books ye quite denaye.
We by the Hebrew, and the Greek, their primer Pens, expound
Each Scripture, by the eldest Clarks, when doubtful Textes be found:
Not by the Latin only, as ye would that all wear bound.
So farforth yeat the Fathers and the Councels we approve,
As do their Expositions tend to sincere Faith and Love.
Else fully Scriptures, in themselves, explain themselves, say we:
I^ searched with that humble Spirit by which they written be:
Through which is often from literal speech a spiritual sense set free,
Upon which sense the Catholic Church did, does, and must agree.
Nor does our Church admit, at least allow, of those in her,
That teach not Faith sincerely, win to Heaven, from Hell deter,
That with new Glozes tainte the Text, or such as be vnreade
In that sweet Promise of the Seed should broose the Serpents head:
The Alpha, and Omega, of all Scriptures, and whereby
Of grace, through faith in Christ, our Souls revive, and Sin does die:
Our Church affects, how so effects, such pure Theology,
And Guides, and to our Natural Prince, graunts sole Supremacy.
Gods Cou'nant with the Patriarchs, and extending to the Seed,
Us Gentiles to coequall is a Primate in our Creed:
And Christ we know the end of it. In Circumcisions place
Is Baptism. And entirely we the Tables Two embrace
Which God himself in Synai wrote, and gave to Moses then,
To publish to the people, Two Commandments in Ten:
Scriptures Idea, couched in our Love to God and men.
The Apostles, Athanasian, Nice, and Bizain Creeds we hold
Autentick, by the holy Spirit in sacred Writ inrowld.
One Godhead of three Persons, in coequall Majesty,
Do we believe: of whom the Son did for Believers die:
The only Ransom that redeemes from Satan's Tyranny:
Even Christ, the Way, the Truth, the Life, not crooked, glozed, frail,
But right for Rule, in Promise firm, in Guerdon near to fail:
Who to reprove the bad, approve the good, and to assure
The Wau'ring, and against the Devil our safety to procure,
Did, giltles, die, that we, lost Souls, might live: naught else did make
That he, his Deity adiorn'd, did human Nature take:
Nor, glorifide, disclaimes he us, unless we him forsake:
And what is fruitles Faith, but such Apostasy? and what
Ensues Apostasy, but to be doomed Damned for that?
No Doctrine, or Traditions, we hold current, save the same
That Gospel, or The Apostles Acts, or Pens, include or name.
Baptism, incorporating us in Christ, and us in one,
Christ's mystical last Supper, whear in Sign his death is known,
Be Sacraments, except which twain, do we accept of none.
By only Christ our Advocate we to the Father pray:
Nor think we Saints deceased can our Suits to him convey:
Howbeit still most reverently of Saints we think and say.
Unnecessary Burdens, on our Christian Freedom laid,
Contrary these, that but belief, and virtuous life perswaide.
Yea only Faith does justify, say we, of Gods free grace,
By Christ: not Faith is idle, but does Charity embrace.
Who may, but will not help does hurt, we know: and curious they
That, dribling Alms by Art, disband wel-Meant from wel-Dons pay:
And he that questions Ones distress, and does not help endeavor,
Than he that sees, and nothing says, or cares, is less Deceiuour.
Then hope we health when sin is felt repentantly in heart:
Add then new life, and we to God, God does to us conuart:
Thus Peter used his Keys, not thus play Popes S. Peter's part.
For Cleargie-men, and Lay, our Church has godly Discipline:
Laws worthy better than sometimes are those the Laws define.
Our Princes in their Policies and Laws do we obey:
Though God his Cause they seek to cross, yeat we for the^ do pray,
In patience, not perverse Attempts, for better times we stay.
Not as denide, but as devout we do and should abstain
From Meats even meet, the proud Flesh from sins excess to waine,
Which, should we ska^t, and yet be dronk with lust or like were vain:
Save also public Policy does public Sparing crave,
In Fasts or differences of Meats, no other keep we have.
Alms deeds, and works of Charity we practiuely profess,
And follow Saints as they did Christ, and leave whear they tra^sgresse:
Such, and so much, as said, are we: forgive us, God, if less.
For godly though Religion, Prince, and Policy they are,
Yeat things, that of themselves be good, abuse brings out of square,
And sundry Faults in sundry Folks we sometimes must forbear:
Howbeit with best gouernd States our State may now compare.
CHAP. LIII.
A Wise man living like a Drone, an Old Man not devout,
Youth disobedient, Rich-men that are Charity without,
A shameless Woman, vicious Lords, a Poore-man proudly stout,
Contentious Christians, Pastors that their Function do neglect,
A wicked King, no Discipline, no Laws men to direct,
Are Twelve the foulest Faults that do all Commonwealths infect:
In most of all which Twelve erres not, or much in any one,
Our State, respecting this, of some, Laws not abused none.
Our God-blest Queen, Palladium of our happy public Weal,
For worth so far beyond all words, we one only touch in zeal.
No Realm than ours has wiser Laws, for every Right and Wrong:
Nor is through Meed, or Means, the Weak betrayed to the Strong,
Though of vn-Quaifed Judges some is sung another song.
But who the Person, not the Cause, respects, has forgot
The Creator of Persons, who and Justice differ not.
A Council watchful for the State. Our Benches every wheare
Supplide with Judges learned, just, and such as God do fear.
Wink here and there at Auerice, Incharitie, and Pride,
And better Clergy than is ours not Europe has beside.
Say me, who can, whether Extreme has harmed Religion more,
That old of theirs too prodigal, or This of ours too poor?
Then Giving gave too slack a Rain, now-Gleaning curbs too sore.
By Slavery and by Symonie now Church-Preferment comes:
Like Nabal and to Helies Sons get Churchmen up their cro^mes.
Too much, too little, or a mean, sort out alike, we see,
Housekeeping, nor Humility, in any of the Three.
Be hospitalious, Churchmen: Lay, cease sacrilegious sin:
Your Soules-sore, but their Stores-salue, whence, eue^ whiningly, they win,
By pinching from the Pulpet, and their Purses, with this note,
Scarce will their Studies stipend them, their wives, and Children cote.
And verily it is a fault, and maimed Learnings Foe,
That Church-Possessions should amongst the Lay be shared so.
And verily it is a fault, if so the Clergy live,
As theirs to take be thought no Sin, nor Meede to them give.
Almighty everlasting God, which only work'st great wonders,
Amend such Pastors, and unite thy Flock that Satan sunders.
No one thing quails Religion more than foundring Presbytrie:
Each S^ot, impugning Order, says, and does his Fantasy:
Our Book of Common prayer, though most sound Divinity,
They will not read, nor can they preach, yeat up the Pulpet tower,
Thear making tedious Preachments, of no edifying power.
O learned Seers, whose good lives and Doctrine do agree,
(Not barren is our Land of such) heer-hence vn-meant are ye:
To you, in Reverence and Reward, may nothing wanting be:
It Is only wished your work from Dolts, your Hiues from Drones were free:
It Is wished in These, in Fugitives, in Papists, and (more bad,
Whom to persuade to reason, were with reason to be mad)
In Calophantick Puritaines, amiss amendment had.
THese Hypocrites for these three Gifts to their Lauerna pray,
Just to be thought, all to beguile, That none their Guiles bewray:
Their Art is fayning good they want, and hiding bad they have:
Their Practice is selfe-praise, of praise all others to deprave.
On Love, say some, waits Ielosie, but Ielosie wants love,
When curiously it ouer-plus does idle Quarrels move.
Best Puritaines are so ore-zeal'd: But should I term the rest?
Inhospitalious, Mutinous, and Hypocrites the best,
Insociable, Maleparte, foxing their private good,
Exiling hence wel-neere al Troth, meet Sports, and Neighborhood,
Learnings Foes, contemptuously by them be Laws withstood,
Self-pleasers, Skorners, Harlots, Drones, against the Hair in all:
Of their extreme, whence Atheism breeds, be warning Hackets fall.
If ever England will in ought prevent her own Mishap,
Against these Skommes (no term too gross) let England shut the gap
Their giddie heds gave color first that Spain against Flanders armed,
And the^ their Countries Foes they helped, and most their Country harmed.
If Hypocrites why Puritaines we term, be ask't, in brief,
It Is but an Ironized Term, Good Fellow so spells Thief.
Well-working single-hearted Men in silence (such be some)
Will not apply, but saintish, not in Deed, but by the Dromme.
To Union that our stablisht Laws for public Prayer ties
Not all, is wondered, and offends obedient Ears and Eyes.
The rather, for known Humorists, Sots, noted Mal-Contents,
Here innouate: and each one to a diverse Sect conuents,
Too much irreu'rent, in, and to, the Church and Sacraments.
But leaving these, retier we to ill-drifting Rome and Spain,
Who^ does our Queen, next under God, from Europs Spoil detayne.
THE TENTH BOOK OF ALBIONS ENGLAND.
CHAP. LIVE.
IN Scotland, France, and Netherland, whilst Phillip, and the Pope,
Did Swords, and civil Tumults broach, of prizing them in hope,
Her Highness, through the highest Power, enabled was to aid
Those Countries, to defend her own, and Phillips to inuayde.
Of Scotland first, and then of France, and Belgike shall be said:
When first of other things shall be this short Remembrance made.
It is a saying ancient (not autenticall, I win)
That who-so England will subdew, with Ireland must begin.
Imagine Stukelies only name includes all that is ill:
He forging worth, and to our State Maleuolent in will.
Of bounteous Pensions was therefore possessed in Spain long while,
Until (for it a Nature was in Stukelie to begile)
The King, whom he had cozened long, him purposed to exile.
Then for the Pope the Fugitive a welcome Agent was:
(For nothing ill, might work us ill, has Spain and Rome let pas)
Of him he had an Army, that for Irelands Conquest sails:
When through a fight in Barharte that Expedition fayles.
But how had guiltles England then deserved such hostile Spite?
Her Rebel why relieved they? why armed they him to fight?
Why did? (why do I ask that know the Spanyard so ambitious,
The Remain Prelate pompious, and, which more is, auaticious?)
Why did, I say, Pope Pius Bull, and Gregory's Calfe disqueate
Our Sovereign, and her Subjects, that did never them mis-treate?
Unless for Princes to give Laws to theirs be to offend
Proud Spain, and Rome: if so, as so, let God the Quarrel end.
What else had England done, when they did foster there, with more,
Our Traitor Moereton, sent from them to stir an here-Vprote,
Which cost the North, two northern Earls, and their Consorts full sore?
He whispring, how that Bull had made Anathema our Queen,
Deptiu'd her all Authority, discharged her Subjects cleene,
Blessed all her Foes, cursed all her Friends, left England Anies pray,
And all for damned that did aid or her in ought obaye.
So arrogant, malicious, and mischievous is that Bull,
That Beelzebub, the Prince of hell, appears in it at full.
Why sent they it by Felton to be bishoped at Paul's?
Why fe'ed they Fitz-Morrice, that in Ireland marshaled Brawles?
Saunders, that false seditious Priest that fortified theare,
Why marched he with their Ensigns? Why did they those charges bear?
They shook our Hiue, and forced us forth to sting the^ when they fee'd
False Desmond, and the Rebel now, that as the rest shall speed:
For God against all Traitors has assured wrath decreede.
These Sturres, and more in Ireland, and a many Treasons here,
Have they abetted, to the King of Catholics full dear.
More than his paper Pellets too the Pope has been at cost:
His Alchumie, a dram to win, a pound of Gold has lost.
Was it for love they did erect Receptacles for Ours?
Or so by schooled Treacheries to add unto their Powrs?
Their Iesuists, our Iudasses, act so remote from Love,
As Faiths Relaps, Rebellion, and to Rege-cide they move.
Story, insatiate of the blood of Martyrs, and a many
Blood thirsty Priests, bloodier than whom nor has nor might be any,
Tutting their Natural Princes death, and Ruin of our State,
Do they, and then did nourish, when twixt us seemed no debate.
That Spanish-Iewish Atheist, and Lop-heauie-headed Leach,
(Unworthy a Physicians name) foul Lopas, we impeach,
And Parret, that aspyring Knight, hence bryb'd for duties breach:
Hence have been poysned of our Peers, whom Bribes could not orereach.
But what are these, and more than these, to it the Guizian Scot,
Fatal to Seas of blood, and to her own by earned lot,
Did with our Foes against our State and Sovereigns life complot*
Wherein King Phillip and the Pope especially wear hot.
Here, but in Reverence of her Son, an happy Prince in all,
Religion, Peace, his Subjects Love, of Emperie not small,
Precelling his Progenitors, a Iusticer upright,
Yea ouer-long it wear we should Particulars resight,
How Nature, Wit, and Virtue, deck his body, words, and mind,
Or, if his Fames Deminatiue in any thing we find,
It is but part of Majesty, through Puratizme declynde,
Yea, if for him, whom (and deserved) we have so firm a friend,
It might, I say, be spoke, not thus of Marie should we end.
Her Favors unto David Rize, offensine to the King
His Highness Father, but for him in Story would we bring,
With hers, and Bothuels double wrong to Eithers married Bead,
How they wrought Paricidie, how the treacherous Couple wead,
How, odious to their Own, with hard escape of lives they flead,
How, since our Prisnor, blood she sought, and much through her was head,
Yea all that Buchana^ does wright should largely here be read.
Yeat not her Infancy should be vpbraided with the blood
Of many thousand slaughtred Souls, when perjured Scots withstoode
Her Marriage with Prince Edward, which Eight Henry swore the^ to,
When they esteemed us Heretics (so Papists Oaths vn-doe.)
But, for th'aforesaid Reverence, touch we but Hers and Her,
That, indirectly, here did her abortiue Clame prefer.
CHAP. LV.
KIng James the fourth, that fayld his faith, and lost for it his life,
Had James the Fifth, by Margaret, our Daughter, and his Wife:
Fifth James, through Melancholy ends, for Ours against him good chance,
And left this Marie, whom the Scots, an Infant, wead to France:
Thus, from Seventh Henry's Daughter, she her Title did advance,
But howsoere by blood, or by our Laws, she here could clame,
It Is sure, too soon, and treacherously, she did prefer the same,
And first and last unto our Queen her self a Foe did frame.
Our Marie was no sooner dead, but that her G^izards arm,
And into Scotland Locusts-like in her Pretext did swarm:
Whilst she, in France, did undertake our royal Arms and Style:
Behoou'd therefore our Queen to stand upon her Guard mean while.
Lord William Graie, our English Mars, not Martial more than wise,
Did with an Army hence pack thence our dangerous Neighbor Guise:
Nor died few of Either part, whilst Marie thus would rise.
Since when the League did often her Right, with wrong too much, surmise.
Of Co^quests Spain, of Co^uerts Rome, our Queen does chiefly ba^r,
Against her therefore they chiefly wrought Conspiracies and War:
And not for Maeries Title, or her any virtuous Gifts,
Think that they her inleagued, but from her to plot their Driftes.
Her self mean while, false-Paradi'zd, besybbing Aesops Croe,
Vainglorious through the Foxes gloze, did end her song in woe.
Suffices what is said before, to show wherefore she flead:
Here was she taken, whom if Scots had taken, had been dead:
Put under Guarde: and so was meet should one that quarreld Ours,
And not her self alone, but had Abbettors foreign Powrs.
Yeat Princely her Allowance, and more stately, as is said,
Than had she been in Scotland: nor was Liberty denayde
Of Hauking, Hunting, and Disports: that, had she been content,
Her merriest and securest days a Prisoner she spent.
Scotland, though labourd of our Queen, would not receive her and
Such Treacher, though imprisoned here, proved she to either Land,
That death awaited her at Home: and had we let her go,
She was the Leagues shot-Anchor might our Queen and State o'rethroe.
Percie and Neuell, ancient Earls, This yet in Spanish pay,
(Though bacely ill, too well for him his Country would betray)
That other headed, both her Wreckes, we touch but by the way.
With that succeeding County, who concurring with his brother,
Once pardoned, still conspyred and (Laws Progress so to smother)
Dispatched himself: Paget and more, like guilty as those other,
Whose faults and falls had Rome and Spain their Father, her their Mother,
Omit we. No^folks House, from first of Howards made a Prince,
Though fauor'd of the Commons, have defected ever since.
As Absalom used Courtesy but as Ambitious smoke,
Last Thomas Duke of Norfolk^ so did his aspyring cloak,
Who^ promised faith once fre'ed, but that promise soon he broke.
Him for Confedrate with those Earls, rebelling Proofs did touch:
And with this Scottish Queen that he Intelligence had much:
Her Marriage that he closely sought, and her Escape pretended,
And in her Right, had fadg'd their wrong, her Highness Rain had ended:
Of Rising near to London, and to take it Plots they laid:
From Netherland the Spanish King had promised them aid:
From Scotland into Spaeyne should be the Infant-King betrayde:
Ireland meane-while with adverse* Arms should also be ore-layde:
And hereof, to, and fro, the Pope, wear Letters still conuayde,
All which, and more, directly proved, he, pi^ied, lost his head:
Nor ought his death, from being such, did stand his Son in stead.
So hardly find we Great-men in their Greatness satisfide,
Or for their Greatness, not to be of other Men enuide.
But what is All to those have All, when but in Trifles crossed,
Disgrace, or Greese, or Grudge, vn-queare no less than all wear lost?
Conclude we then, all Riches, Form, Pomp, Worlds-Applause, but Wind:
Conclude we then, to Monarchize is to command the mind.
Throckmorton yeat, more privy and more practicing than those,
With her, Mendoza, Papists here, foreign, and Land-leapt Foes,
Did Mischiefs that imported more our practiced State disclose.
But when of Parry, Babington, and their Attempts I think,
With whom this Scottish Lady in their bloody hopes did link,
All foresaid Practices seem then Epitomies to it
Whereto these folliall Traitors did themselves and Senses fit.
Our Sentinels, almost too late, the Larum bell did ring,
Yeat hardly then to arm her self our General they bring:
The Queen of Scots from Ours almost her Crown and life had priz'de,
Ear to prevent the same she would, by audience, be aduis'de.
When gentle Durance might not salve that Danger, did remain,
(Which God, and Nature tolerate) slay rather than be slain:
To which our Parliament advised our Queen, but long in vain*
So far was she from foing her, that sought her life and Rain.
CHAP. LVI.
NOw thirty six our greatest Peers and States had power to hear,
And to determine, and, as found, her to condemn or clear,
By Statute passed in our Queen her seven and twentieth year.
These noble Tryers, justly then examining the Cause,
With reverent Note of her, who heard and spoke to every Clause,
Did, after diverse Days so spent, adiudge, by Verdict true,
Her guilty of most traiterous Conspiracies, not few:
And then from Fotheringhaye themselves to Parliament withdrew.
Of this Infection, that our Peers and People had, and would
Remediles impoyson, if not medeine it we should,
By some Decision of the Lymme whence all the bayne did floe,
Our public Weals Physicians much did argue to and froe.
Did never English Parliament, fully conuented then,
Consist of Noblier, Learneder, Wiser, and Worthier men:
By these it was debated how this common Foe might live,
Without her death whom God to us a common Bliss does give.
Much was it labord, wished much some Course herein might hold,
But to resolve of any none had reason to be bold.
Her still obdurate Malice to her Majesty was clear:
If she preuaild, Religion was assurd an Altring here:
Our Nobles Crocodile, at home, and hence, our Foe-hop't Head:
Then must our Queen, Religion, Realm, or She for them be dead.
Wherefore from Either House were sent the chiefest Men, to crave
Her Highness that the passed Doom might Execution have:
Whereof She asked to be aduisde, and (earnest her to save)
Dismissed them with loving words, and bids them expect
Her Answer shortly: nor did She the sending it neglect:
Though contrary to it that all did, hopingly, affect:
For she, perplexed in that case, did lastly them direct
To study Means how Both might live, the Peril ouer-past:
Which much amazed: yeat solemnly they handell it at last.
Mercy to her, Malice in her might happily prevent,
Was said, but not resolved, for often she Mercy underwent,
When rose the Earls, and other times, yeat never did repent,
But of our Queen to be destroyed had made her Testament.
A straiter Guard, Bonds, Hostages, were also named in vain:
For, should she prize our Queen, who then durst her or the^ detain?
Or what were these to recompense the Loss we should sustain?
For Loyalty to take her Oath, was thought to purpose small:
Such Oaths she often had falsifide, nor thought it Sin at all
To break them to an Heretic (our Queen so Papists call.)
To banish her, were to possess our Foes of their desire,
For us to rid away the Smoke, and run into the Fire,
To set her free to make a Head for them against our Queen:
In few, no safety for us, but in her Death was seen.
So wholly by the Parliament concluded was, and so
Reported to her Majesty, still pitying her Foe.
In more sententious, learned, and delibrate sort, than I
Can set it down, past all touched here: So did her Majesty
In answering erst, and now to them her Answer Aunswer-lesse,
Sweet Adumbrations of her Zeal, Mercy, and Wit express.
But with her Oracle that bod them do, and do it not,
Played they as Alexander did with King Gordians Knot.
OF Spay^s huge Navy, touched before, great Rumor now was spread,
And that th'Inuaders meant to make this Scottish Queen a head:
For which continued doubt of her in English hearts was shead.
Not in the Vulgats only, but some Nobles of this Land,
Who had (not knowing it our Queen) then got into their hand
The Writ of Execution, that her Heading did purport:
The which was executed soon, and in a solemn sort.
This nature-frended Lady, had she bin as wise as witrie,
Who by the Massacres in France had learnt to leave off Pity,
Made there too apt for bloody Acts, the Pope for it too blame,
To take her death, too much deserved, her self did meekly frame.
She bids commend her to her Son, and will him to eschew
All Practices and Policies, for thence her Sorrows grew:
True Romish, Scottish, and true French, tell all my Friends I die.
When Meluin (unto whom she spoke) did, weeping, thus reply,
The wofulst Message, Madame, this that ever me befell,
When of my Queen and Mistress Death I shall the Tidings tell,
She, kissing him, says pray for me, and bids him so farewell.
Then of a debt was due from her she did the payment crave,
And that her Servants might enjoy those Legacies she gave,
And to attend her at her death some of her Own to have.
All which the Earls commissoned did yield unto: and so
She to the black-clad Scaffold, there to take her death, did go.
Now Mary Stewards Troubles shall have ending, once or twice
She said, and not to moan for her did give to Hers advise:
And whilst the Writ in reading was no more regarded it,
Than if it had secured, or concerned her no whit.
Beads at her Girdle hung, at end of them a Medall, and
An Agnus-Dei bout her neck, a crost-Christ in her hand.
They prayed her to set apart those popish Toys, and pray
In says to Christ, in only whom her whole Salvation lay:
And, offering then to pray with her, that Offer she withstood,
Alleging that our Prayers can do Catholics no good.
So does the Popes false Calendar of Saints of Sense bereave
Our Traitors, who dye Papists that therein it them receive.
Was never yet Religion heard so pestilent as this:
Their murdring us for Lawful, of their Creed a portion is.
So had they schooled her, and that her bloody Mischiefs past
Were meritorious, which the Pope would honor so at last.
That, even then, the Gospels Light illuminate her heart
Was prayed of Ours, whilst she with hers prayed, as pleased her, apart.
Then to her woeful Servants did she pass a kind a-dew:
And kissing often her Crucifix, unto the Block her drew,
And, fearles, as if glad to dye, did dye to Papisme true.
Which, and her other Errors (who in all did ever err)
Unto the Judge of Mercy and of Justice we refer.
If ever such Conspirator, of it impenitent,
If ever Soul, Pope-schooled so, that Sea to Heaven sent,
If ever one, ill-liu'd, did dye, a Papist, God-wards bent,
Then happy she. But so, or not, it happy is for us,
That of so dangerous a Foe we are delivered thus.
The bravely managed Journey of the County Sussex, who
Did merit praise beyond my Pen, Sir William Druries too
Made into Scotland, added to Lord Grets sayd-Prowesse theare,
Did lesser rid our Queen and Realm, their Realm and King of fear,
Than Mary's end: Who of her self all Treasons did prefer,
Against either State: our foreign Foes derived Pretexts from her.
But thus Elizabeth has salu'd, Ours, and Scotch Troubles. Now
Ensues we show her Aids to France, who wrought their Broiles, and how.
CHAP. LVII.
IT by a Politician a German (to whose lore,
Have Machiuilian French-Euents since sorted euer-more)
Before Queene-Mother, Charles the King, and others private, was
Aduisde a Monarch absolute in France to bring to pas.
Aristocratick Government, nor Democratick pleased
But where to one Man's Emperie is Monarchia seasde.
He told his Ttauels, and in States his Observations: how,
Besides the only Turk, he none a Monarch did allow:
Who suffers none by Might, by Wealth, or Blood, to ouer-top:
Himself gives all Preferments, and whom lists him does lop.
His Bands of Ianizaries, who are formed and nourisht still
From Childhood his own Creatures, hold all at his own will.
He out of these his Captains, and his Bassies does elect:
They, to deserve their Founders trust, his only Weal affect:
The rather, for their Dignities, and all that All possess
Determine at his will, behooues therefore not to transgress.
Save his Religion, none is used, unless in Conquests late,
And that of Policy, thereby to add unto his State:
Nor even there permits he of Religion to debate.
Nor walled Towns, nor Forttesses, his Empier does digest:
Except upon the Fro(...)ires, for securing of the rest.
His Subjects thus, Theirs, and the whole, at his Devotion, needs
No Imposts, Taxes, or the like, whence Tumult often breeds.
Discourst of his Experience thus, he then descends to it,
Whereby of Monarchia might himself the French King fit.
Whereas (quoth he) French Policy consists of Three Estates,
The Princes, Nobles, Commons, and each one of the other wates
For hearts and helps, and often the King is bridled of those Three,
Himself therefore, such Lets removed, sole Monarch thus might be.
Of ancient Peers, of valiant Men, great Lords, and Wisemen all,
By forced War, or fraudfull peace, to temporize the fall:
Whereto Religions quarrel then presented mean not small.
Mean while, until of them by turns wear Riddance, did behoue
To work them Mal-contents, the King to labor vulgar Love.
Immediately, even from himself, No whit at their Request,
To pass Preferments, not to them, but else as likes him best:
And but of Pieces ru^ous the Great-Ones to possess:
And when his Creatures shall grow to more, those Great to less,
To quarrel then those Nobles, when in them great hearts would lurk
That for the Soldier, or the sword of Justice, should make work.
So to provide that of the States be no Conuention named:
Religion not disputed of: Strong Towns, which often have tamed
The French Kings, be dismantled: And when things as thus be framed,
His Majesty (quoth he) shall hit the Mark whereat is aimed.
WHen this, and worse than thus, this worse than Machiuel had said,
With that Conuenticles Applause, so working was not staid:
For hence, if Accidents we shall observe, may be collected
The civil Wars and Butcheries in France to have effected.
Religion gave the color, whear though infinite were slain,
The Church reformed did not resist, yeat still by loss did gain:
For blood of Martyrs well is said to be the Churches Seed,
Where Massacres have plashed there is spread a triple Breed:
In Sense it seemed a silly Spring, should Europe ouer-floe,
Whence Luther his occasion took against the Gospels Foe:
As Scotlands foresaid Guizian Broils, even France her Tumults so,
From other then Religions cause did at beginning groe:
So (...)ph sold, and Christ betrayed, was meeter than was meant
Of Ben-Iamests, or by the false Iscarots foul Intent:
But howsoever ill haps well, Woe stays whence ill is sent.
French second Henry's Favorites, the Constable, and Guize,
The one of them ambitiously the others Hight enuies:
Each growing cross, and crossing, it to Factions grew at length,
Poor Huge^^tz undoubted then, nor dreamed they of strength:
Alone in Henry's, Francis, and ninth Charles their Reigns, of them
French Papists, as our Mary's, did to Martyrdom condemn.
But by such Law as Wolves do Lambs, those Innocents for most
Were slaughtered, whole Towns sometimes with these in the^ they roast:
And like now threatened Guize against the Saints in every Cost.
The Guizians (so that Faction shall in this Report be said)
Through Nonage of the Pope-taught King, grew Mighty and obayd:
Queene-Mother (Mischief-Mistres) in their Pageant featly played.
Mean while the Royals and the Peers they Practice to betray:
Some in the bloody Massacre at Paris made away.
But what offend I Christian Ears with horror of that deed?
From Sarazens, nor Savages, did ever like proceed.
Let that black Marrage-Feast, when were so many Thousands slain
O^ Saints, at peace with God and men, be never named again:
Let be a Law in every Land, to punish such as speak
That Christians should, like Hel-hou^ds so, with God and Nature break:
Far be it that Posterities should hear, that Charles the King
For such ^oule Murders, bon-fiers bod, and caused Bells to ring.
Yeat tell the Popes Procession, and his Iubilee for this:
For Popes be impudent, and bads their blessings never mis,
To have them Fathers of those Acts, no News at all it is.
But more than twice six years ere this the civil Wars begun,
When on the Lambs of Vassie did the Guizian Butchers run:
(...) when the Edict had given Peace unto the Church reformed,
And odious to the Papists seemed that Peace, who therefore stormed:
And then the Duke of Guize, who erst had figured for the Crown,
Hence calculating hopes, did set his bad Designements down:
Alonly quarrelling, till then, the Princes of the blood,
Who, partly quail'd, were yet vnkild, and to their tackling stood.
So with the Papists band's the Duke himself, not for Devotion,
But aduantagious seemed that Mean for blood-Drifts and Promotion
This Faction thus had Heart and Head, the Other yet unborn,
Till to the Prince of Condie flockt the Hugenotz, forlorn,
And told the savage Butcheries at Vassie newly made,
By ruthles and seditious Guize, on Thousands, whilst they prayde.
Like skathed Sheep, escaped from blood-sucking Dogs, they quake,
Imploring his Protection: which he then did undertake.
Thus, through Necessity, this Part had also Heart and Head:
Even after hundreds thousands such good Christians so were dead.
This known, to him from every Part the Persecuted fly,
So was the Prince of virtuous Troops possessed by and by.
CHAP. LVIII.
NOt Spain this while, that held for France great Signories, did sleep,
But through the Fingers into It, with lusting Eyes, did peep:
At least, by nourishing those Broils, all got She hopes to keep.
For It, whose Situation so Spayns scattred Realms disioynes,
Her Teeth had watered long, and now, to weaken France her Loins,
Against France she France does stre^gthe^ with the Soother of her gold:
And for that Purchase to the Devil, is feared, her Soul is sold.
Directions also came from Rome, that sets all on fire:
That, by what Right or wrong so ere, the Guize should still aspier:
To send the royal Bloods to Heaven or Hell, it skils not how:
Were Pardons sent for Murders: Bulls to clear Allegiance vow:
That, on Damnation, none persuade, much less of Peace allow.
And not alone against France this League was hallowed, but against all
That work the Gospel to erect, whereby the Pope might fall:
Was more than time, troe we, to go, should not the Church us call.
But call did they, and come did we, and to their labors fall,
When wear their Towns demolished, with Slaughters thear not small.
This busy Head of that bad League (for yet the monstrous Beath
Of Sextus Quintus and the Devil, the grand League, had not breath)
This Guize bereft us Calice, and in France our Pieces all:
Then fell in hand with Scotland, thence with us in hand to fall:
This, in his Niece the Scottish Queen her Claim, did all he might
To dispossess Elizabeth our Queen of regal right:
This on the infant-Person of his natural King had seazde:
And prosecuted now in France what Violence him pleased:
The Popes sworn Butcher, and proud Spayns Forerunner, to prepare
Her ways for France and England, which their Owners cannot spare:
This with the blood of Innocents made Channels ouer-floe:
Against this Cham, and his Beau-Peeres, invited English go.
Else, save that God can all, was feared Religion should have quaild,
And Spaniards, nestling over near, had easlier us assaild:
For on the Theator of France, the Tragedy was meant
Of England too: Wherefore our Queen her Interruptors sent.
How Ambrose, Earl of Warwick, did in France high Feats not few:
How bloody Claudie, Duke of Guize, a common Soldier slew:
How Francis, son of that Guize, did civil Broils renew:
The persecuting Francis, Charles, and Henry's, hence a-dew:
Which Kings, as said, to work themselves French Monarchs Atheists played,
Or doting overmuch on Rome, their Realm and selves betrayed:
The skarlet Borbone, whom the Guize a painted French King made:
Salcedo failing Monsiurs Death, which did the League conspier:
The Prince of Orange, murthred through Farnesian Parmas hier:
Renoumed Condie poysned, in his time the Leaguers fear:
How Pope-sent Saunders against our Queen in Ireland Arms did bear:
(For Leaguers then in Policy prevented Lettes each-wheare,
By Armies, or Armadas, or their scattred Iesuists, who
Have had small cause to brag that they with England had to do:)
The often sworn and for-sworne Peace, that happened in the while
Queene-Mother, Phillip, Pope and Guise, the French Kings did begile:
Queene-Mother for Ambition of employment, King of Spain,
To make his Usurpations sure, yea France it self to gain,
The Pope to keep his pomp in plight, Guise for the regal Ring:
How all, though drifting d^ffringly, at length to end did bring
The House Valoys, of Capets Stock, which Stock had quite decaide,
But that it pleases God the Crown in B^rbons House is staide:
How till the Barricados Feast, when Guise vn-vizard was,
Under Religions Cloak the Routes in wasted France did pas,
And after then, both Hugenote, and Papist too, if friend
Unto Valoys, or Burbon, sound like Enmity, or end:
Of Sauoy, (hoping France) his Aids, and ^ll success in all:
How Guise, and Lorraine, in the Pit for Henr^e dig'd did fall:
How Friar James, Pope-blest, and bribed of May^e, did Henry kill:
How unto Main, pursewing Broils, it wrought not to his will:
The Massacres, and Stratagems, did in these Tumults chance:
How God in all his Wars did bless Nauar, now King of France:
And how, next God, that France is French our Queen is Author cheef,
All These, thus blanch't, we leave, and shall of Belgic be as brief.
But be it first remembered how, even for the Parents sin,
God plagueth in Posterity: as came to pass, I win,
In second Henry's, of the Church reformed a bloody Foe,
(Though otherwise a worthy Prince, nor tache we him but so)
And Katherine de Med^^^s, whose Athisme wrought much woe.
These had sour Sons, not one of which did die a natural death,
All (...), (...) Males none extant on the Earth*
No marvel, even to David's Seed, for David* sin, hap't such:
And though the holy Writ contains that Story, yeat to tuch
This Purpose, let us hear it here: Example urges much.
CHAP. LIX.
OF holy David, and his House, the Man approved to be
By Gods own Mouth, to Gods own heart, the ^y Troubles see.
His Soul was ever godly firm, though frail in flesh as Man,
For who of Adams Seed from sin acquit them could or can.)
Our walls of flesh, that close our Souls, God knew too weak, and gave
A further Guard, even every Man an Angel Guide to have:
And Men to us be Angels, whilst they work our Souls to save.
For, ear his Fall, Man was not left unto himself so free,
But that he had a Law, and Those that should his Temptors be,
And tempted, then the Spirit, that for God himself was made,
Was dared by the Flesh, that to the Spirit erst obayde:
The Soul by either labored to thrive, or be betraide.
To err is proper then to Men, but brutish to persist:
With Praise, and Prayer, still to God, as David thriu'd, or mist,
He plied: more in loving God, than living godly blist.
V^gratefull Saul distressing him, or what ear then befell,
Or afterward, nor other then Domestic Greefes we tell.
The troublous Sequels Nathan told of David's House, when as,
To wanton it with Bethsabe, Vrias murthred was,
Effecting now, is only it whereto our Pen shall pas.
Thamar, Ammon, Absalom, Adoniah, also wrought
Unto their Father woe enough: let these on Stage be brought.
Now to the Tribes was David as the Zodiac to the Signs,
Even Signifer to every Prince that circled his Confines.
A Monarche great, in Acts and Fame more great, but great'st of all,
In that he was beloved of God, nor ceast on him to call,
Who rai^d him, often falling, for his Crosses wear not small.
Love-(...) M(...), (...) King of Geshurs Daughter, baire
To David (...) Ab(...)m, and Thamar peereles fair.
No^ ^a^rer ye^^ than vi^^uous, though disastrously she speade:
Su^^ is admyred Beauty that has Worlds of Mischiefs bread.
A(...)mon, whom Ahinoam bore to David, was the cause
Of hers and his own grief, in that he gave his Eyes no laws.
For Love is but a Term, like as is Echo but a Voice,
That This does babble, That does breed, or not, is ours the choice.
And Virtue curbs* Affection, and for Conscience flies sin:
To leave for imperfection, fear, or shame, no praise does win.
But not so happy he as fear, or shame, or ought, might stay
His rankled thoughts, but sick, lust sick, for Thamar Ammon lay:
And subtell Ionadabs Advise did her to him betray.
For never was Pretense so foul but some would flatter it:
No any thing so pestilent as mis-applied Wit.
Seem feeble sick, and when the King thy Father visits thee,
Then feign an Appetite to Meat by Thamar dressed (quoth he:)
Ask that she dress it in thy sight, and of her hand to take it,
When private so, thy Market be as thou do mar or make it,
Well pleased this Counsel: Ammon asks, and David yields anon:
Nor readier David to bid go, than Thamar to be gone:
Like Phil^mela, ^^attering (...)on she might go
With Tereus, that prepos'd her shame, was Thamar forward so:
When either Virgin was surprisde, wheare least they looked a Foe.
So gracious, portly, ^resh, and fair, and (which no less attract)
So modest, witty, affable, had Nature her compact,
That such as, in his Canticles, her Brother has purtrayde
His Loves Idea, litrally might Thamar such be said.
Goodly thus, and gladly then, and not suspecting harm,
She, entering wheare he lodged, did afresh the Leacher warm.
His leasing Sickness then to act by Art was more thau need,
For, seeing her, imagine all his Senses sick in deed.
More could she nor, more should she not, than she in kindness did:
All adding Fuel to the Fire, which yet from her lay hid.
Now, save of him and her, the Room was cleard by his devise,
When he did ask to eat, which she did bring him in a trise:
Full glad (good Soul) her Cookerie might please him any whit,
When not her Cookrie, but her self, his appetite did fit.
Concerning which he breaks with her, indeuoring her consent:
Whereto, amaz'de, she counter-works, nor would for ought relent.
How precious her Virginity, what sin it to defile,
How for their Fathers Lust much woe was prophesied ere-while,
How Incest was much more a sin, she wished him to way,
How permanent the Shame to both: Enough did Thamar say,
To have preserved Virginity, if lust had brook't a Nay.
But pleading tears and words lackt wait, by force he ravished her,
And having forced, he forced not to hide how he did err:
Nor more he loved her ere while, but hates her now as much:
Of Lust and unchaste Coiture still is the Sequel such.
Her now vn-virgin'd Eyes did shame to view the common Light:
She therefore would have stayde, at least not come in common Sight,
Supposing, by her Blusshings, all would aim her altered Plight.
But out his Doors by violence he shuts her, wherefore
Aloude she cride, with bitter tears, her fair attier she tore,
And did all Signs of sorrow, whilst the cause admired was.
But when her Br^ther Absalom found how had come to pas,
He comforts her in all he might, and to his house conuaies
His woeful Sister, wheare thenceforth, as desolate, she states:
Like Prognes sister pensive, but her moodes wear milder still,
This wanting will, not speech to rail, That wanting speech not will.
Contrary-wise thought Absalom of nothing but Revenge,
And with his choler think ye him these thoughts among to menge:
Did Cadmus, for his Sister rapt'e, so many Countries rome?
And shall I sleep my Sisters Rape, that may be quit at home?
For Rape of one, scarce honest, was at Troy such tenne-years Fight?
And shall one easy bloe seem much, sweet Thamers wrong to right?
Simean and Leui, worthy Sons of Israel our grand Sier,
Ye in revenging Dinas Rape have set mine heart on fire,
Even mine, that for a greater wrong should greater things acquier.
Poor Sychem, thou did love in deed, and Marriage Rites affect:
Lewd Ammon, thou did lust in deed, and then thy Rape reject:
Poor Sichem, she a Stranger was whom thou so much did wowe:
Lewd Ammon, she thy Sister was with whom thou had to do:
Poor Sichem, thou to prize thy love, did leave thy Gods for hers:
Lewd Ammon, thou to please thy lust, no God at all prefers:
But howsoeare in these, in this no diffrence shall remain:
Poor Sichem he was slaughtred: lewd Ammon shall be slain.
Nor more Maachas goodly Son in stomaking did threate,
Then did this News his Father now offensiuely disqueat^*
At point almost to act as much as Absalom did plot,
Even Ammons death, had not he been his Isiue first-begot.
For which he erst had hild him Dear, and present Nature wrought,
And that himself had amorous Slyps, is likely too, he thought:
But howsoeare, in woe enough, he ouerpafseth it
Which Absalom, in wroth enough, nor could, nor would, forgi^:
Two years in silence, neartheles, he labord of that fit.
To his Sheep-shearing David and his Brethren he inuites,
(Such as our Wakes, conuenting Kinnes to Feastings and Delights)
Now listen what Catastropha this Stratagem behightes.
The King excused his coming, whom his Son importunes so,
As Ammon, and his other Sons, had leave, and will, to go.
Unwilling though (by what Presage I wot not) David seemed
Of Ammens going: but what Fate ordained has none redeemed:
Go did he, wheare full merrily he frollicked that tide,
When, by his Ostes Attendants there, of sudden Wounds he did:
(Which after Bar quet did their Lord for only him provide.)
More skar'd than hurt the other Sons of David flead with speed,
Yeat ear their home-returne the King had notice of the deed,
And feared much their safety, till them he saw, and then
Was such confused sorrow, more was never seen among'st Men.
To Geshur Absalom escapes, three years an Exile thear,
Till David's kind relenting heart to Ioah did appear:
By means of whom, recalled home, he lastly purchased Grace,
Yea well-appay'd was David, if wear Absalom in place.
Loved never Father more a Son than him his Father lon'd,
Proved never Son ungrateful more than he ungrateful proved:
For having stolen the Peoples hearts, by affable Pretexts,
He faines his vows at Hebren, but the Diadem affects:
And, by collected Forces theare, distressed David more
Than S(...)le, the Cananites, or all, hap't after, or before:
Enforcing him to fly the Land. But dwell we not of this:
God never fauor'd such Attempts, or ever sailed His.
When David seemed, in common Sense, already on the hip,
Was Absalom himself ore-throne, whom God made ouer-slip,
What, wisely, false Achitophel had counseld him to do,
Whose Counsel not received, he hong himself, and worthy too,
Ambitious Absalom, now foiled, as on his Mule he flead,
Was carried underneath an Oak, wheare, caught-vp by the head,
(Eue^ by those bewtious Locks, of which, and him, such praise is read)
He sight (and cause he had) and said (or say he might) that All
Which so unjustly seek to clime, most justly so do fall.
But whatsoeare he thought or spoke, this holds autenticall:
We think no greater bliss than such to be as be we would,
When blessed none, but such as be the same that be they should
Had one Man all that all Men have, he nothing had, vulesse
He also had a Soul that All as nothing did possess.
Natures Mynion, Eyes Admier, and now in-ayred Earth,
(For, hanging, Ioabs ruthles spear had vented vital breath,
Although the King his counter-maunde should have contraried so)
Effected had his Fathers heart no Substance else but woe:
So kind and ouer-kinde was he in moving such a Foe.
But thus of this, and thus to him this following Cross did groe.
CHAP. LX.
TO epilogue our Tragedy, now Adoniah acts,
With whom, old David to depose, even ^oabs-selfe compacts:
Yeadiuers Captains did revolt, and with the Son rebel,
Which not a little grieved the King, that loved the young man well,
Who, next his brother, did for form, and soly now excel.
Remayn'd but Solomon and he, and he the elder Son,
Too forward yeat to practice Rule ear David's Reign was done:
But what God means is, Amen: The Scepter was behight
To Solomon, and Nathan moved the King to do^ him right:
Who, aged, caused his Diadem to ro^alize the head
Of Solomon, anointed now: which heard, the Rebel, flead.
So have we seen, not years farre-past, long-Plottings ouerthrone,
Even in a trise, to day a Queen, to morrow less than none:
Such was her Fate, but not her Fault, that stood for Mary's Throne*
Nor cite I this A Novelty, like Pul-backs many an one.
Repentant* Ad^ni^^ now unto the Altar flies,
(...) thereof: which one, that it espies,
(...) unto Solomon, that sent to fetch him Thence:
Which would not be, till swore the King to pardon his offense:
Whom^ (...) we now so pardoned, or rather in suspense.
^or though a Kings Competitor in one same Land may live,
^eat take he heed, the sleightest cause a cause of death does give:
Which happened him, ear happened this, inserted by the way.
David decea'ste, in Solomon was sole and sovereign Sway,
When (...), to prolong his life, did at the Altar stay.
Even he that in so many Brunts for David did prevail,
That, save for Adoniah now, Allegiance near did fail,
That, save for sheaded blood of twain, could none impeach of wrong,
Even this grand* Captain of the Hosts, a luckie Knight so long,
Hand-fasting now the Altar, clames that Privilege, in vain:
For thence he would not, and the King commands he th^are be slain.
Which Warrant did Banaiah serve: And so this Worthy died,
For Abner and Amasas blood, like-Worthies and as-tried.
With Adoniah now remains we act and end our Scenes,
To who^, might seem, small good was meant, what il soere he means:
Wheare crowned Might, and crossed Right so near together dwell,
Behooues that Forrest-flying Fear, whereof the Fox did tell:
Our factious Lancaster and York thereof could witness well.
Abisag, David's Hebe, that in comfort of his Age,
Attended him at Bed and Boarde, when natural heat did swage,
Howbeit still a Virgin, and the goodliest Wench alive,
Enamours Adoniah: at the least with her to Wiue
He drifteth, not detayned but for Salomons consent:
Of which he moved Bethsabe, for which she Suitor went
To Solomon, that thearewithall was only not content,
But also took occasion hence, of more, perhaps, than meant.
^^na^ah, by the Kings Command, did Adontah slay:
For Crime perhaps, perhaps because a Crown might come in Plea.
For Sal^mon, divinely wise, could Subtellizings sound:
That much the Ma^d knew David's mind, that Many she had bound,
Whilst gracious erst, with Benefits: her Kindred strong he found:
That ^oa^ and Abiat^ar wear on his Brothers side:
That his aspiring sleeps, nor must be slept, the King espide:
Or else-what? Adoniah was dispatched out of hand:
So sped his Su^e, so was confirmed to Solomon the Land:
If Others otherwise, not I as others understand.
Nor better Meede for Merits could these Dauidists allege:
Yeat did their Father eat the Grapes that set their Teeth on edge.
Then charitable, godly-wise, and continent, wear fit
Should Parents be: So prosper they, Theirs, and whom Theirs begi^*
Of Scotland, quieted by our Queen, and France, by her kept French,
Is touched: Of Belgike, long selfe-vaind, rests how the blood does ste^ch.
CHAP. LXI.
THe Inquisition threatened, wrought in ^etherlanders fear,
And Signs of altring Regment in their ancient State appear.
As ful-fead Children with their Food, by Peace this People play,
Till, in world-matchles Wealth, did them Security betray.
They hearing what King Philip meant against their State, did mind
What in the Fables Moral of the Stock and Storke we find:
For in the Lady Regent, and her Brother Philips Corse,
Their hearts presa^g'd like diff^ence (...) twixt Rigor and Remorse.
Armed was the Duke of Al^^, who by wars, by wiles, by (...)
Should co^quer, circu^uent, consume, those Lands, their Lords, the Good.
These to have his sufficed not the Spanish King, unless
He Monarchize their Land, their Laws, and Liberties depresse.
The other Part their Consciences and Privileges pleades,
Nor other cause than only thus to Armor Either leads:
For There, Elsewhere, and ever Spain, when Spain would Scepters lurch,
Concludes for Spain, though ever Spain begins for holy-Church.
No Army was as yet ariu'd, when as the Belgies send
To Philip their Submission, and their Loyalty defend.
If ought were done contrary to his Edicts (as indeed
Wear some too busy Protestants did order-les proceed)
They did transpose that Fault upon those Innouators rude,
Protesting all that Subjects should: and humbly too they sude,
(Vouching his Father, Uncle, Kings, and Emperors of old,
Who let their Liege-men, diffring from their own, Religion hold)
To suffer Consciences in them to God-wards uncontrolled.
For which, and for their Charters, did they offer Masses large,
And evermore to undergo all Truage, Tax, and Charge.
But Theirs to alter quite in all was it that Philip meant:
To plague therefore those seauenteene Shires was war-flesht Alua sent.
This, like a pleasing poysning Aspe, to act as he did arm,
At first Ariuall, Pardons did for passed Crimes proclaim.
That tractable kind People so he to his bent did frame:
Who wear not then by hostile Means men easily to tame.
He thus of them possessed, did then, by she Degrees, surprise
Their Towns, puts Garrisons in them, built Cittadels likewise,
Inducts the Inquisition, and strange Imposts did devise,
Calls pardoned Crimes in question, faines the Guiltles to offend,
And thus an Aspe, and poysnous more, proved Alua in the end.
For why? ambitious men seek, get, possess, and practice State,
With restles minds, by fawning Means, enuide, in haughty rate.
This privileged Province, and this Paradized Earth,
Thus stripped of her golden Fleece, and faintly drawing breath,
Was phisickt of Elizabeth, who with her English Balm,
Then much the poysnous biting of that Spanish Aspe did calm:
Even Pity preaching thus to her, that Nature is reuerst,
When as her self is not amongst her own Consorts dispersed.
She to that Countries Father, to that honorable Knight,
The Prince of Orange (armed then in Christ his cause to fight,
And for his Countries Weal) permits first Voluntary Aides*
And lastly, when that neighbor Fire too nearly her inuaides,
And their extreme Oppression did her Charity inuit,
(By Ambassies Spain often moved to do the Belgies right,
Howbeit touching Peace in vain) she franklier friends their Cause,
Who were enforced to defend their Faith, their Lives, and Laws,
Against their Souls and Bodies foes, and (which might all perceive)
Our cause was handled then in theirs, then theirs were ours to leave,
And (which had often aided us in many a Field and Fleet)
In ancient Love with Burgundy to break, was thought vnmee^e.
How thence tyrannous Alu^ was revoked, all too late,
When pitiously declined was their flourishing Estate:
Ere which Oppression, where the King had countles Profits thence,
Now Ownes he nothing there, not held with infinite Expense.
The Slaughters, Sackings, Mutin^es, the Kings Vicegerents sense,
How Horn, and Egmo^d, Cou^ties brave, through Aluas Athisme spead,
How Orange, through our Soueraigns Aids preuaild, how lastly dead,
How Anioy also, through her Means, became that Peoples head,
How now her Highness only-selfe, next God, does them secure,
Her valiant Warriors there, whose Lauds might special Pens allure,
And here meanwhile should ours, save that I cannot but divine,
Their Chivalry to be reserved for higher Muse than mine,
To which though some we leave, yeat (by their leaves) of some is said
Themselves they have misgouern'd, and their Companies be^rayd,
Some More regard of Tents, and Trash, in their Retreats have made,
Then of our craised men, may such Conductors low be laid,
And some too ruthles, rioteus, and of their Charge r^misse,
Have starued up in number more than Foe-slaine number is:
Thus some of some report, perhaps not true in all as told:
But somewhat somewhere faulteth, for no fire, no smoke be bold.
To be officious gets Friends, plain Dealing hated is:
Yeat better plainly to reprove, than fainedly to kiss:
We cannot also love our Friends, and flatter their A-misse:
How these and thus-like passed Thear we ouer-passe it here.
Note in our Queen, Religion wronged, it self did ever cheer:
And whear the Spanish Nimrod hunts in Monarchy to hold
All Christendom, through God, that Drist she chiefly has controld.
Nor did, nor does, nor shall it need, that thus to her be told:
Who would to God, but works no good, who seeks fame by ease,
Comes short of both, no less than Mappes to very Lands and Seas.
Not only thus, but in this while, her Fleetes have oftentimes
Set prosprously her Men on Shore, even in the farthest Climes:
Whence have they brought, by fair Co^merce, great riches to our Land,
Or glorious Spoils from such as did their peace or Prowse withstand,
Of which shall be digested here the Progress, in some part,
Though stately be the Subject, and too slender be our Art.
THE ELEVENTH BOOK OF ALBIONS ENGLAND.
CHAP. LXII.
INexplicable Nature, by the God of Nature wrought,
Makes things seem Miracles to some, by some not Wonders thought:
And every Climates People, both as they are Men, and live,
Do differ: nothing, if observed, She, not admired, does give.
The Workman rather than the Work extol we though in her,
Not curiously, and all things to his Providence refer.
Who reads Sir John de Mandeuil his Travels, and his Sights,
That wonders not? and wonder may, if all be true he wrights.
Yeat rather it believe (for most, now, modernly approved)
Than this our Story, whence suppose he was to Travel moved:
Not contradicting though such Pens that write, perhaps more true,
That Penance him to Pilgrimage hence, of Devotion, drew.
Of noble Parentage and rich was Mandeuil exact,
To whom nor Feature, Actiuenes, not Peoples favor lackt.
A Lady of the royal blood, Third Edwards Cozen say her,
On whom, for rarest Raritie, might Arte-spent Nature stay her,
Inamours him: who held it sin, if ought he should omit,
Might please and prize his Lady, coucht in Courage, Cost, or Wi^.
But quite it quail'd his hopes to think he strained to reach a Star:
Often Reason therefore did with Love, Love did with Reason war.
She is too high: and what of that? it has, thinks he, bin seen,
As High have stoopt as Low: For Love, right-labourd, wins a Queen.
And can I hope that Beauty, which is Adamant to all?
Yea, Beauty, Natures Iuy-bush, each Passenger does call.
But should she love (Fool that I am to hope, that should despair)
Such Births as she not else must love, but as they licen'st are.
Yeat were it Mandeuil she loved, mislike it then that list,
Our virtuous Love, elsewhere, as here, should render us as blist.
O, would she love, it should suffice: such, not of vulgar Moulds,
Who^ once she blesses with her Love, for him she Sooth-fast holds:
The only virtue of which Love, all other Lacks controules.
Such were his Ebbes, and Floods, and down although in Love he sits,
Yeat, fearing Death and her disdain, his Heart devoured his Fits:
(...)* (...) not meanly passionate, he fared not as those,
Whose melancholy Fooleries be Pylots to their Woes.
Already was he gracious both with her and all the Court,
And, more to be, did exercise Arms, and each active Sport,
Nor was he Stoicall in ought, but affable in all,
And apprehends, even fleelingly, her Humors as they fall.
If sad were she, then sad was he: if merry, merry too:
His Senses liable to all, she did, or did not do
If her he heard to speak, he said Cassandr^ spoke less true,
If her he saw, hers praisde he more than Cythereas hew,
If odorifrous Sents he smelt, he fathers them on her,
If but her hand he touched, that Touch did highly him prefer,
But D^ified swore he him her bed-game Sweets might taste,
And swore his Thoughts: for where we love, eue^ there our Souls be plaste.
His Virtues and Officiousnes to her wards, so had wrought,
That unto little less than love she, by Degrees, was brought.
Then errant Knights, and every Knight, yea Kings, would ost defend
The Beauties of such Damsels as it liked them to commend:
And Prizes were preposde for such, whose Champions bore the^ best
At Tilts and Turnies, and his Dame was Sovereign ore the rest.
Such Iusts, in England to beheld, were now proclaimed, and
The Chivalry of Christendom conuented in our Land.
Three beauteous foreign Ladies, with Sir ^ohn his Lady stood
Competitors, to win the Prize, maintained with Champions good.
To show the fourfold March of Knights, whose Prowse should plead ano(...),
Under distinguished Flags her Form their Fancies waited on,
Their rare Accomplements, and each Devise to see or reed,
To show the richnes of the Prize, behight the Victors Meede,
The Damsels richer hew for whom such Triumphs were decreede,
The Cost, and great Concurse was there, were ouer-long to say:
In few, was nothing wanting that might honor such a day:
Now sound they to the Iusts, and now vn-horst was many a Knight:
For Foyles were Foyles: most bravely al their Sides and selves acquit:
And almost grew the day to end, before it could be seen
Who bore him best, when, mounted well, both Man and Horse in green,
A Knight appeared: his Banner had the Picture, and in gold
King Edwards Cozen Elenor was legibly inrould.
Against him ran right hardy Knights, that thundered on his Beuer:
But he unhorsed the most of them, himself vn-horsed never:
At least he lost his Stirrops, that incounters him, wherefore
Of Knighthood he, and Elenor the Prize of Beauty bore,
The Jewels set for Victory, and adverse Banners three,
Were yielded him: whilst Elenor did long her Knight to see.
But as they marched to depart, with Beuer shut, he made
To her a Conge: closely then he Thence himself conuaide.
Much wonder all, who, and of whence the hardy greene-Knight was:
But secretly, unknown of all, he to his Home did pas.
A Woman's Love is Riuer-like, which, stopped, will ouer-flow,
But when the Current finds no let, it often falls too low:
Fair Elenor wished nothing more than that she might him know:
Mean while the Fire of Love in her, from sparks to flame did grow.
But Mandeuil was more discreet, than that for Men's applause,
He would be known: as knowing that from Praise takes Envy cause.
To move the King threats death, in vain to labor her he wist,
Since many mighty Potentates had labord her, and mist.
She, he, and England seemed too near, his hopes, thought he, too far:
He absence therefore points to plead unto his Love in bar.
Of Ceur-de-Lion, Erigen, William the Pylgrim, who
Wrote Richards Syrian Watres, Curson, Glanuile, and Longe-spee too,
Long-shanks, ear King, his Knights, and of our English many more,
That through the triple Orbs did Arms and Travels undergo,
And famous thus alive, and dead, Here and Abroad, did groe,
He called to mind: resolved in mind his Life to finish so.
Bills of Exchange and all things else, prepared for Travel fit,
Unto his Friends, grieved he would go, he then imparteth it.
The King did give him Letters for safe-Conducts, of the Corte
Loath him to leave, he takes leave: But in more special forte
Of Elenor: good Sadness she thus spoke, as if in sport.
Some discontented humor sends you hence, as I divine,
Which, be whereso you shall, will be with you, be yours as mine.
Nay, Madam, quoth Sir John, twixt us this diffrence is to find,
I both in mind and body, you need travel not in mind.
Yes yes, quoth she, my Mind I wot meet may you far away,
If so, then give to it this Ring, and that I sent it say.
So, with that Riddle, and a Ring she gave, they kindly part:
Nor knew she him the Knight she meant, nor he to him her heart:
For, second to the greene-Knight (whom she thought not him) was he
Her Fauoret, of him therefore she would remembered be.
Now let us say the Lands, the Seas, the People, and their Lore,
This Knight did see: whom, touching which, not story shall we more:
But to our English Voyages, even in our times, shall frame
Our Muse: and what you hear of Theirs, of his the like do ame,
For Countries, not for Customs (then, and now, not still the same.)
