Yeat interlace we shall, among, the Love of her and him:
Mean while about the World our Muse is stripped now to swim.
CHAP. LXII.
FRom then, when first my Father, ear my birth, was one of those,
Did, through the Seas of ysie Rocks, the Muscouites disclose,
We shall our English Voyages, the chief at least, digest,
Of which in this her Highness Reign have been performed the best,
And herea while let Mandeuil and his Beloved rest.
To name the diverse Peoples that in Europe be, wear much,
Not but remotest Regions, of our Natives seen, we touch.
But, Moderns, Ye (of whom are some have circum-sail'd the Earth)
Here pardon us your Sails, and give your proper Praises bearth.
Infuse ye Penn-life too into ore-taken Fames by death.
Caboto (whose Cosmographie and selfe-proofe brake the Ise
To most our late Discouerers, Debtors to his Advise)
Had us, ear Spain, possessed of that which Spaniards now abuse,
But, he inuitin^g, idly we did offered Gold refuse.
Yeat him to say for most the Mean, it wear not us to shame,
Of English new Discoueries, that yield us Wealth and Fame.
Reserve we to the Actors though (of whom lost some their Wealth,
Their lives a many, all at least endangered their health,
In trewer Perils, and more brave Achieuements, than the Tails
Of Jason and Ulysses, of their fabled Sea-toyld Sails)
The Glory of the dangerous Gole: Nor let us here forgit
(In which I first did breath this Air) London, preferring it.
Some Merchants theare, of Worth, did mind with Nations, then, unknown.
New Traffiques, and the Pass thereto was by Caboto shown.
By his Instructions and their costs three Ships were rigged out,
Hugh Willoughby the Admyrall, a Knight both wise and stout.
Next place (whose brave performance of Employments ever live)
To Chancellor, grand Pilot for that Voyage, did they give.
Now sail they for the Northeast Parts, Cathayas Shores to find:
Incountred with huge Seas of Ise, with stormy Gustes and Wind.
Scotland, Aegeland, Halgland, th'Isles of Roste, and Lofoot past,
Tempestiously Arzinas Rhode received Sir Hugh at last.
Theare he, and all of two his Ships, attempting bootles shifts,
Wear in that Climate Frozen dead, shut up with isie Driftes.
Thus died he and all with him, if so to die be death:
But no, says Heaven, no, says their Fame, suruiuing them on Earth.
Then Chancellor, his only Ship remaining of that Fleet,
For Fynmarke, at the VVardhouse sails with his Conforts to meet.
There day it is two months of length, and Mal-strands Poole it makes.
Such hidious rore, devouring floods, that ten miles distance shakes.
Wheare frustrate of his Friends in quest, with courage not deiect,
He for the Course preposed did his ventrous Sails direct.
KIng Arthur, Malgo, Edgar, once to have subdewd are said
Orkney, Gotland, Island, and those former in that Traide:
Gronland, VVireland, Curland, and cold Scrikfyn them obayde,
Newland, with others, and those Isles wheare men, save Eyes alone,
Are hid in hides of Beasts, and Beasts save Fish, have Fothernone.
Now Chancellor, ariuing mong'st the Laplande^s, at last,
They seeing uncouth Men and Ships, wear wondringly agaste,
(For ear that day was heard no Ship that churlish Pole had past.)
The Lapland Bay wheare he ariu'd, now called Saint Nicholas Bay,
Though Russiah, fifteen hundredth Miles from Mosco is away:
Theare Winterd he at Newnox, till Safe Conduct being sent,
Thence to their King on swift-drawne Sleads through frozen ways he went.
Not like Sarmatian Scithians (for the Moscouites be so)
He found them plain, but rather much in Pomp to ouer-floe,
They never in the Russian Court till then did English see:
His intertainment therefore was as stately as might be.
In sundry Rooms wear hundreds seen in Gold and Tyssue clad:
A Majesty, Augustus-like, their King inthroned had.
Let pass what paste in speech betwixt our Pilot and their King:
Full well could Chancellor demean himself in every thing:
Let pass how in Basilius Court most royally he fead:
Suffice it that our Agent of his arrant thither spead:
That is, that Ours might trade with the^, of which large leave is read.
More, not unworthy note, here of our purpose is to say,
But this be first of Mandeuil remembered by the way.
CHAP. LXIV.
WE left him formed a Trauelet (brave Pilgrim Knight farewell,)
And Elenor (sweet Soul) in Love, with whom she could not tell.
He travels for to leave his Love, not caring where he lose it,
She for her Love to find, it skils not whom, so him that Oose it.
The greene-Knight, be whosoever he shall, her heart had branded hers:
Wheare is that Second She that Love for Virtue so prefers?
Her only, speed howso he shall, his heart had branded his:
Wheare lives that second He that so, by loving, betterd is?
It Is often seen, Love works in Man a weak dejected mind,
It Is ever seen, a Woman's Love does alter as the wind:
Example then be Mandeuil for Men, not to be idle
In Amorous Passions: Labor is to love, at least, a Bridle:
Example too be Elenor: But let us say no more,
For Women ever alter, says the Gospel preached before.
Conclude we then, when Elenor and Mandeuil did die,
The Method of true loving did with them to Heaven fly,
For ever since too fervent in their Love are Men, for most:
But, Iris-like, in Women's Hearts Love too and fro does post.
One Stafford, of a Noble House, a Courtiour of good hauor,
A friend, and fast to Mandeuil, and in the Prince his Favor,
From Gyprus, from his Friend received two Letters, one was his,
The other sent to Elenor: and that purported this.
Of you received I a Ring, a Token to your Mind,
If so I met it: and it is my fortune it so find.
For if the Heart may, as it might, for Mind be understood,
My Heart is yours, your Ring so mine: Hearts interchang'd were good.
More did I fear, than ever in your Ladyship I found,
Disdainefull Looks, from those fair Eyes that me with love did wou^d.
Now speak I Love, far from those Looks so forceable to kill:
Howbeit that I love is not to work or wish you ill.
Not more than this (though Princes Frowns bear death with them) feared I:
For had you loved, the King mislikt, what had I for'st to die?
Wheare I have been (were blasphemy from Women to detract)
Great store of Beauties have I seen, but none as yours exact.
Courts also more than stately, with fair Ladies in the same:
Which seemed common Forms to me, remembering but your name.
When in the Holly-land I prayed, even at the holy Grave,
(Forgive me God; a sigh for sin, and three for Love I gave,
Against the fearce Arab^ans I the Soldans Pay did take,
When often, at O^set, for Saint George, Saint Elenor I spoke.
The Amazones, those lusty Girls, believe me liked me Well:
But nothing in the best of them but does in you excel.
I looked upon the sterile Lake where Heauen-fir'd Sodom was:
For one, thought I, here such as you not so had come to pas.
Most sweet and Sovereign Balsamum in Indian Fields I saw:
More soueraign sweeter Se^ts, thought I, my lips from yours could draw.
Nere Aethiop when the spice-sweete Well of youth I saw, I said,
My Lady lacks not hereof, Perfection needs not Aid.
I saw (nor wonder you I should, who sees you sees as much)
The only Phoenix: Foul, and Faier, but it, and you, none such.
But, flying thus about your Blaze, your Gnat does burn his wings:
To my despairing Passion more your praised Beauty brings.
Not Travel tiers my Love a whit, but Love does tire on me:
Which should I wish, me better, or you baser of degree?
Be still the same you are, let me exile my self for ever:
Two diffidenses I conceit will let me hope you never:
The first my self, unworthy you, the latter, and the least,
The Kings Consent: But, well I wot, Love is a Lordly Feast:
Aguize (so should you) so, and so despair is part releast.
One comfort is, before you doom is Execution done:
My voluntary Banishment already is begone.
Which if you never shall repeal, shall never end, or when
(Ah, can I hope it?) should you, not for us is England then.
Nor is it but our minds that make our native Homes our Grave)
As we to Ours, Others to theirs like parciall Fancy have:
Transmute we but our Minds, and then all one an Alien is,
As if a Native: One resolved makes every Country his.
Your Answer that by Pen our speech to this returned shall be,
Voutsafe it unto Stafford, for an Other-I is he:
In perfect Friendship no suspect, for two are one in all,
Community or doubling joy, or making grief more small,
But would you to an Unity of hearts twixt us incline,
Wheare Friendship is angelical, our Love should prove divine.
More write I not: blessed may you live: tears overtake this Line.
When (Stafford present) Elenor this Letter had perus'de,
She said, as elsewhere shall ensew: Now is in us infus'de
Fresh matter of Discoueries. How Chancellor he speade
Is said before: of Russia thus remains to be read.
CHAP. LXV.
AVaste and spacious Empier is Moscouie, in the same
Be Rivers, Tanais, Volga, and Boristhenes of fame,
With yearly hallowed Mosca, which the Primate having blessed,
(Whom to attend the Clargie, Lords, and King himself be pressed)
He thinks himself an happy Man may touch the yse-hewne Pit,
But him in Heaven already whom the Primat sprinks with it.
Euxinus, and the Caspian Seas, do wash those frozen Shores,
Which us with fish, oils, ho^ny, salt, furs, and good Traffiques stores.
More temprate be the Inland Parts: They reap what they do Sow
Within the compass of four Moons, inn all their harvest, mow,
And house their Beasts: Themselves keep close in Stoues until the Spring,
And sport with their face-painted Wives, hild thear a comely thing.
In Customs of the Greek Church, much corrupted, are they lead:
Monks, Friars, and Priests swarm theare, not more tha^ in their Portesse
read:
Nor more have Priests, or People, than a ceremonious care,
Gross Worshippers of Images, which in their Houses are:
In all too superstitiously devoted. Though the Pope
Theare stickell not, their Primate takes as large and pompious scope.
Besides these Christians (for unto themselves they arrogate
The soundest Christianity) are subject to their State
Idolators, that do adore even Devils, or did of late.
Not of the Samoeds rude-wrought Gods, or blood-Rites will we tarry,
Or of the Stone, near unto which did Willoughby miscarry,
To which bring Sailors whit-Meats, least their Ships should Tempests harrie,
But that same female-Idoll called Zelotibab, in part
Of Russia, or the Devil himself acting in it his Art,
Is worth the note. When ought amiss amongst them does befall,
An Instrument of Music, and a silver Toade withal
They lay before the Idol, and before her prostrate fall.
Then, Music sounded, he to whom the Toade shall come is slain,
(For come it will) when presently the man reuiues again,
And tells the cause why hap't the ill, and how to pacify
The angry Idol: which is done, though some for it should die.
The King by Monarchia rules, more absolutely none,
Great Duke of Russia late his Style, imperial now his Throne.
He holds a Majesty not mean, and all of All his Own,
When to employ their goods upon the common good is known:
Himself, both Judge, and Iuror, ends with Equity Debates.
Armipotent in Warte, and has subdewed mighty States.
An hundred thousand leads he forth against his Foes to fight,
That scorn both hunger, thirst, and cold, wounds, yielding, fear and flight.
Of cloth of gold, rich Stones, and Plumes, his royal Tent is pight:
Nor to his Soldiers skants he Gifts, that well themselves acquit.
But what particulate we thus, that much in few would write?
NOw wheare we left of Chancellor: he gracious with that King,
Obtained for our Merchants, as he wished, every thing.
With Letters then of Credence for himself, and Mart for them,
He puts to Sea for England, whom theyse about did hem.
But with undaunted Courage, to his never dying Fame,
All Difficulties ouerpast, to London safe he came.
Thence, after some aboade, with new Consorts, an other Fleet,
And Notes digested for their new attempted Traffic meet,
He did resayle to Russia, there received as before:
Chief Agents Gray, and Killingworth, bearded five foot and more.
In all things with the King for ours did Chancellor prevail,
And now our Age^ts knew their Homes, and where to make their Sail.
But now must end our Swan-song, now the Swan himself must end,
Even he, that toyld such tedious Seas his Countries weal to mend,
Returning Homewards, near at Home, even on the Scottish Cost,
Did wrack, and those aboard his Ship then perished for most.
But that he drowned his care to save the Russie, sent to us
In his Conduct, is said the cause: but drowned he was, and thus.
This Mart, thus set a foot, was Thence for Ob an other sought:
Wherein not little was it, that praiseworthy Borough wrought.
Of him and (whom I never can commend with praises dew)
Of Ienkinson ensues: But first of Elenor anew.
CHAP. LXVI.
YE heard how she his Letter read, whilst Stafford was in place,
Which having read, she stood as if astonished a space.
Her blusshing, and vn-blusshing, made that Stafford doubted whether
It pleased, or displeased: which, the troth to say, did neither.
Variety of Men to court a Woman is her pride,
Than which their Vanity of Men is nothing less espyde.
What are to us, but common hutts, those common hopes they give,
If then their Love does dye to us, when ours to them does live?
They Nero-like sing Troy, when Rome by them is set on fire,
Jest at our earnest. Let us now to ^lenor retier.
More modest she tha^ such (though most, in Men's behalf, might better)
And comes, quoth she, from Mandeuil this ill-come-welcome letter?
Believe me, Stafford, ill it comes that says a cause in me,
That from his Native home he should thus selfe-exiled be.
If amorous Hopes, or Hopes vnheld, to him from me had past,
I had indeed bin guilty, as too forward, or not fast.
But to the Cause, Effect, and all, not-guiltie, do I plead:
His Love is News, mine to despairewhat was it him should lead?
Our Court (I will not wrong the Man, nor flatter him a whit)
Can hardly show another-such for Person, Prowse, and Wit.
But as for me (setting apart my Birth, to which, or any
So borne, our State is interest) what am I more than Many?
If beauteous now (here let me chide his Indiscretion, who
Far from a Mean of it so mean does make so much ado,
And least perhaps he should have glib'd, a virtue does not note,
Whence love should spring, which makes me guess he does not love but dote)
If beauteous, as I said, what else is Form but vaiding Air?
Yea often, because assaulted often, it hurts to be fair.
And were not my Descent, and I a Favorite in Court,
My common Form, vnheeded then, might pass without report.
But, were my wishes mine, the Court by me should be the less:
So much it has of Vanity, and painful Idleness.
Since such is he, and of my self my self but so esteem,
Himself by Silence, me he wronged, disdainful me to deem.
I could be angry, were he here, with him for erring so,
Disdain (the Volgars Fault) is not in Gentry found, I troe.
But fear he did, and wisely too (for God forbid that I,
Unworthy that I am, should have endangered him to dye)
The Kings displeasure: Or, perhaps, impossible he thought
My love should equal his, or I a trans-Marine be wrought.
But if he intertain'd such thoughts, there also did he err:
Love is a lordly Feast, he writes, and I the same auerre.
For if (not rashly though, I hope) upon my Choice I stand,
My hand shall never give my heart, my heart shall give my hand:
Yea, so I live with whom I love, what care I in what Land?
With Women is too usual now Theirs, and themselves to sell
For Ioynters, by Indenture, with imperious Men to dwell,
And he does her, and she does him, with his, and hers vpbrayde:
But that I chiefly match for love shall, when I match, be said.
Good Days beget, bad Days try Friends, nor him a friend esteem,
Whom firm, as to thy self thy self, thou dare not to deem,
Say some: But Mandeuil, I see, of you accompteth so,
As of his firm and choycest Friend, then, least I tedious groe,
I tell thee, Stafford, next to one, is Mandeuil the Man
I could have loved, but I love, whom not vn-loue I can.
Yeat if you ask me whom, or where, that one beloved is,
I cannot answer whom, or where, yeat am, and will be his.
Madame, quoth Stafford, yet your speech has head, nor foot, nor Middle,
Not naming him, you end a Sphynx, and tie me to a Riddle.
Well, friend you are to Mandeuil, nor foe (she said) to me:
The greene-Knight, Victor at the Iusts a few years past, is he.
In sooth, quoth Stafford, if for him be resolute your Choice,
Choose not again, with you for him consort I also Voice.
Nor think, in that I him prefer, I Mandeuil reject:
Friendship may brook Triplicitie, and shall in this respect.
For your own sake, and for his sake (than Mandeuil no less
My Friend) I shall (his Souls desire) you of that Knight possess.
Almost an Ecstasy of Joy her from her self did sever,
Hearing of him, for whom her heart did hunt, and else should ever:
And though she did observe his soon Revolt from friend to friend,
And him thereof had tacht, wear not her private Cause to end,
Yeat was she silent touching it, and modestly pursewes,
In Quest of her beloved Knight. But tarry yet that News,
First Borough, Ienkinson, and what by them was done pe^use.
CHAP. LXVII.
IT is no common Labor to the River Ob to sail,
Howbeit Borough did therein, not Dangerles, prevail.
He through the foresaid frozen Seas in Lapland did ariue,
And thence, to expedite for Ob, his Labors did revive.
What he amongst the Vaigats, and the barbarous Samoeds notes,
Their Idols, Deer-skin Te^ts, how on their backs they bare their Botes,
In which, but Hides, securely they do fish those Seas all day,
And how on Dear they ride, and all on Sleds by Dear convey,
Do eat their Dead, to feast their friends their Children sometime slay,
Their store of Sables, Furs, and Pealts, fetched thence from far away,
How, at our ^ra^ and Lion Signs, their Frost and Snow is great,
Let be, and many things we might of this new Tract entreat,
By Borough found: whose Praise not much is Chancelors behind,
As Master in that Ship with him that first did Russia find:
And in this Northeast Trade, with Praise, do Pet and Iackman mind.
Yeat longer (for not largelier One yields Matter) let us dwell
Of Ienkinson. But where shall we begin his Lawdes to tell?
In Europe, Asia, Afric? For these all he saw, in all
Imployd for Englands common good. Nor my rejoicing small,
That from Elizabeth to Reign, and I to live begun,
Has happened that Commerce and Fame he to his Natives won.
NOw, under his Conduct, was hence unto his Home conuaide
The Russian first Ambassador, Here honored, whilst he staide:
Nor Captain Ienkinson was there less graced, where he wrought,
That all things to a wished end were for our Traffic brought.
Here-hence also a friendly League twixt either Prince effected:
Nor little is their Amity of us to be respected.
For, though the Moscouites from us be People far remote,
Yeat, if how Danes, and Norses, have invaded us we note,
And how the Russies, in the like Attempts, might hold them back,
For only it, were thence no Trade, ill might their Friendship lack.
From Mosco then by Iournies long the Caspian Sea he crossed,
Himself and Goods by Tartars often in danger to be lost.
Their Hordes of carted Tents like Towns, which Camels drew, their Kings,
By names of Murses, Seltans, Cans, to who^ for Pass he brings
The Russian King his Letters, how (and royally they troe)
With Wild-horse flesh, and Mares milk, him the Kings did banquet tho,
Their hawking for the Wild-horse (For their Hawks will seize upon
The horses neck, who chaffing tiers, and so is killed anon)
Their often Remoues for Pastures fresh (nor Grass their Pasture is,
But heathie Brush, few Cattle though do thrive as theirs with this)
Their naither use of Coin, or Corn (for Tillage none is theare)
Such Warriors, and Horse-Archers, as they live not whom they fear,
Their crosse-leg eating on the ground, Plurality of wives,
In Turkeman (So the whole is said) and more of their rude lives,
And how the Merchants, travailing by Carauan, that is,
Great Droues of laden Camels, Meat and Water often mis,
And how for us did Ienkinson in Bactra Mart begin,
Let pass, to pass to it for us he did in Persia win.
With this Memento, in Return from Bactra, diverse Kings
Sent in his charge their Legates, whom to Mosco safe he brings.
Thence did he sail for England, Hence for Mosco back again,
And, with our Queen Elizabeth her Letters, did obtain
The Mosicks Letters, to the Kings by whom he then should pas,
For Persian Traffic: And for this he thence embarked was.
NOw in Hyrcana, Shyruan, or Media, all as one,
Suppose our ^enkinson before King Obdolowcans Throne.
Though sumptious Cities he possessed, yeat for the Summers heat,
On airesome Mountains held he then his Court, in Pleasures great.
Of silk and gold imbroyderie his Tents, his Robes inchac't,
With Pearls and precious Stones, and Looks of Majesty him graced:
On Carpets rich they trod, rich Trains on him attendance gave,
With six score Concubines, that seemed so many Queens for brave.
Before his fair Pauilion was of Water clear a Fount,
Drink for himself, and his (for most of Water they account.)
Scarce Cleopatras Anthony was feasted with more cheer,
Of varied Meats, and spice-Conceits, than ^enkinson was here.
In formal Hawking, Hunting, Chase, not the^ came Tristram ^eere.
Such was this King for stately, such for affable, and kind,
There and abroad so loved, and feared, as like was rare to find.
Yeat, notwithstanding such his Wealth, his Signorie, and State,
He of the Persian Sophie held his Land, subdued late.
But in such friendship, as the Shaugh (the Sophie so is said)
Would yield to Obdolowcan in what so he should perswaide:
Which well in Ienkinsons behalf, but shortly after, made.
Him often questoned this King of Us, and Europs strength,
And him, with Gifts, and Privilege, for Mart, dismissed at length.
Silks, raw, and wrought, Spices, and Drugs, and more-els, worth the Mart,
Our Merchants fetch from thence, and there our Merchandise co^uart.
Things wisely thus dispatched there, with men for his defense,
And Letters from that King unto the Shaugh, he traueld thence.
In travel thitherwards, he grieves, in wonder, to behold
The down-Fals of those stately Towns, and Castles, which of old,
Whilst Persia held the Monarchy, were famous over all,
Nor Alexander won of those one Piece, with labor small.
The mighty Cities Tauris, and Persipolis, he past,
Two ruined Gates, sundred twelve miles, yet extant of this last:
The Giants Wonders on the Hill of Quiquiffs heard he told,
And of the yearly Obit, which their Maids to Channa hold.
This was indeed a wonder, for this Virgin so was bent
To Chastity, that by selfe-death, she Marrage did prevent.
Here Mandeuil, perhaps, had bin, and took occasion here,
To fear least Flenor in like might imitate too near:
Even Toys in Love discourage, Love from Toys resumeth cheer.
Of him therefore, whilst Ienkinson rests at his Iornies end,
With Obdolowcans Son, that on the Sophie did attend.
CHAP. LXVIII.
SO Knightly Mandeuil demeanes himself against the Foes
Of Melek Mandibron, that he in Egypt famous groes.
And, of himself, that Sultan did to Mandeuil commend
A greater Match, than else his Thoughts with hope could apprehend,
For virtuous, beauteous, Birth, and Wealth, a Match for none to me^d.
This Lady (also of the blood, and heir unto her Father,
A mighty Prince in those same Parts) he courteth now, the rather
To intermit that homebred Love, which seaz'd on him indeed,
As for to prove how such as he with such as she might speed,
And there experimented, here to cease or else proceed.
Fie, Mandeuil, how good so ere thy Merits otherwise,
In making Love vnmeant thou did thy self but mis-aduise.
Though thou could buzze about the flame, and keep vnskorcht thy wings,
Few safely play with edge-Tooles, sin to jest at holy things.
With women, made for Men, therefore soon won, yeat edging Suit,
With Marrage, praisde enough in him did first it institute:
With Women, who when all was made, and Man of all possessed,
Yeat lacks Man an Help, said God, and Man with Woman blessed:
With marrage, that legitimates our Propagation, and
Two Hearts in one transplants, in all befalne, or taine in hand:
With women, that no less attract our Senses them to leeke,
Than Hunger for to labor Food, or Anguish Ease to seek:
With Marrage, that prefers us, and stays us in content,
Vnanimieth weal or woe, as either us is sent:
In Nature Women, Marrage by Tradition, either twain
So sacret, and autentick, as we naither should profaine.
To trifle then or Them, or This, were not so slight a sin,
As that thy Virtues, Mandeuil, would fault, think I, therein.
But for she was a Pagan, and thy self a Christian theare,
And she the Soldans Tender, thou did form a Love for fear.
For that thou should reny thy Faith, and her thereby possess,
The Sultan did capitulat, in vain: the more thy bless:
For than a Woman evermore the Devil tempts less.
Yeat that they tempt, not theirs but ours the sin: for if I see,
And steal, a precious Gemme, the Gemme faults not, the Theft in me.
Howbe^t when to active and to passive love it groes,
And Women then shall alter, them as Devils then suppose:
And like of Men, if Men alike shall Reputation lose.
This fair Egyptian Lady (of the English Toy in this,
To wit, in forward Love to whom the farthest Commer is)
Might easly have bin won of him, not at an easy rate,
If to his Faith a Recreant had Miscrent bin his state.
But he, immovable as well in Faith, as former Love,
Did there so well, as he from thence with honor did remove.
On Elenor he still deuisde: yeat sometimes, to allay
Those Moodes, by mustring in his mind these thoughts, did thus assay.
Full soon the fairest Face, thought he, would cease from being such,
If not preserved, curiously, with tendering more than much.
Or age at least, and that not old, so alters it that was,
That Helen did disclaim her self, for Helen, in her Glass.
That great Physician that had lived, in health, an age admirde,
Did answer, asked the cause, not he had done as Flesh desirde.
Then Mandeuil bethinks him of the Labyrinth of Cares,
Incumbring married Men, and near that life, and love, forswares.
How tedious were a Shroe, a Sloy, a Wanton, or a Fool,
(All four a-like threatening Mislike, when time should Dotage cool,)
How seldom Women come vndow'd with one, or some, or all,
Or answerable Faults to these (to men not Crosses small,)
The Flattries, and the Fooleries, whereby are women won,
With fishing long to catch, perhaps, a Frog, when all is done,
And all that Sexs Infirmities, his Thoughts did overrun.
But like as Mothers beat their Babes, and sing them when they cry,
Loves Incantations so did he with Malice such defy:
The Amorous with the sea-Crabs gaet do angring Amors fly.
This humor, and the honor, by this Knight in Egypt won,
Ore-passe we, and in Persia see what Ienkinson has done.
CHAP. LXIX.
AT Casben hild the Shawgh his Court, who thirty years, and odd,
Had not been seen abroad, thereof by Prophe* sie fo^bodd.
Like Majesty he kept, as those great Monarchs did before
The Macedons subdewed them, of Wives he had like store,
Besides most bewtious Concubines, not less than fifteen score:
And yearly of the fairest Maids, and Wives, does make new choice:
When much the Friends, and Husbands, of those chosen do rejoice.
Him blesses he to whom does he one of his Relicts give:
Yeat Persian Shaughs esteem themselves the holiest Kings that live.
For when a Christian (whom they call an Infidel, because
He not believes in Mahomet, nor Mortezalies Laws)
Is called to audience, least the same prophaine wheare he does stand,
Must doffe his Shoes, and to and fro tread on new-sifted sand.
Our Sovereigns Letters to the Shaugh so Ienkinson presents,
Who, being ask't his arrant, said those Letters like Contents.
But new-made Peace with Turkey him of new-sought Trade preue^ts
The Turkish Merchants, fearing least their Traffic might decrease,
Had, by that Basha, mard his Mart that then had made that Peace.
The Sh^ugh did also question his Belief, and quarrel it:
So, well appaid is ^enkinson, if well away he git,
Whom, with our letters to the Turk the Shaugh, to send was bent,
Had not the Hyrcane Murzey Posts unto his Father sent,
And Obdolowcans Letters then dissuaded that intent:
When, with a Present for himself, he Thence to Hyrcan went,
And theare did him the heart-trew King most kindly intertayne,
And thence dismiss with Gifts, when he no longer would remain:
Nor only his Ambassadors unto his care Commends,
But moment of that Ambassie which he to Mosco sends.
There now suppose them well ariu'd, and bringing grateful news
Of weighty Messages, whearin the Mosick him did use.
Convenient time he nerethelesse, for Persian Trade attends:
Which Arthur Edwards, thither sent, succesfully theare ends.
This Edwards, and a many here unnamed, deserved well
In these Employments: but of All wear tedious al to tell,
For, saving of Discouerers we purpose not to dwell.
Else would we here revive, but that through Hakluits Pen they live,
(To him, your Fames sweet Trumpetor, Ye English, Garlands give)
A Catalogue of Names, that in this North, and Northeast Clymes,
Have more observed, and more deserved, than perish shall with times.
Nor be my Father here forgot: for he, amongst the rest,
Deserves in this General remembrance with the best.
And here, from out those churlish Seas, with Ienkinson we sail
To London, theare an aged Man, to tell this youthful Tail:
How he had past All Europe, seen all Leuant Islands, and
Greece, Turkey, Afric, India, Sur, Egypt, the holy Land,
And all the foresaid Lands in all imployde, and intertainde,
Of Emperors, and Kings, as if him self a King had reigned.
Rest may thy honorable Boanes, good old* Man in sweet Peace:
Nor have thy Phoenix-Ashes since been barren of increase.
But late had we a Foul like rare, used oftener Sea than Shore,
Often swam he into golden Strands, but now will so no more,
For, though he wear a dyuing Foul, to Heaven did he sore.
In England, not Arabia, now the Phoenix Bird is bread,
And evermore shall theare revive, when shall the old be dead:
The Maiden Empress, and her Knights their Enterprises rare,
Which now have pearst through every Pole, of all admired are.
Remains now, that we entreat of great Achieuements done
By English, in contrary Clymes, since first her Crown begone.
But ride we first at Anchor, though a roomesome Sea we have,
To listen Staffords Comforts which to Elenor he gave.
THE TWELFTH BOOK OF ALBIONS ENGLAND.
CHAP. LXX.
WHen Stafford sounded had, as said, the Ladies Loue-seaz'd heart,
He to a Banquet her inuites, and did for then depart.
Now to his Lodging (which had erst been Mandeuils also)
Comes Elenor, accompned with a crew of Ladies more,
Of which was one that Stafford loved, of her beloved: She
Instructed was by him of all should there effected be.
The Banquet ended, Dorcas (so was Staffords Ladies name)
Thus speaks unto Elenor, as if it wear in game.
In yonder Chamber (poynting to a locked Door thereby)
Has Mandeuil his Closet and no common Armory.
Full many things theare-in, quoth she, both rare and rich saw I:
Howbeit Stafford, even to me, did long that sight deny.
Wear he and all the rest abroad, wheare lies the Keye I wot:
Then said you sick, and sleepie, so the rest may hence be got.
Soon Ladies listen Novelties, and serve themselves with Shifts,
Not sooner Dorcas had devised, but Elenor it drifts.
So finely fayn'd she ill-at-ease that none believed her well,
Save Stafford and his Lady, so preposing as befell:
And all save only Dorcas, whom she prays with her to stay,
Did sadly leave her to her Rest, and that she rest did pray.
Mean while the Ladies and their Oste into the Garden walk,
Where Stafford did invent them Sports, and hild them pleasant talk.
The Ladies twain, thus left alone, the door they open, wheare
To them in complete Armor seemed the greene-Knight to appear.
The Burgonet, the Beuer, Buffe, the Collar, Curates, and
The Poldrons, Grandgard, Vambraces, Gauntlets for either hand,
The Taishes, Cushies, and the Graves, Staff, Pensell, Baises, all
The greene-Knight erst had tylted with that hild her Love his Thra^,
She saw, on Crosses and els-what, by Staffora so set out,
That to have seen her very Knight made Elenor no doubt.
At first she fears, but lastly finds the Armor was vn-man'd:
When skaerd, and cheerd, with Dorcas she did enter, theare at hand,
The Closet wheare the Prizes of the for said Iusts did stand.
These, and that Armor, Dorcas said, are Mandeuils, the same
Your Knight, that won himself, for Force, to you for Fair such fame*
But, Sweet, let this be spoke in Shrift, so was it spoke to me:
Long have you thirsted it, know I, which now you hear and see.
Though Stafford was by Mandeuil coniured to conceal it,
And I by Stafford, what is it but that I may reveal it?
What shame, a Gods name, can redowne to him, by being known
The Knight that honored both himself and you, as more might none.
Believe me, reason none his fame should thus obscured lie:
But whatsoeare the Scruple be, now out it is, for why?
To you lies bare my heart, and shall. With that (by pre-conclusion
Twixt him and Dorcas) Stafford made a mannerly Intrusion.
Why how now Ladies, sick, and heal, and taine so soon in ^a^me?
You should, quoth he, to Pownd, wear this a churlish Ruslicks fa^me.
In vain, I see, has Mandeuil forbidden me or sight,
Or speech hereof to Elenor: And Dorcas serves me right:
Bab'd be our Shrifts to women, of simplicity, or spite.
Well, Lady, (for by now I guess you know this Armors Knight,
And you, concerning other things, have read what he did wright)
If firm you hold for him (as you have said, and should do still)
For you, and him, and both, therein imploye me at your will.
Who mightier than the Sultan? Yeat, as Mandeuil me wrights,
A Lady of the blood, right fair, he him for Wife be hights:
But, wear she Empress of the World, his Resolution is,
She either shall be Elenor, or never any his.
Full well she knew those Sights, and at those Speeches did rejoice:
The greene-Knight had been Mandeuil, had been in her the Choice.
That Wish, the Man, his Worth, their Love, concurriug in this wise,
Had he been also present, what more Bliss could she devise?
Thence went they out into that Room where had the Banquet bin,
And theare was she about to speak, when came the Ladies in:
Full well appayde to see her cheered, and frained then her quaem,
(For nothing less than forged Panges, or what had past they aem.)
Then went they all to whence they came, and Elenor did plie
For Mandeuil: What was it now for him she would not try?
Often Stafford, She, and Dorcas did consult, and then conclude
This Course, not to be changed: which ear said, be first pursude
Of English, that in Afric, and in Asia, bring to pas,
That England now is famous, wheare but late unnamed it was.
CHAP. LXXI.
OF Northern Regions partly is epitomed before,
Of other later Voyages ensues a few, of store.
Great Personages cannot want great Poets theirs to tell.
Nor may they want, nor want o them would I, wrote I like-well.
But of some Meaners, that their lives have ventured no less,
Performed as much, Some more, and Some that have, as many guess,
Unto their Betters made the way to glory they possess,
Shall we digest: Or it we speak of great-Ones, they be dead,
For Writers aduantagiously are of the Living read.
Thy Reign also, Elizabeth, shall bound our Pen in it,
Which to our Theme infers Texts, no times yield more so sit.
Like amotous Escape from England as of Elenor to Rome,
Made Macham in Madera rear his hence-stolne Lovers Toome,
Then reigned here Third Edwara, when so trauel'd Mandeuil,
And in those days th^interring There of Machams Love befell.
A Chapel built be there, his name and hers engraven in Stone,
To Jesus dedicated (then, and England, there unknown.)
Of him, this Islands Port is called Machico, to this day,
Whom Afric Mores to C^stile, as a Wonder, did convey:
For in an hollowed Tree, or Trough, not having sail or Oats,
(The Ship they came in leaving him) discovered he the Mores.
By which discovery, and by his Instructions, did ensew,
The ^berians did Madera and Canaries Isles subdew.
Hence (else had Macham past our Pen) did time effect our Trade
For Guinie, in her Highness Reign acquired, and patent made.
For not in Mary's wear those Parts, as now, so fully found,
Yeat still to grateful Ears may those Negotiators sound,
To wit (although an Alien) good Pinteado, abused
By moodie VVindam, Guinte first, and Benyn, these perused:
Next Gainsh, then Towrson diverse times, and theare my Father did:
Since, rife that Voyage, Brasile, and to Cape-verd Isles beside.
Gold, ^iuet, musk, grains, pepper, woad, and iuory thence be brought:
In Barbary, old Mauritaine, like Trade this reign has wrought:
Of Afric, and America, by Ours no part unsought.
In Tewri^, Sirta, Egypt, Greece, the Turks whole Empier now,
Our Queen is gracious, our Commerce, and Agents, they allow.
Of World-admired Drake (for of his Worth what argues more,
Tha^ fame enuide? Some, for was his so rich, thought theirs too poor,)
And his brave Breeder Hawkins (yeat be honored every Pen,
That, howsoever, honor them as high resolved Men)
In Fiction, or in Mystery, to read would less delight,
Than would significantly some their glorious Ior(...)es wright:
The pains of such invited Pens such subject would requite.
Add Gilbert, Greenuill, Frobisher, of Knights to make up five,
All in their better Parts with God, with Men their Fames alive.
Add ^hilton, Oxnam, Fenton, Ward, Dauis, an other Drake,
With diverse here not catalog'd, and for a Cheifest take
All actions Candish, and of these eternal Pen-worke make.
And, for a gowned Cicero, and one that did not live
But to his Prince, and Country, Lawdes to VVaisingham do give.
The (...)ds, and Ae^eados, for Text, and Truth, might yield
Unto that learned Muse that should manure that plentious Field.
Was never Prince employed Peace, with praise, to profit more,
Or R^alme could, in the Reign of one, boast worthy Men like store:
Out Foes can also witness her armipotent, in few,
Religion, Virtue, Wealth, Peace, War, her Throne with same indue.
And here mine altered Muse this Theme surceaseth to pursue.
Of These, East- Indian Goa, South, and South-east People more,
And of their memorable Names those Toils did undergo,
Is one elaborated Pen compendicusly does floe.
Omitted then, and named Men, and Lands (not here, indeed,
So written of as they deserve) at large in Hakluit reed.
To him, and (who deserves like-well of England, both as Any
Have wrote of England) Camden, and to English Poets Many:
Of which are some (praiseworthy though) that tower their Wits too hie,
To make a Pitch for Love, when they at fairer Fowls might fly,
Now veil I Bonnet. Rest thee, Muse, abroad what need we rome?
Our seauenteene Kingdoms once, now One, yield Work enough at Home,
All which her Highness owneth now, as shall anon be said:
But of our English Lovers first be this Addition made.
CHAP. LXXII.
NOw at Constantinople (once Byzantium) in old Thrace,
Had Mandeuil to Stafford wrote should be his Wintring Place:
Next Summer would he be at Rome: That Stafford should direct
For either Place his Letters, which he, longing, did expect,
So S^efford had to Elenor the same informed, who,
And Dorcas, evermore their Loves did argue fro and too.
They may, quoth Dorcas, make more nice, but few or none, I troe,
That labor not of our disease: and why, I pray you, noe?
To be with God, what good more good? For it we all should ask.
But for by death it must be done, but few affect the Task.
Virginity, though praised, is alike performed, for why?
As much the Flesh is frail therein, as in the fear to dye.
What, was it said to all, but us, Increase and Multiply?
No Clarke will so expound that Text, God shield they should, say I.
All yield, that Marrage is no sin, if chastely then we live,
And Man and wife their Bodies each to other wholly give:
If so, as so is granted, what needs curious struggling then?
Since God and Nature formed Men for us, and us for Men.
Ill match those dallying Girls, pray I, that intertaine by Art
All Lovers, giving Hopes to all, of all to make their Mart,
And, having blaunched many so, in single Life take pride:
When not a Strumpet Men so much abhor, and more deride.
As Well as too remiss in choice, we may be too precise,
And lose, as Aesops swimming Dog, a Substance for Surmise.
But if we mark, in matching, this (which perfecteth Content)
That in the Man of Virtues be and Love a Couplement,
For either Fortune work we that we never shall repent.
We coyly may consume our Youth, till times may alter so
Or form, friends, wealth, or fame, that we out of Request may groe.
But lose that list their Prime, since now I have that may delight,
He shall participate my best, that must my baddet Plight.
For true it is, as Vessels of first Liquors ever taste,
Love, seasned so with Sweets of Youth, the same does ever last.
Nay should my Stafford (God forbid) less kind than think I prove,
I neretheles would still be his, in chaste and cheerful Love.
No men, troe I (the rascal Sort except) but women may,
In Patience, temporizing well, inform, and, erring, stay.
And reason (were there Scripture none, so bidding) we forbear
In men their Moodes, because of us they wholly take the care.
Admit we bring them Portions great, and beauty sought of Many,
Alas, what bring we one, that might not else have hap't to any?
For (let me speak it to no Blab) it is a Question, whether
That longer think it we, or Men, until we come together.
Well Wanton, well, quoth Elenor, if Men should hear your chat,
This last I mean, at least they would conclude for us in that:
Concerning which, upon my Tongue shall mine Opinion dye,
Though should I say to it, and all, Amen, I soothed no Lie.
Saint Stafford thine, Saint Mandeuil for me, God for us all,
I have bin, a(...), and will be still resolved, what ere befall.
In saying which came Stafford in, and wills them to dispatch
To Ship-boord: (for before had they determined of that Match.)
Already had he shipped their Stuff, lack't only they a board:
Which Opportunity did now that time and place afford.
How they escaped, or how disguisde, what skils it? escaped they are,
All three imbark't for Italy, and had the wind so fair,
That almost thither had they reached, before they missed wear.
For them great search, and sorrow much was made, but all in vain,
None knew or when they went, or whence, or where they did remain.
Here see you, what can mighty Love in either Sex effect:
Here see you also friends for friends nor weal nor woe respect:
Here see you one that feared to speak, is followed far to speed:
Here see you that a Woman dares, if she conceits the Deed:
Here see you one in love, not moop't at home, but mapping Lands:
Here see you how against all things else, for Ladies Virtue stands:
Here also hear what they, ariu'd in Italy, did see:
And first at Rome, when first shall this of Else-what spoken be.
CHAP. LXXIII.
Elizabeth's now-Monarchie ore seauenteene Crowns of old,
As formerly was promised, shall briefly here be told.
Before the Scots did plant them Here, owned ancient Brutaines All,
And still, take I, her Homager may England Scotland call:
Which ouer-passe, not now possessed, in this Account we shall.
Of either Land the Marches, and much more, for most now Ours,
The warlike Picts possessing Here, built Castles, Towns, and Towers,
(...)tes, Scots, and Romans (then our Lords) often daunting with their Powers:
T^^^^stly civil Strife, and Scots, disskingdom'd the^ from Hence,
Whom Orkney Islands, as is said, have harbored ever sense.
The Br^^aines, by these Picts of long oppressed with thraldom sore,
To be deliu'red of such Foes, did Saxon Aides implore.
So playing as did Aesops Horse, that angry with the Heart,
To be revenged, did crave in aid of Man to take his part:
Till when the Horse was never backed nor bitted, Either now
He having suffered, would have helped, but then he knew not how.
The Brataines, having called so the Saxons to their aid,
Could not be rid of them, to whom they had themselves betraide:
But by the same were they at length debelled into VVailes:
Each of whose Kings, of long time Three, in Englands Monarch fails.
The Saxons getting Brutaine thus (which they did England name)
At once of them in several Parts Seven Kings did rule the same:
All which, by Western Adelstane, in Monarchy was got,
Which since (save Iron-sids and the Danes once Deuide^t) changed not.
Five Irish Kingdo^s likewise add, now drowned in Englands Crown.
The Seventeenth was the Isle of Man, in Ours now also down.
Our Mandeuil, here cited, of Earl Mandeuil exact
In Stephens Reign so famous, Man, in Scots Possession sacked,
And with the Spoil of all that Isle thence, and his Brother, pack't.
Consent of times, Names, and Records, affirm, may seem, no less.
But Monteacute, Count Salsburie, it won, and did possess.
Of Percies after, Stanlies next, and still (guess I) holds Man,
From honorable long Descents: and from they first began,
All loyal, hospitalious, loved, still powre-full, and I pray,
That in that Noble house those Terms may neuer-more decay.
This Man was diverse hundred years a Kingdom, and not small,
Ruled Hebrides, the Orcades, to Thul the Islands all,
And chances there, and Changes, worth the note, did often befall.
As how the natural Incolants the Iselanders subdew,
Them Norses, ^rish them, them Scots, and English them, in few.
Beauchamp, the Earl of Warwick, (first, and last, and but a while)
Was King of VVight, Sixth Henry's Gift that Honor, and that I Will:
Which added, Eighteen Kingdoms, al possessed, ieke Englands Style.
These now, thus couched all in One, save Parcel Scots withhold
Of Penthland, and some barren Isles subdued to Man of old,
Since has one Monarch ruled, us has rendered secure:
Whereas Plurality of Kings did ever Loss procure.
Twice joined our Ilanders in one, when twice did Caesar fail:
Disioyning, He, Picts, Saxons, Danes, and Normaines did prevail.
The Spanyard, in Conceit, deuoures our Country, in no hope
But of Disiunctiues, who, thinks he, less love their Prince than Pope.
Let France admonish England, turned Religion turns not Spain
From thirsting France: Neutralitie brought late-lost Calice Baine:
And Spanish't-French live Peasants-like, that, French, did Princes reign.
Our Elders* illy did they well, for so should not be done,
Much less to causeles Arms against their Prince ought Subjects ron)
So badly brook't this Spaniards famed Espousall with their Queen,
That, even at VVestminsters Law-Barres, were harnest Pleaders seen,
Feared with the trooped Bands yt would that Banes with swords forbid:
Of which were pitied those that did, the Rest winkt-at, and hid.
Their meaning had it Praise, had not the Manner bin in fault:
The Manner now does hold, may none in selfsame Meaning hault:
But armed be every hand, and heart, hence Englishly to beat
Spayn, that our Bodies would inthral, Rome, that our Souls does threat.
Yeat (which has blinded, bridled, and beguiled, them many a day)
Their Inquisition wish I from the Spaniards rid away.
What good remains to wish the Pope, this, that had none bin borne,
From him to steal his ill-got Coin, from us to ship our Corn.
The best things brought from Rome, to us convert, troe I, to ill:
But new Rome left, of old Rome now abreuiat we will.
CHAP. LXXIV.
WHen Stafford and his double Charge to Italy were come,
In health, and rich (for hence brought they in Coin no little Some,
With Jewels of rare estimate, and else-what of great worth)
For Mandeuil they seek, and him at last did listen forth.
That in Constantinople yet he was, a Merchant said:
And thither Staffords Letter was to him with speed conuaide,
Purporting only that himself at Rome his coming staide.
Mean while in Rome (the Mistress once of all the world) they view
Such wonders, couch't in Ruins, as unseen might seem untrue.
Once was it compast, as is read, with fifty miles of wall:
Now some to twenty, some to less, in that account do fall:
It having Towers so many as the year has days in all.
From forty miles was water brought in Pipes o^ Arches thither.
Were vaulted walks through every Street, against Sun, and rainy weather.
The sumptious Bathes, with Palaces thereto of rare delight,
The roomesome Ponds, where very Ships some Festiuals did fight,
The Trophie Arches, where to life Triumphants were purtraide,
The Statures huge, of Porphyrie and costlier matters made,
The Theaters, Pyramides, the Hill of half a mile,
Raisde but of tribute Pot-sheards, so to boast their Power long while,
The Obelisks, of one whole Stone near forty yards or more,
Huge Pillars, carved in Masonrie with Prowse of Knights before,
The stately Bridges, sometimes Eight, now fewer, Tyber cross,
The Thirty goodly Gates, of which is now of number loss,
The huge Colosses, Conduites, and else-what, that showed a State
Beyond belief of ruined Rome, in part repaired of late,
They wonder at, and how the world could yield such Pomp, debate.
Though some the seven enclosed Hills, did ancient Rome contain,
Lie waste, or Vine-yards, more does yeat of Majesty remain,
Even in the Rubble of the old, than in the now renewed,
Though Rome retains a Statelines, nor fairer Pyles are viewed.
The round Pantheon, once the House of all the Heathen Gods,
Stands yet a Temple, but less decked for rich by too much ods.
On Auentine the down-fals are of Temples store to see:
On Tarpie of the Capitol, were wont their Guild to be:
On Palantine of Palaces, on Caelius signs of Plays:
Quirinall, Exquell, Viminall, of Bathes show brave decays.
These Hills, with Vatican and old Ianiculum ore-past,
Show we how Rome did rule, was ruled, and ruined at the last.
FRom Ianus (called Noe of some, ^aphet of some Noes Son,
And Iaphets son of others) Rule in Tuscan first begone,
Reigned in six hundredth twenty years of Latins Twenty one:
(Son of the Eight of which was Brute, first Soueraign of our Throne)
The last of these Numitor was, whom Romulus did kill,
And, building Rome, slew Remus, there to lord-it at his will.
* This City, then Receptacle for all, how vile skils not,
Of Italy by one and one the Sixteen State-Lands got.
Tarquinius reigned the Seventh in Rome, when for the Outrage done
Upon Lucretia, Kings did cease, and Consuls then begone:
When had two hundredth thirty years bin Kings, that much had won.
* In this estate of Consuls (Two, remoueable each year)
Rome flourished in Victories five hundredth years well near.
No age can boast like valiant Men, or Senators so grave,
That War, and Peace, unto the world at their Devotion gave:
Whose only naked Majesty not Armor then could brave:
Like Monarchy none ever had, or likely is shall have.
* When now the world was wholly Romes, and Pompei overcome,
Then Julius Caesar did Usurp the Commonwealth of Rome.
Had Thirty eight bin Emperors successively, when as
Did Constantine to Siluester Rome, by Donation pas.
* This Constantine (surnamed the great, Our heir, and of our blood)
Baptized of Siluester, did hurt, as hap't, by doing good:
When to Constantinople he removed the imperial Seat,
Rome proved too proud for Priests, or pride of Priests for Rome too great.
Besides, was little Policy in Partnership of Reign,
For Rome, and Greece, one Empier erst, was ruled as if of Twain:
Who also chose their Caesar's, that their private Turns did wait:
Whence, after at Bizantium Nine had held imperial State,
Rome by the Gothe Alaracus was sacked in barbrous rate.
And here the Roman Monarchy did palpably decline:
As does her Prelacie, and soon shall quite, may we divine.
But shall we yet digress of Rome, subiecting us so long?
Then least, as now your Ears, our Pen your Memories should wrong,
Remembered be wheare stayde our search of Countries, where left we
Of Manaeuil and Elenir, and wheare these Lovers be.
CHAP. LXXV.
ANon as Constantine left Rome, for Greece, to Sylvester,
(So called of living in the Woods, whence erst he durst not ster)
For consecrating after. Popes they golden Rites prefer,
And, hansling Rome with Heresies, in factious Schisms did err.
Scarce was it come unto the Third, when those that lived before,
(But few escaping Martyrdom) in Deserts, meek and poor,
Did wrangle for Saint Peter's keys, and Primacie of all
The christian Church: which to their Sea, though long, at length did fall.
Hence Dispensations, Jubilees. Pardons, and such racked geere,
Wear had at Rome: nothing, naught worth, theare had, not paid for dear.
Yeat not, till long, the Emperors they dared to provoke,
That now in choice of Emperors did labor greatest stroke,
And S^culers from church-Affayres in all did sequester,
But wrought that Seculers should all unto the Church refer:
Effecting so, that Emperors and Kings did kiss their feet,
Deposing, and disposing, them, and theirs, as they thought meet.
When now the blind seduced World was brought unto their bent,
And more their busy heads could not, ambitiously, invent,
Was Pope, and Pope, and sometimes Three: Popes poysond, Popes exild,
Popes strangeld, cozened Popes, a Pope deliuerd of a Child:
Was never heard such Tyrants, or of other Potentates
So many did miscarrie, through Ambition, and Debates.
But be observed, in highest pitch of Pride in Cleargie-men,
God still has wrought, Religion, Realms, and all have ruined then.
So, in her Roof, it fared with Rome: he whisel'd, and did swarm
From out the North Barbarians that against Italy did arm.
At sundry times the Goth, the Dane, the Vandale, and the Hunne,
With others, four times sacked Rome, and often the Land ore-runne:
Nor only so, but there did reign, in spite of who said nay:
Whence Cisalpine is Lumbardie unto this present day:
And Rome, which since our Brennus, none durst enterprise, was made
A Booty to each barbrous Force that would the same invade.
In vain the Roman Emperors their ancient Right defend,
For through seditious Popes the French begun where Those did end.
From Pharamonds to Pepins House they foyst the Crown of France,
And to the Western Empier then did Charlemaine advance.
From France to Italy again, to Germany from thence,
Whereas the naked Title has inured ever sense.
Thus erring Rome has, does, and will, our christian World vnqueate:
May therefore Princes join to race that Monster from his Seat.
What, will ye see a glorious God of earth? go see the Pope:
Aspiring Lucifer? who else? Truth fals't? reuersethe Cope:
Queanes like to Queens? There halfe-mile Streets afford no other Sort,
If Skarlet Hats mean while and Stoles have not ingro'st the Sport.
Full forty Thousand Cur^izans there, Ladies-like, do live,
That to the Pope for wantoning no small Reuenew give.
Their wives they mew from churchme^ there: whence does this Byword come?
More Privileged Harlots live tha^ honest wines at Rome.
Well, by their Fruits ye may them know. Thus is cast up this Some.
WHilst were those Broils in Italy, did many there reject
Obedience to the Emperors, who could not them protect:
So diverse Cities did themselves infranchize, growing States:
And each of them Dominion to her Commonwealth delates.
Wear ouerlong although we should but briefly ouer-ronne
The free- Italian States, of which the Spaniards part have won:
As Naples, Myllaine, royal That, and Duchy This, both twain
So peopled, fertile, pleasant, rich, as neither show their waine.
But should we speak of Venic's Pomp, the City, and the Site,
Too little should we speak, although too much we here should write.
But, Gallants, will you view the Court of Venus, and not so,
But Myrrors too of Courtesy? to rich-built Genoa go:
But far from drifting Florence keep, least Machiuels ye groe.
Ferrara, Vrbine, Mantua, Placence, and Parma are
Brave Cities, great for State, and please those which to them repare.
I wot not what this spacious Land does lack that Man can wish:
Air te^prate, fertile Grounds, vnmatch't for fruits, beasts, fowls, and fish:
Men valiant, rich, kind, courtly, and fair Academies many,
For brave and bewtious women do, nor need they yield to any.
Now to our English Trinity of Lovers let us return,
That still, in expectation of the Fourth, at Rome soiourne.
Theare, after Prayers, Church-times, Sights, and Stories sometimes read,
Amongst their merry Tales was this, how one, enamored, spead.
CHAP. LXXVI.
AFaire young wife of Lyncolne-Shire (if say our Author truth)
In traueling to London-wards, squir'd of a clownish youth,
Was by a Yorke-Shire Gentleman ore-taken, and together
This Cock of game, and Hen (as he supposed) of that Feather,
Do journey on: And, liking her, when words of course had past,
And nodding pricked on before her Serving Creature fast,
He thus began. Fair Mistress, since our Travel lies one way,
If so you please, one Inn, one Board, (and I for all will pay)
May serve us both: I also was about, one Bead, to say.
The Gentlewoman (formally then modest) blushing, said,
For Inn, add Board, as pleases you: And so her Answer staide,
But would you add the Third, quoth he, more would it please in deed.
What that you pay for all? quoth she, nay, so wear more than need.
Tush, that (And yeat best Orators to Women knew he Gifts,
And therefore named, Pay, as if by chance, to edge his drifts)
Was out, quoth he, at un awares: I also named, Bead.
You said you were about, quoth she, which still let go for dead.
Thus off and on they dialogue best part of all that day:
He could not win her to consent, nor would he take a nay:
For long a go the Calendar of Women-Saints was filde,
Few not to Opportunity, importunated, yield,
Thinks this our Northern wily Ladde, hearty, and hardy too,
Who never would giue-out: nor more, than thus, yields she to do,
That is, to bead: he swearing but to kiss, and her embrace.
Then merrily for Huntington they mend their former pace.
Alighted theare, for Supper he bespeaks the dantiest Cheer,
And either in one Gallerie had Chambers, somewhat near.
Betwixt their Chambers placed was a Southern Gentleman,
That by officious Signs twix't them to sound their Match began.
Her extraordinary Form, on work the rather sets
His heart, and Senses (such an hand of us such Beauty gets.)
Resolved at last of what was meant, and how therein to deal,
This Smel-feast from the bidden Guest, did thus the Banquer steal.
He gave it out, that all might hear, he early would away,
His Man fayn'd fear to ouer-sleepe, and would not down him lay:
But, when that all besides betook themselves to sleep and rest,
One while he walks the Gallerie, another while he dressed
His rustie Sword, which badly did the Northern-man digest.
In vain he cha^te, in vain he wished the Seruing-man were gone:
Nor durst he out to board his Love (for much it stood upon
Their Credits to be cautilous.) The Southern-man, this while,
Got to the Gentle-womans bed, and did (no force) beguile
Her Expectation: Swore you not? quoth she, and he did smile.
But, had he bin the Man forsworn, if God forgave the Sin,
She pardoned him the quo advi that he had trespast in,
And, for that Nights work, swore to swear no Man from like, I win.
This Chaer thus chaer'd, as closely as he went returns he back
Unto his proper Bed, nor long he sleeps ere thence he pack.
No sooner cleared was the Coast, but that the bidden Guest
Steals to her Chamber door, then locked, (for now she means to rest.)
A Malcontent retireth he, not dreaming what had bin,
But better Opportunity hopes at their next-nights Inn.
Next Morn they meet, when, blushingly (but angry not a whit)
Ha Sir, quoth she, I Will trust again your Oath, so kept you it.
Well, bite, and whine (quoth he) who trusts a Woman so is saru'd.
First museth she, then jests it out, soon finding how was swaru'd.
But thus the Northern-man did fail, that did no cost omit,
And thus the Southern-man prevailed, at charge no more then Wit.
The best is yet behind, but ere be told the Story out,
Amongst our Lovers, now at Rome, hear how was brought about.
AT Rome is Mandeuil ariu'd, Stafford and he are met,
To say their Greeting, for the much, were here too long a Let.
Of Elenor her health, and more, suppose not Questions few:
For yet full little Mandeuil of her Ariuall knew,
Nor shall, till of his Loyalty, and life, be further View.
At Staffords Lodging had he seen (as is The Italian Guies)
Two portly Ladies, Head and Face, all veiled, save their Eyes:
Twixt one of these, and Stafford, much of Kindness to have past
Had he observed, and thereupon thus breaks with him at last.
I have not seen the covered Dish, that so your Diet fits,
But much I fear it surfeit may, quoth he, your queasie wits:
Needs must I enter now the Lists, to combat Dorcas Foe,
Even Staffords reason that from Heaven to Hell is posting so.
I will not ask, nor do I care, what beauty, wealth, or wit,
Your here-found Mistres has, why you should home-left Love forgit:
But this, I know, not Rome affords whom more you might affect
Than her, whom wronged here I see, and more than seen suspect:
And wherein differs Man from Beast, but in Affections checked?
What, is she married. Then do ye superlatiuely sin:
Or Maid? I like not Maidens that so forwardly begin:
Or Curtizen? What does she with a Veil that is so vile,
As not to blush at shame, but, baer'd, is wonted to beguile?
But Married, Maid, or Curtizen, or what you please her name,
I like not him makes love to one, and wrongs the very same.
I tell thee, Stafford, be she good, or bad, thou here do court,
Thee I pronounce too bad, yt with fore-plighted Love do'est spourte.
Let it suffice my Friendship hates Absurdities in thee:
Far be it Trauelers should play the Spyder, not the Be.
I would thy courted Lady here, and her Consorte, heard this:
(A needles wish, next Room wear both, and over heard he is)
Then should they hear thee false to one, a Choyser is not here,
And, fearing like, suspect thy Love, of Precontracts not clear:
Or falsed Matches, finished in wrong of Others, might,
By still improsprous Presidents, detetre from wronging Right.
To honest Ears might this suffice to interrupt herein,
Or spoke I unto Harlots, this at least from Lust should win:
Al Touch-sweet, Tast-sweet, Eye-sweet, Eat-sweet, Sent-sweet, Soulesweet, is
A virtuous Match, but vicious Love in all contraries this.
Suppose this firm and naked Love, and Friendship, much to please
His Auditorie, seen, and not, and S^afford, to appease
His Discontent, pretends a soon Return for England thence,
And so this Parlie ended, and on either part Offense.
Stafford had said to Mandeuil, that Elenor, to try
Whether his Love did not with time, new Loves, and Travel die,
Required back the Ring she gave, which if he could her send,
She would believe him loyal, and requite it in the end.
That Ring, in this Regard, did he commend to Stafford now,
And, save to her, he give the same to none exacts a Vow.
The next third following day was fayn'd the time he would from thence,
Whom to accompany on his way had Mandeuil pretense:
Wherefore they feast their Friends, and the^ their Friends, amo^g'st the rest,
Unknown of him, was Elenor with Mandekil a Guest.
Such Art she used, and such Attier she wore, and who would look
For her at Rome? that present her not for herself he took.
Upon her Finger he espide his Ring, delivered erst,
Yeat silently deuoures the Grief, that to his Soul had pear'st.
He, and the rest, invited wear to sup abroad that Night:
Night, Guests, and Suppers end are come, when (grieved though in Spright
He, to recover back his Ring, did use this clenly slight:
In one had he a Mommerie devised and a Mask,
And every masking Mommer took a Lady to his task,
He her, with whom he had espyde his Ring, and Dancing donn^,
To look, as if for somewhat lost, to ground-wards he begone.
Was ask't what myste, he whispers her, that he had lost a Ring,
Which wanting in each Mommers Mouth, was made a penal thing:
Fair Lady, lend me this, quoth he, that on your finger is,
And (giving her a Tablet rich) for Gage accept of this.
Her Courtesy, his colored Want, and Gage, effected so,
That she, the Pawn accepted, did her loned Ring forgo.
Now on the Board wear cast the Dice, her turn was come to play,
Which Opportunity takes he, and shifts him thence away.
Nor knew she him, nor her knew he, for her she was indeed:
But thus he spead his purpose, and of this did thus proceed:
Which, ear we shall report, insewes our Jests Remain to reed.
CHAP. LXXVII.
AT VVare (in Hertford-shire a Town, not bettered, I win,
Of thorough-Faires, from Thence to Twede, for many an harbrous Inn,
Washed with the once ship-bearing Ley, by Alfred slu'ste in Three,
To dissipate the Dane-Fleete, that expugning Hertford be,)
Next day they timely took their Inn, had sup't ere Sun was ser,
Abroad walked she, unseen did he into her Chamber get.
First giving out he would to bed, least, mist, be marde the Sport,
And, least she fear, the day before had made to her report,
In Merriment, that oftentimes he walked in his Sleep,
And then nor Lock, nor Let, could him from Place, or Person, keep:
If in this fit I chance on you, be not afraid, quoth he.
But, if you come, I Will whip you thence, then best not come, quoth she.
Thus, and with this Preparatiue, he counts the Booty his,
And on the Rushes, underneath her Bed, he couched is,
And what with watch the night before, and wearines that day,
And to be fresh, anon, he slept, as soon as down he lay.
Mean while, a lusty Yeoman of a Northern Bishops, who
Was often there a Guest, and good, alights and goes into
The Gentlewomans poynted Room, and say what they could say,
There would he lodge, for that had bin his Lodging many a day,
Loath were they to displease him (for an Harbinger he was)
And then were Bishops bo^^tious, as they too and fro did pass.
For whenever they removed to Place, or Parliament,
Their common Meany (not a few) on Foot with Bows fore-went,
Nor niggardly was then to them allowed to be spent.
Themselves, with learned Chaplains and great Trains did follow then,
And frankly spent in every Place, relieuing many Men.
Yea, Monasteries, Colleges, Schools, Hospitals, Hie-waies,
Bridges, and like, were founded by the Prelates in those days.
But Laters could not so, for why? were Those so fleeced Theirs,
As but a thousand yearly Fee, some purcha'st to their Heirs.
But now be some (may such be long, and such to them succeed)
To whom in all does nothing want of reverent Worth indeed.
But say ye Sooth, have Cleargie-men Coshairers? tush a Lie:
To ask do Courtiers, Churchmen shame to offer Simonie:
And that should be such Fault, appears small likelihood, say I.
Obscurely more, with lesser Port, less hospitalious too,
The great-Ones never lived, nor racked their own, as now they do.
Then either this is false that thus they fleece, or this is true,
That even Consumption of their own, is Sacrilegers due.
But wander do we from our Jest, of that does thus ensue.
The Bishops man sups, and to bed, whilst soundly sleeps that other:
The Gentlewoman, barred that Room, is lodged in another:
That in the Bed fell fast a sleep, This underneath awakes,
And, in his creeping out, and up, no dinne at all he makes.
One heard he breathe, ar't there? thought he, have with thee by and by:
And softly kissed, wheare felt so rough, he feared to have kissed awry.
He grop't therefore her Face, and caught the Yeoman by the Beard:
And rudely starting up (not more in all his life afeard,
The^ buskling to his Sword) cride Thieves: That other in such taking,
As though he were a Man right good, he stood amazed, and quaking.
The Oste and Ostlers with a Light and Tools, then next to hand,
Came in, where he, almost vnstript, but wholly skar'de, did stand.
They wonder (for they knew him well) that he should be a Thief:
Good Sirs, quoth he, be still, we all deceived are, in brief.
Then, taking some apart, he told his Cause of being theare,
And prays their silence, and in Wine was washed away that Fear.
The Gentlewoman, hearing this, vn-Inn'd by day did peep,
And (honester than would himself) left him to wake, or sleep.
This stale-Iest told, Of Mandeuil now let us Promise keep.
WIth modest Mirth were ended now the Mask, and Revels, when
Unto their Lodging Stafford did the Ladies Usher then.
There Elenor was passing sad, and, being ask't, did tell
The Circumstances of her Ring in order as befell,
And shows the gaged Tablet, seen and known of Stafford well.
Her, weeping ripe, he, laughing, bids to patient her a while,
For Mandeuil supposde him wronged, and wrought, quod he, that wile.
But how for you he loves yours, it argues, and withal
A gentle-manly mind his great Exchange for value small.
Nor think I less, nor argue less of Love, and Gentry too
May what, in both preventing him, I mean, quoth she, to do.
His Tablet sent she, and therewith this Breuiat, by a Page.
Of you I ask not back my Loan, but take it, and your Gage:
Yours Either, Neither say I mine, Both shall be, I presage.
He musing of her Meaning, thus to her lets his be known.
Of you I ask not back my Gage, but take it for your Loan:
Mine triples yours, not yours, and yeat for Price gets yours, but One.
Not musing of his Meaning (which to her was not for News)
She of her Meaning also means that he no longer muse.
Now Mandeuil, though grieved much at Stafford, who^ he thought
In Rome to riot, Dorcas not esteemed as she ought,
And for his blabbing him to herthe which had had his Ring.
Against an Oath, means nerethelesse him on his way to bring,
And comes to Staffords Lodging, who prepared for no such thing.
But for a Bridegroom trimmed, and trim was All, and She for Bride
Him contradicted, when, as said, had Mandeuil espide
That likelihood: who, sighing, said, not henceforth will I chide,
But pity Stafford, now so far from wonted Stafford wide.
A Friend should not (not you will I in this Infirmenes) fly,
Alone of Dorcas wronged here not witness shall mine Eye.
Came you to Rome to lose your self, and find at Rome a Friend,
There to begin his Sorrows where your Senses make an end?
Or thrive they more, or travel less, may Englishmen, wish I:
Hence should they profit, whence for most they least do fructify,
How stoicall grows Mandeuil, quoth Stafford, since his Travel:
With you, for like, may I ere long, have cause no less to cauell.
With that, from out another Room to him, that would away,
Then Dorcas-like came Dorcas out, entreating him to stay.
What should we say his Joy, that his Mis-deemes did sort to this?
Was not a gladder man, and yeat anon he gladder is,
When Elenor, then like her self, to him came also out:
But whether gladder he, or she, thus met, shall rest a doubt.
That very day both Couples wead, but what they did that night,
Not men vnpractis'd can report, for Action, or Delight.
Nor creeded be this Loue-Tale of this Lady and this Knight.
THus, of what worth so-ere, is brought this Work of Ours to end:
More have we done, hence outed, more we purposed to have pend,
Which to intelligenced Men, more daring, we commend.
Well wotring, Acts heroic, and great Accidents, not few
Occur this happy Reign, here-hence, of purpose, blanched view.
Nor Perpetuity my Muse can hope, unless in this,
That thy sweet Name, Elizabeth, herein remembered is.
And this, hope I, does bode me good, that very day wherein
Was finished This, did of thy Reign year Thirtith nine begin.
May Muse arte-graced more than mine, in Numbers like supply,
What in thine Highness Praise my Pen, too poor, has passed-by.
A larger Field, a Subject more illustrious, None can ask,
That with thy Scepter and thy self, his Poesy to task.
Thy Peoples Prolocutor be my Prayer, and I pray,
That us thy blessed Life, and Reign, long bless, as at this day.
An Addition in Proese to the second Book of ALBIONS ENGLAND: containing a
Breuiate of the true History of Aeneas.
ACcept him (friendly Reader) where he is, not where he ought, and as he
speaks, not as he should. Misapplied he is not for Matter precedent,
howsoever the penning or misplacing may like or mislike for the English or
Order. Rather has my Remisnes borrowed of Decorum and your Patience, than that
a Patriarch of our Brutons should be abruptly estranged: Of Aeneas therefore it
thus follows:

Aeneidos.
WHen the reuengefull Flames of Tr^y, properly called Ilion, then the
principal City of all Asia, had perfected the more than Te^ne years Siege of
the Grecians expugning of the same, then Aeneas (howsoever by some authorities
noted of disloyalty toward Priam, in this not unworthily surnamed virtuous)
burthening his armed shoulders with his feeble and most aged Father Anchises,
that labored also under his load of the Trojan Gods and sacred Relics,
Aeneas (as I say) with such his Burden, leading by the hand his Son Ascanius
of the age of twelve years, followed not only of the beautiful Creusa his wife
the Daughter of King Priam, but also of a many Trojans participating that
common calamity, brake through the wastfull Flames, mauger the wrathful Foes,
into the fields of Phrigia. Theare the Air emptied of down-burnt Turrets, and
filled with smoke of fired Buildings, assured from their hearts more tears to
their eyes, than the benefit of their present Escape could promise them
comfort. Tr^y therefore not to be rescued, or Creusa (in this business lost
and perishing) to be recovered, Aeneas and his Followers imbarking themselves
in Simois, after long and weary Seafaring arrived in a part of Thrace called
Cressa, bounding on Mygdonia. Here Aeneas, purposing an end of his tedious
Saylings, and not meanly furnished of Treasure conuayed from Troy, laid
Foundation of a City (after the Founder) called Aenea. This City going
forward less effectually than was expected, Aeneas, supposing the Gods to be
yet opposite to the Trojans, knocking down a milke-white Bull pitched an Altar
to do Sacrifice. Near at hand were growing diverse shrubbed Trees, the Boughs
whereof (for the greater reverence and exornation of the present Solemnity) he
cutting and sliuing down, perceived blood in great abundance issuing from the
broken Branches: whereat long admiring, and with great terror and devotion
intercessing the Gods to reveal the meaning of that miraculous Accident, at
the length he heard a pitiful and feeble voice (for diuessly in those days,
did the Devils answer and give Oracles) thus answering.

Reason were it (Aeneas) that the Graves of the dead should privilege their
bodies from the tyranny of the living: but by so much the less do I esteem
my prejudice, by how much the more I know thee unwittingly injurious. Thou
Aeneas, in these Branches, thou tearest the body of thy unfortunate
Brother-in-law Polydor, Son to the likestarred Priamus. Troy as yet was only
threatened, not besieged, when my Father (as thou know) delivered me with a
world of Treasure to Polymn^stor the barbarous King of this Country, here
daungerles (as he pretended) to abide, as the Conseruor and Restoration of his
House and Empire, whatsoever should betide of him, his other Issue, or the
Wars then beginning: but the Greeks prevailing, Avarice, and the declining
State of Phrigia, imboldned my Gardein (fearles of Reuengers) to the murdering
of me: which he traytrously accomplishing on this Shore, secretly raked me up
in these Sands, without honor of better Sepulcher: and of my body (so has it
pleased the Gods, and Nature) are sprung these Branches, in tearing of which
thou torme^test me. Howbeit, in respect of my desire to profit thee by
foretelling of thy Destiny, I account me happily harmed: For know (Aeneas)
that in vain thou do build where the Gods deny thine abode: leave therefore
these defamed Coasts, and prosperously plant the Remain of Troy and thy
Posterity in the fertile Italian Clime.

The voice thus ceased to speak, and Aeneas, without further touch of the
forbidden Shrubs, continuing his fear finished the Sacrifice, and after the
Phrigian fashion solemnlie held an Obit to the Ghost of his murdered Kinsman.

THen, by this admonishment, he and his Trojans leaving the new reared City,
disanker from Thrace in quest of behighted Italy. But no sooner had they put
to Sea, the^ that the winds and the waves solicited (a Poetical fiction) by
the wife of Jupiter, so tossed and turmoyled the disparkled Navy, that the
horror of the circumstances continually threatening their lives, left only
hoped-for death as the remain of all comfort. At length, these instruments of
their long wanderings, and the causes of Anchises and of many noble personages
there perishing, cou^terpleaded (as is fabled) by Venus, tossed their
distackled Fleet to the Shore of Libya. Near to the place of their arriual
stood the beautiful City of Carthage, which Elisa (whom the Phaenicians for
her magnanimious dying, did afterward name Dido) had newly builded: Aeneas by
safe-co^duct received from her repayring thither, found such royal
entertaineme^t, that in respect of the present solace he had forgotten al
passed sorrows, and his hart-spent Trojans found bountiful supplies to all
their late endured scarsities. In the mean while Aeneas, for personage the
Iouilist, for wel-spoke^ the Mercuxilist, and no less fortunate under Venus
her constellatio^, with his comelines so intised the eyes, with his speeches so
inchaunted the ears, and with his virtues so enflamed the heart of the amorous
Cathagenian Queen, that hardly modesty discented that her tongue affirmed not
the love which her eyes outwardly blabbed and her heart inwardly nourished: In
the end, not able longer to contain such extraordinary Passions, in this order
she vented the same to the Lady Anna her Sister.

My trusty Sister, quoth she, (then sighing out a pause) how many great
Princes, since the decease of my late husband Sichaeus and our departure from
Tyrus, have in vain labored a marriage with me, has been unto thee no less
apparent, than of me hitherto vnapproued: but now (haled on I know not by what
destiny) our new-come Guest Aeneas the Trojan (whose matchless personage and
knightly prowess I partially commend not, all confirming of him no less than
I can affirm) the same even the same has supplanted my chaste determinations
of continual widowhood, with an amorous desire of a second marriage: howbeit,
to determine without him, as I must, were to be deceived of him, as I may.
What counsel? My self (Anna) ah my self to motion love were immodestie, and
to be silent less tolerable than death. I would (a violent no voluntary wish)
that Elisa knew her self beloved of Aeneas, Aeneas not knowing himself so
loved of Elisa.

Her Sister with a cheereful countenance promising comfort performed the same
in this answer. Dum signs (quoth she) have their speeches: not any that
observes your looks, but easily aymeth at your love: greater is the wonder of
your strickt chastity, than it would be a novel to see you a Bigama. That
privately peevish and curious, This publicly wish^d and commodious: Or ever
Carthage be perfected in the air, Elisa may putrify in the earth: What
Monument then leauest thou to thy Tyrians, scarce warm in A(...)k, than a
Cities imperfect foundation? Which being already an eye^ore to the wild and
warlike Libians, Barc(...)s, Getulians, and the rest of the Africans, shall
then be v(...)erly extinct, with the very name of the Foundres* but in
matching with so great and valiant a Prince as Aeneas, thou shall not only
live with whom thou do love, and by consorting thy Tyrians with his Trojans
strongly disappoynt the envy of those Nations, but (for Nature has not given
thee such beauty to die barren) being a wife, become ((...) doubt) a mother,
and by legitimate propagation so glad thy Subjects feareles of civil
dissentions. Who can then dislike that Elisa should so love? Bury Sister the
thoughts of Sichaeus with his dead bones, and prosperously prosecute and
prevail in thy sweet passions of Aeneas: Plie him with all provisions and
amorous entertainements: only for his Shippe-works feign delatory wants, and
by Winter be past he, partly coming, will (fear not) be perfectly reclaymed.

This counsel of Anna, though it heaped as it were Athos on Aetna, yet was it
